انتفاءه في الأزمان مطلقاً، فالحمل على أزمان الدنيا انصراف عنه، لأن النصوص القاطعة دلت على ثبوت الرؤية، وهي متواترة المعنى ولا يحتمل التشكيك فيه وأما هذا القدر من الانصراف عن الظاهر فشائع لا بأس به، بل يجب لإيجاب القواطع ذلك ولو سلم أنه يستدعيه باعتبار الأزمان فالإدراك أخص من الرؤية، ولا يلزم من نفي الأخص نفي الأعم فتدبر السكاكي وأتباعه (قالوا: أولاً) لو كان استغرق الجمع للآحاد لما صح النفي عنه إذا ثبت الحكم بواحد أو اثنين فقط و (قد رجال في الدار إذا كان فيها رجل أو رجلان دون لا رجل (قلنا جواز لا رجال ممنوع حقيقة) وليس إلا دعوى مثل المطلوب (و) إن أريد جوازه من جهة التخصيص فلا حجة فيه كما قال : (أما التخصيص فيجوز في كل عام ويحتمل ههنا مجاز آخر وهو إرادة نفي الاجتماع فلا استغراض والمثال المذكور إنما يصح بهذا الاستعمال، وأما حقيقة فلا يصح فافهم (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : إن الحكم على كل جماعة لا يستلزم الحكم على كل فرد) كما يرشدك الجماعة تطيق حمل هذا الخشب فلا يلزم من استغراق الحكم كل جماعة استغراقه كل واحد (قلنا) الحكم على كل جمع مستلزم (لغة الحكم على كل فرد (وإن لم يستلزم عقلاً) بناء (على أن الجمع المحلى بل الجمع المستغرق مطلقاً يبطل الجمعية ويكون عندنا لكل فرد) ويمكن أن يقرر الكلام جوابين أحدهما أن هيئة تركيب الحكم على كل جماعة بصيغة الجمع تدل لغة تناول الحكم لكل واحد وإن لم تستلزم عقلاً، ولا يصح استعمال هذه الهيئة التركيبية إلا فيما يكون حكم الجماعة والآحاد واحداً لا أنه يدل التزاماً غير مقصود حتى يرد عليه أنه غير صحيح، وإلا لم يصح الاستثناء فتأمل فيه، والثاني : أن كون الحكم على كل جماعة ممنوع، بل الجمع يبطل حينئذ هذا (و) قالوا (ثالثاً) روي عن ابن عباس أن الكتاب أكثر من الكتب) يصح إلا إذا كان استغراق المفرد أشمل (قلنا) أولاً (مراده) رضي الله عنه أن استغراق الكتاب بدلاً أشمل من الكتب حال كونهما (منكرين) فليس مما نحن فيه، وثانياً: أن ابن عباس 
لا 
صح 
كما 
لا 
مر
Issue: The encompassing (istighraq) of the plural – whether defined by the definite article, construction, or negated indefinite – applies to every individual, like the singular – i.e., like its encompassing according to jurists, usul scholars, and the majority of Arabic linguists. According to al-Sakkaki and his followers, the encompassing of the singular is more comprehensive; so its encompassing in his view is for every individual, while the encompassing of the plural is for every group. Thus the one and two are excluded from the plural. For us is what preceded of exclusion – the exclusion of the one is valid and customary, and it is to expel what would otherwise enter, so inclusion is necessary. As for reading the book except a leaf etc., it is because one means reading all parts of the book except a leaf. As for excluding a part without this interpretation, even if permitted, it is a false statement not to be heeded, or it is interpreted. And for us is consensus that the encompassing of the plural is for every individual. Do you not see how the successor of the Messenger of Allah, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (may Allah be pleased with him), argued against the Ansar with his statement ﷺ: 'The leaders are from Quraysh.' They affirmed it, submitted to it, and reached consensus by it that no right to the caliphate belonged to any one of the Ansar. If it is said: On this basis, the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' (6:103) indicates absolute negation of vision from every eye. He said: And the verse 'No vision can grasp Him' only necessitates the negation of generality, not the generality of negation, so it does not contradict vision by some eyes. Even if it necessitates the generality of negation by considering individuals and that is conceded, it does not necessitate it by considering times. So the meaning – Allah knows best – is that the eyes do not perceive Him in this world, and it does not contradict its occurrence in the Hereafter. So reflect. And pay no attention to what is said by the people of innovation that negation of an action necessitates its non-existence in all times absolutely. So restricting it to the times of this world is a shift from it, because definitive texts indicate the occurrence of vision, and they are mutawatir in meaning and do not admit of doubt. This degree of shift from the apparent is common and harmless; rather it is obligatory due to definitive proofs. Even if it is conceded that it necessitates it by considering times, perception (idrak) is more specific than vision (ru’yah), and the negation of the more specific does not necessitate the negation of the more general. So ponder. Then al-Sakkaki and his followers said: First, if the plural encompassed individuals, then negation of it would not be valid when the ruling is established by one or two only. And [you say] 'there are men in the house' when there is one or two men, unlike 'there is no man'. We say: The permissibility of 'no men' is denied as a reality; it is nothing but a claim like the desired. And if its permissibility is intended from the perspective of specification, then there is no proof in it, as he said: Specification is permissible in any general, and here another metaphor is possible: intending negation of collectivity, so there is no encompassing. The mentioned example is only valid with this usage, but as a reality it is not valid. So understand. And they said: Second, the ruling on every group does not necessitate the ruling on every individual – as you see, a group can carry this wood, so the encompassing of the ruling on every group does not necessitate its encompassing on every one. We say: The ruling on every collective necessitates linguistically the ruling on every individual, even if not rationally, based on the fact that the definite plural – rather the encompassing plural absolutely – invalidates the collectivity and is in our view for every individual. And the statement can be formulated with two answers: One is that the structure of the ruling on every group with a plural wording linguistically indicates the application of the ruling to every one, even if not rationally. And this structural form is not validly used except where the ruling on the group and individuals is one – not that it indicates an unintended implication such that it could be objected that it is incorrect, otherwise exclusion would not be valid. So reflect on it. And the second: That the ruling on every group is denied; rather the plural then invalidates this. And they said: Third: It is narrated from Ibn Abbas that 'al-kitāb' (the book) is more comprehensive than 'al-kutub' (the books). This is valid only if the encompassing of the singular is more comprehensive. We say: First, his intent (may Allah be pleased with him) is that the encompassing of 'kitāb' (indefinite? actually the text says بدلاً) as a substitute is more comprehensive than 'kutub' when both are indefinite, so it is not what we are discussing. Second, that Ibn Abbas... [text breaks off]
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage presents a major usul dispute between the Hanafi school (and majority) and al-Sakkaki (rhetorician) regarding whether a definite plural (الجمع المعرف) indicates generality for every individual or every group. The Hanafi position is that it encompasses every individual, just like the singular. Proofs include: (1) The validity of exclusion (istithna') from a plural – excluding one individual is sound and customary; (2) Consensus – the Companions understood from the hadith 'الْأَئِمَّةُ مِنْ قُرَيْشٍ' (leaders are from Quraysh) that no one from the Ansar could be caliph, indicating the plural covers every individual leader. (3) The Quranic verse 'لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الْأَبْصَارُ' is interpreted by the majority to negate vision in this world only, not absolutely, using the principle that negation of generality (سلب العموم) is not the same as generality of negation (عموم السلب). They also argue that 'idrak' (perception) is more specific than 'ru'yat' (vision), so negation of idrak does not negate vision. The Sakkaki school objects: (a) Negation of plural should hold even if only one individual exists; the Hanafi response is that 'la rijal' ('no men') is not literal but a metaphor for collective entity. (b) The ruling on a group does not entail ruling on each individual (e.g., a group carries wood); the Hanafi response is that linguistically the plural wording implies each individual, even if rationally the group action is different. (c) A narration from Ibn Abbas: 'الكتاب أكثر من الكتب' (the book is more comprehensive than the books); the Hanafi response distinguishes between definite and indefinite forms. The passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
استغراق الجمع (istighrāq al-jam‘): The encompassing/universality of the plural form; whether it covers every single individual or every group
عموم السلب (‘umūm al-salb): Generality of negation: the negation applies to every individual
سلب العموم (salb al-‘umūm): Negation of generality: the statement negates the general property, not every individual

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How did Abu Bakr (ra) use the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' to prove that no Ansari could be caliph?
A) He understood the plural 'الأئمة' as including every individual Qurayshi leader, thus excluding all Ansar.
B) He argued that the word 'أئمة' is a singular referring to one person.
C) He claimed the hadith applies only to the first four caliphs.
D) He interpreted the hadith as metaphorical, not literal.

Key Takeaway
The definite plural covers every individual, as proven by the Companions' consensus on the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' and the validity of exclusion (istithna').

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
The Definite Plural Covers Every Individual: The Hanafi Claim and Its Ijma Proof
Presenting the Hanafi claim that the definite plural extends to every individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the definite plural (الجمع المحلى) indicates generality for every individual, and evaluate the consensus proof from the incident of Abu Bakr.

Core Idea
The definite plural covers every individual, proven by the Sahaba's consensus on 'الأئمة من قريش'.

In Hanafi usul, the scope of a general term (عام) determines how rulings are derived. A key disputed principle is whether the definite plural (الجمع المحلى باللام) extends to every individual member of that plural. This lesson examines the Hanafi claim that it does, focusing on the linguistic and consensus proofs presented in the text, including the famous argument of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
State the claim: the definite plural covers each individual (استغراق الجمع لكل فرد فرد). This is a foundational usul principle that governs how general terms are interpreted.
2
Locate the primary proof: Ijma of the Sahaba
Recognize the consensus argument: Abu Bakr used the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' to argue that no Ansari could be caliph, and the Sahaba accepted it. Their acceptance constitutes ijma that the plural covers every individual from Quraysh.
The ijma is qat'i in both transmission (thubut) and meaning (dalala).
3
Examine the objections and Hanafi responses
Follow the text's three main objections: (1) from the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار', (2) from al-Sakkaki's example 'لا رجال في الدار', and (3) from Ibn Abbas's statement that 'الكتاب' is more general than 'الكتب'. For each, note the Hanafi rebuttal.
The responses invoke principles like 'salb al-umum' vs 'umum al-salb', metaphorical usage, and the specific context of Ibn Abbas's remark.
4
Understand the epistemological weight
The ijma proof is qat'i, establishing the rule firmly. The Hanafi position is that the definite plural is 'amm and covers every individual, though specification (takhsis) is permissible. This contrasts with the Shafi'i/Mutakallimin view that the definite plural is not general but zanni.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
لغة جماعة، فالواحد والاثنان خارجان عنه لنا ما تقدم من الاستثناء) فإن استثناء الواحد صحيح وعرفاً، وهو لإخراج ما لولاه لدخل فوجب التناول، وأما قرأت الكتاب إلا ورقاً ونحوه فلأنه أريد به قرأت جميع أجزاء الكتاب إلا ورقاً وأما استثناء الجزء من دون هذا التأويل، وإن جوّز فقول باطل لا يلتفت إليه أو مؤوّل (و) لنا الإجماع على أن استغراق الجمع لكل فرد فرد ألا ترى أنه كيف استدل خليفة رسول الله أبو بكر الصديق السلب باعتبار (الإفراد وسلم ذلك (فلا يستدعيه باعتبار الأزمان فالمعنى والله أعلم لا تدركه الأبصار في الدنيا ولا ينافي ثبوتها في الآخرة (فتأمل) ولا تلتفت إلى ما يقال من قبل أهل البدع أن نفي الفعل يقتضي انتفاءه في الأزمان مطلقاً، فالحمل على أزمان الدنيا انصراف عنه، لأن النصوص القاطعة دلت على ثبوت الرؤية، وهي متواترة المعنى ولا يحتمل التشكيك فيه وأما هذا القدر من الانصراف عن الظاهر فشائع لا بأس به، بل يجب لإيجاب القواطع ذلك ولو سلم أنه يستدعيه باعتبار الأزمان فالإدراك أخص من الرؤية، ولا يلزم من نفي الأخص نفي الأعم فتدبر السكاكي وأتباعه (قالوا: أولاً) لو كان استغرق الجمع للآحاد لما صح النفي عنه إذا (أما التخصيص فيجوز في كل عام ويحتمل ههنا مجاز آخر وهو إرادة نفي الاجتماع فلا استغراق والمثال المذكور إنما يصح بهذا الاستعمال، وأما حقيقة فلا يصح فافهم (و) قالوا :(ثانياً : إن الحكم على كل جماعة لا أن الجمع المحلى بل الجمع المستغرق مطلقاً يبطل الجمعية ويكون عندنا لكل فرد) ويمكن أن يقرر الكلام جوابين أحدهما أن هيئة تركيب الحكم على كل جماعة بصيغة الجمع تدل لغة تناول الحكم لكل واحد وإن لم تستلزم عقلاً، ولا يصح استعمال هذه الهيئة التركيبية إلا فيما يكون حكم الجماعة والآحاد واحداً لا أنه يدل التزاماً غير مقصود حتى يرد عليه أنه غير صحيح، وإلا لم يصح الاستثناء فتأمل فيه، والثاني : أن كون الحكم على كل جماعة ممنوع، بل الجمع يبطل حينئذ هذا (و) قالوا (ثالثاً) روي عن ابن عباس أن الكتاب أكثر من الكتب) يصح إلا إذا كان استغراق المفرد أشمل (قلنا) أولاً (مراده) رضي الله عنه أن استغراق الكتاب بدلاً أشمل من الكتب حال كونهما (منكرين) فليس مما نحن فيه، وثانياً: أن ابن عباس وحده لا يصلح لمعارضة سائر الصحابة كافة، وثالثاً كما قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية» إن مراده أن الكتاب المعهود وهو القرآن أشمل وأكثر جمعاً للحكم من الكتب الأخرى المعهودة وهي المنزلة على الأنبياء السابقين، فليس قوله مما نحن فيه في شيء فتدبر .
And the matter ultimately comes to... Language of collective, so the one and two are excluded from it. For us is what has preceded of exception: that the exception of the one is correct in custom, and it is to remove what otherwise would have been included, so coverage is necessary. As for 'I read the book except a leaf' and the like, it is because by it is meant 'I read all parts of the book except a leaf'; as for excepting a part without this interpretation, if it is allowed, it is a false statement not to be heeded, or it is interpreted. And for us is consensus that the coverage of the collective is for every individual; do you not see how the Caliph of the Messenger of God, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, may God be pleased with him, argued against the Ansar, may God be pleased with them, with his statement (peace and blessings be upon him): 'The leaders are from Quraysh'? And they affirmed it and accepted it and agreed with it that no one from the Ansar has right to the caliphate. If it is said: Then this would be implied by His statement, the Exalted: 'Vision perceives Him not' (al-An'am 103), the absolute negation of vision from every eye. He said: And His statement, the Exalted: 'Vision perceives Him not' only entails the negation of generality, not the generality of negation; so it does not contradict the seeing of some eyes. And if it entails the generality of negation with respect to individuals, and that is granted, then it does not require it with respect to times. So the meaning, and God knows best, is that vision does not perceive Him in this world, and it does not contradict its affirmation in the Hereafter. So ponder. And do not pay attention to what is said by the people of innovation that negation of an action requires its negation in all times absolutely. So restricting it to the times of this world is a diversion from it, because the decisive texts have established the affirmation of vision, and they are tawatur in meaning and admit no doubt. And this degree of diversion from the apparent is common and acceptable; rather, it is obligatory due to the requirement of the decisive texts. And if it is granted that it requires it with respect to times, then 'perception' is more specific than 'vision', and from the negation of the more specific, the more general is not necessarily negated. So reflect. Al-Sakkaki and his followers said: First: If the collective covered individuals, then negation would not be correct when the ruling is established by only one or two, as in 'there are no men in the house' when there is one man or two men. We say: The permissibility of 'no men' is denied in reality; it is nothing but a claim similar to what is sought. And if what is meant is its permissibility by way of specification, then there is no proof in it, as he said: Specification is allowed in every general term, and here another metaphor is possible, namely intending the negation of assembly, so there is no coverage. The mentioned example is only valid with this usage, but in reality it is not valid. So understand. And they said, Secondly: The ruling on every group does not entail the ruling on every individual, as you can see that a group can carry this wood, but it does not follow from the coverage of the ruling on every group its coverage of every individual. We say: The ruling on every group entails, by language, the ruling on every individual, even if it does not entail rationally, based on the fact that the definite plural, indeed the absolute plural, negates collectivity and is for us for each individual. And the statement can be formulated as two answers: One: that the syntactic structure of the ruling on every group in the plural form indicates, by language, the inclusion of every individual in the ruling, even if it does not entail rationally. And this syntactic structure is not correctly used except where the ruling of the group and the individuals is one; not that it indicates an incidental implication such that it could be objected that it is incorrect. Otherwise exception would not be correct. So ponder on it. And the second: that the existence of a ruling on every group is denied; rather, the plural then becomes void. And they said, Thirdly: It is narrated from Ibn Abbas that 'the book' is more general than 'the books'. But this is correct only if the coverage of the singular is more comprehensive. We say: First: His meaning, may God be pleased with him, is that the coverage of the singular as a substitute is more comprehensive than the plural when both are indefinite, so this is not what we are discussing. Secondly: Ibn Abbas alone cannot oppose the rest of the Companions entirely. Thirdly, as the 'Miftah al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: His meaning is that the well-known book, i.e., the Quran, is more comprehensive and collects more rulings than the other well-known books revealed to previous prophets. So his statement has nothing to do with our discussion. So ponder.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the linguistic principle that the definite plural (الجمع المحلى باللام) indicates coverage of every individual member. The Hanafi position is that it does cover each individual, as supported by the consensus (إجماع) of the Sahaba in the incident where Abu Bakr (ra) used the hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' (leaders are from Quraysh) to argue that no one from the Ansar could be caliph, and the Ansar accepted this argument, thereby consenting that the plural 'الأئمة' includes every individual from Quraysh. The text then addresses several objections. The first objection involves the Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision perceives Him not). Opponents argue that if the plural 'الأبصار' covers every individual eye, then the verse would negate seeing Allah in the Hereafter, which contradicts established texts. The response is that the verse negates the general category of perception (سلب العموم) not a general negation (عموم السلب); it negates any eye perceiving Him in this world, but does not negate seeing Him in the Hereafter. Another objection is from al-Sakkaki and his followers: if the collective covers individuals, then 'لا رجال في الدار' (there are no men in the house) would be false if there is one man. The Hanafi response is that such usage is metaphorical, not real; the real usage of 'لا رجال' requires the absence of any male. They also object that the ruling on a group (e.g., a group can carry wood) does not apply to each individual. The Hanafis respond that linguistically (by language) the ruling on the group does logically apply to each individual, even if not rationally; the plural form is constructed to do that. The passage also addresses the statement of Ibn Abbas that 'الكتاب' (the book) is more general than 'الكتب' (the books), but this is dismissed as not relevant to the discussion because it refers to the Quran compared to previous scriptures. The overall Hanafi position is that the definite plural is 'amm (general) and covers every individual, though specification (takhsis) is permissible.

Key Terms
الجمع المحلى (al-jam‘ al-muḥallā): The plural noun with the definite article (ال), which according to Hanafi usul indicates coverage of every individual member.
الإجماع (al-ijmā‘): Consensus of the scholars, here specifically the Sahaba's agreement on the inference from the plural in the hadith.
استغراق (istighrāq): Coverage or inclusiveness; here, the property of a term to include every individual member of the category.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary evidence the Hanafi school uses to prove that the definite plural covers every individual?
A) The Quranic verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'
B) The hadith 'الأئمة من قريش' and the consensus of the Sahaba
C) The statement of Ibn Abbas that 'الكتاب أكثر من الكتب'
D) The linguistic principle that exception (استثناء) is valid

Key Takeaway
The definite plural (الجمع المحلى) is considered general ('amm) and covers every individual in the Hanafi school, proven by the qat'i evidence of Sahaba consensus.

Does Sound Masculine Plural Include Women? The Debate
Analyzing the scope of the sound masculine plural in Arabic and its legal implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on whether the sound masculine plural includes women, and apply the principles of al-ta'sīs and al-ta'kīd to evaluate the evidence from al-Ahzab 35.

Core Idea
The sound masculine plural alone does not include women; separate mention establishes new meaning.

How do we determine the scope of a linguistic form that is foundational to legal texts? The sound masculine plural is a common structure in the Quran and Hadith. This lesson explores the debate over whether it inherently includes women or is restricted to men, using a key verse and the usul principle preferring new information over confirmation. Understanding this clarifies many fiqh rulings on vows, inheritance, and general obligations.

Guided Path
1
Define the sound masculine plural
Understand the definition given in the passage: a plural whose singular distinguishes masculine and feminine (e.g., Muslim / Muslima), and its usage often predominates for males. Note the exclusions: purely male terms (رجال), neutral terms (ناس), and purely female terms.
The difference hinges on the singular nature: the plural for 'men' is not the same as the sound masculine plural.
2
Identify the two positions
Recognize that the majority (Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis) hold the plural refers only to men by وضع (original assignment), while Hanbalis hold it includes both sexes.
The author supports the majority/Hanafi view.
3
Analyze the Quranic evidence (al-Ahzab 35)
Examine the verse where men are mentioned in the sound masculine plural and then women are listed separately. Understand the argument: if the plural already included women, the separate mention would be تأكيد (confirmation); if not, it is تأسيس (new information). The principle التأسيس أولى من التأكيد decides the meaning.
This is a classic usul argument from the Hanafi school.
4
Evaluate the counter-argument
Consider the objection from 'Sharh al-Mukhtasar' that the plural is used for men as a reality without dispute, so the verse does not prove exclusion. The response clarifies that the plural is not a homonym but a shared meaning (مشترك معنوي), and the argument from تأسيس still holds because the common meaning would include women, making their mention confirmation.
The passage also notes that a single example does not establish a universal rule, and specification (تخصيص) like 'حافظوا على الصلوات والصلاة الوسطى' is common but not تأكيد in the technical sense.
5
Apply the position
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): if someone vows to free 'the believing slaves' (using masculine plural), Hanafi says it applies only to male slaves; Hanbali includes female slaves unless excluded by context.
This shows how linguistic analysis directly affects fatwa.

مسألة (جمع المذكر السالم ونحوه مما يغلب فيه الرجال على النساء يعني يكون مفرده بحيث يصح إطلاقه على المختلط من الرجال والنساء تغليباً، وهو الجمع الذي يفرق في مفرده بين المذكر والمؤنث بالتاء وعدمه، واحترز بهذا القيد عن الجمع الذي مفرده لا يصح إطلاقه على النساء أصلاً كالرجال، فإنه للرجال اتفاقاً، وعن الجمع الذي مفرده متناول لهما لغة ووضعاً نحو الناس، فإنه يتناول اتفاقاً وعن الجمع الذي مفرده مختص بالنساء، فإنه مختص بالنساء اتفاقاً إن وجد فتأمل فيه : هل يشمل النساء وضعاً كما أنه يشمل الرجال (نفاه الأكثر) من الشافعية والمالكية خلافاً للحنابلة) فإنهم قالوا : يشمل الرجال والنساء بالوضع والمصنف اختار الأول وقال لنا أن المتبادر منه عند الإطلاق (من دون قرينة) صارفة (هم الرجال وحدهم) وهو أمارات الحقيقة، ودليل التبادر الاستقراء، لكن الخصم لا يساعد عليه واستدل أولاً بقوله) من تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَتِ وَالْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَالْمُؤْمِنَتِ وَالْقَنِنِينَ وَالْقَنِنَتِ وَالصَّدِقِينَ وَالصَّدِقَاتِ وَالصَّبِرِينَ وَالصَّبِرَاتِ وَالْخَشِعِينَ وَالْخَشِعَتِ وَالْمُتَصَدِقِينَ وَالْمُتَصَدِقَاتِ وَالصَّمِينَ وَالصَّمَتِ وَالْحَفِظِينَ فُرُوجَهُمْ وَالْحَفِظَتِ والذَّاكِرِينَ اللَّهَ كَثِيرًا وَالنَّكِرَتِ أَعَدَّ اللهُ لَهُم مَّغْفِرَةً وَأَجْرًا عَظِيمًا [الأحزاب: ٣٥] فقد عطف له النساء على المذكور بصيغة الجمع المذكر ، فلو كانت النساء داخلات فيها لزم التأكيد، ولو لم تدخل بل اختصت الصيغة بالرجال كان تأسيساً والتأسيس أولى من التأكيد) فالصيغة مختصة بهم في الاستعمال والأصل الحقيقة فهي بهم خاصة أقول فيه نظر، لأن في «شرح المختصر أن لا نزاع في أنه للرجال وحدهم) أي مستعملاً فيهم (حقيقة، فعلى هذا لا يلزم التأكيد فإنه يجوز أن يكون الجمع في الآية مستعملاً للرجال، فلا تأكيد ولا مجاز (فلا يثبت المدعي من كونه لهم وحدهم (كما لا يخفي) قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: ليس معنى كونه حقيقة لهم أنه مشترك لفظي فيهم وفي المختلط كما سيصرح به المصنف، بل المعنى أنه للقدر المشترك، فهو مشترك معنوي وإطلاقه عليهم كونهم من أفراده حقيقة، وهذا لا يضر في الاستدلال، فإن هذه الجموع محلاة مفيدة لاستغراق ما تصلح له فلو كانت متناولة للنساء لكانت مشمولة الصيغ فيكون ذكر النساء بعده تأكيداً، وإذا التأسيس أولى فيجب حملها على الرجال خاصة، والأصل الحقيقة هذا ثم في الاستدلال شيء هو أن مثالاً جزئياً لا يصحح القاعدة الكلية، كيف كما أنه استعمل في الرجال وحدهم، كذلك استعمل للمختلط كثيراً، فلم لم يكن استعمال الاختلاط حقيقة، وصار استعمال الانفراد حقيقة، فأصالة الحقيقة لا تدل على كون هذا الاستعمال حقيقة وأيضاً أفراد فرد من العام للنصوصية شائع، كما في قوله تعالى : حَفِظُوا عَلَى الصَّلَوَاتِ وَالصَّلَوةِ الْوُسْطَى [البقرة: ۲۳۸] وأمثاله وهذا ليس تأكيداً اصطلاحياً،
Issue: The sound masculine plural and similar forms where males predominate over females [in usage], meaning its singular is such that it can be applied to a mixed group of men and women by way of predominant usage. It is the plural whose singular distinguishes between masculine and feminine with or without the ta'. This restriction excludes the plural whose singular cannot be applied to women at all, like 'men' (رجال), for that is for men by agreement; and excludes the plural whose singular applies to both by language and original usage, like 'people' (ناس), for that includes both by agreement; and excludes the plural whose singular is specific to women, for that is specific to women by agreement if it exists. So ponder: Does it include women by original usage as it includes men? The majority (of Shafi'is and Malikis) deny this, contrary to the Hanbalis, for they said it includes men and women by original usage. And the author chose the first opinion and said: For us is that what is immediately understood from it when used absolutely, without a diverting context, is men alone, and that is a sign of reality. The proof of immediate understanding is induction, but the opponent does not concede it. And he first argued with His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, the believing men and believing women...' (al-Ahzab 35). For He appended women to what was mentioned in the masculine plural form. So if women were included in that form, then [their separate mention] would be a confirmation (ta'kid), and if they were not included but rather the form was specific to men, then it would be an establishment (ta'sis), and establishment is preferred over confirmation. Thus the form is specific to them in usage, and the original principle is reality, so it is specific to them. I say: There is consideration in this, because in 'Sharh al-Mukhtasar' it is stated that there is no dispute that it is for men alone, i.e., it is used in them as a reality. On this basis, confirmation is not necessary, for it is permissible that the plural in the verse is used for men, so there is no confirmation and no metaphor. Thus the claimant's proof that it is for them alone is not established, as is obvious. Miftah al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: The meaning of its being a reality for them is not that it is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) between them and the mixed group, as the author will later clarify; rather, the meaning is that it is for the common meaning (qadr mushtarak), so it is a shared meaning (mushtarak ma'nawi), and its application to them is real as they are among its individuals. This does not harm the deduction. For these plurals, when definite, are useful for covering whatever they are suited for. So if they included women, then the forms would encompass them, so mentioning women afterwards would be confirmation, and since establishment is preferable, they must be interpreted as specifically for men, and the original principle is reality. Then there is something in the deduction: a particular example does not validate a universal rule. How is it that it is used for men alone and also used for the mixed group frequently, so why is the mixed usage not considered reality and the isolated usage considered reality? The original principle of reality does not indicate that this usage is reality. Also, singling out an individual from the general is common for specification, as in His statement: 'Guard the prayers and the middle prayer' (al-Baqarah 238) and similar examples, and this is not terminology confirmation.
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the linguistic scope of the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم). The author defines it as a form whose singular can apply to a mixed group of men and women by way of غلبة (predominance). He then distinguishes it from other types: plurals exclusively for men (like رجال), plurals that are gender-neutral (like ناس), and plurals exclusively for women. The central question is: when the sound masculine plural is used without any contextual qualifier, does it include women by original linguistic assignment (وضع)? The majority of Shafi'i and Maliki scholars (and by implication Hanafis) say no - it refers only to men. The Hanbalis argue it includes both sexes by وضع. The author supports the majority/ Hanafi view. The main evidence is the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ahzab (35) which lists 'Muslim men' and 'Muslim women' separately. If the masculine plural already included women, then mentioning 'Muslim women' separately would be تأكيد (confirmation), whereas if it does not include them, it is تأسيس (establishment of a new meaning). Since تأسيس is stronger, the masculine plural must be specific to men. The author then critiques a counter-argument from 'Sharh al-Mukhtasar' which claims that there is no dispute that the plural is used for men as a reality, making the verse's mention of women not تأكيد but simply a separate category. The author responds via 'Miftah al-Asrar' by clarifying that the plural is not a homonym (مشترك لفظي) but a shared meaning (مشترك معنوي) with a common aspect. Its application to men is real as individuals of that common meaning. The argument that تأسيس is preferred still holds because if women were included by the common meaning, their separate mention would be تأكيد. The passage ends with a critique that a single example cannot establish a universal rule, and that this type of specification (تخصيص) is common in the Quran, e.g., 'حافظوا على الصلوات والصلاة الوسطى' where الصلاة الوسطى is specified despite being included in الصلوات, but that is not تأكيد in the technical sense.

Key Terms
جمع المذكر السالم (jam‘ al-mudhakkar al-sālim): The sound masculine plural that applies to males and can be used to refer to mixed groups by تغليب, but in its original meaning it is specific to males according to the majority.
التأسيس (al-ta’sīs): The principle that when a text can be interpreted as establishing a new meaning (تأسيس) or as a confirmation (تأكيد), the interpretation that establishes a new meaning is preferred.
التأكيد (al-tā’kīd): A confirmation of what has already been stated; it is less informative than تأسيس.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of usul scholars (Hanafis, Shafi'is, Malikis), does the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم) include women when used without a qualifying context?
A) Yes, it includes both men and women
B) No, it refers only to men
C) It depends on the context
D) It is ambiguous

Key Takeaway
The sound masculine plural is a linguistic form whose scope is disputed; the Hanafi school adheres to the majority view that it excludes women unless a separate indication is present.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Does the Sound Masculine Plural Include Women?
Usul dispute on scope of sound masculine plural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that the sound masculine plural is specific to men, by analyzing the Quranic verse (Al-Ahzab 35) and the hadith of Umm Salama, and distinguish it from the Hanbali counter-argument.

Core Idea
Separate mention of women after the masculine plural proves ta'sis, not ta'kid — the plural is for men alone.

This passage from 'Fawatih al-Rahamut' examines a critical usul question: does the sound masculine plural grammatically include women? The Hanafi (and majority) position holds it does not, while the Hanbalis argue it includes both. The passage presents three proofs: the Quranic verse that lists men and women separately, the hadith where women complained about exclusion, and the grammatical label 'plural of masculine'. You will learn how each proof is constructed and countered, and understand the practical impact in fiqh rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue and opposing positions
Locate the core question: does the sound masculine plural include women by linguistic usage (wad')? Note the Hanafi/Shafi'i/Maliki position (no, specific to men) vs. Hanbali (yes, includes both).
2
Analyze the first proof: the Quranic verse (Al-Ahzab 35)
The verse separately mentions 'muslimin' and 'muslimat'. If the masculine plural included women, this repetition would be ta'kid (emphasis). Since ta'sis (independent new mention) is preferred, the masculine plural must be for men alone. Examine the counter-arguments that challenge this reasoning.
The principle 'ta'sis awla min al-ta'kid' is key in Hanafi usul.
3
Analyze the second proof: the hadith of Umm Salama
Women complained they were not mentioned, then the verse was revealed. This shows women themselves understood the masculine plural to exclude them, and the Prophet's tacit approval confirms it. Consider objections about 'independent mention' and the women's desire for honor.
The hadith is narrated by Ahmad and al-Tirmidhi; graded hasan gharib.
4
Evaluate the third proof: grammatical terminology
Grammarians call it 'plural of masculine'. If it included women, the term would be misleading. Counter-argument: grammarians call 'insan' masculine even though it includes females. Assess the strength of this proof.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and thamarah al-khilaf
The author sides with the majority. The practical consequence: in fiqh, rulings based on sound masculine plurals apply only to men unless there is a separate indication for women. Identify examples from other chapters.
Example: 'al-sariqun' in Quran applies to male thieves only; female thieves need separate evidence.

التأسيس أولى فيجب حملها على الرجال خاصة، والأصل الحقيقة هذا ثم في الاستدلال شيء هو أن مثالاً جزئياً لا يصحح القاعدة الكلية، كيف كما أنه استعمل في الرجال وحدهم، كذلك استعمل للمختلط كثيراً، فلم لم يكن استعمال الاختلاط حقيقة، وصار استعمال الانفراد حقيقة، فأصالة الحقيقة لا تدل على كون هذا الاستعمال حقيقة وأيضاً أفراد فرد من العام للنصوصية شائع، كما في قوله تعالى : حَفِظُوا عَلَى الصَّلَوَاتِ وَالصَّلَوةِ الْوُسْطَى [البقرة: ۲۳۸] وأمثاله وهذا ليس تأكيداً اصطلاحياً،.  المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية ٢٦٣ فإن أريد أنه لو كان للاختلاط حقيقة لكان تأكيداً اصطلاحياً، فالملازمة ممنوعة، وإن أريد نفس تقوية الحكم ولو في بعض الأفراد، فكون التأسيس أولى منه ممنوع، وإلا لكان أمثال الجمل المصدرة بأن خلاف الأصل فتدبر (و) استدل (ثانياً بالتقرير أي بتقرير رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ((والنفي) أي نفي المؤمنات ذكرهن فيما روت أم المؤمنين أم سلمة أنها قالت: يا رسول الله، إن النساء قلن : ما نرى الله ذكر إلا الرجال فنزل ﴿إِنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَتِ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٣٥] رواه أن جمع لي أحمد كذا في «التحرير»، وروى الترمذي عن أم عمارة قالت : أتيت النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم فقلت : ما أرى كل شيء إلى الرجال، وما أرى النساء يذكرن بشيء فنزلت إِنَّ الْمُسْلِمِينَ وَالْمُسْلِمَاتِ قال الترمذي حديث حسن غريب، فالنساء مع كونهن من أهل اللغة فصحاء لمانعين ذكرهن علم المذكر غير متناول إياهن ثم تقرير الرسول الله يفيد القطع به (وأورد) بمنع نفيهن ذكر أنفسهن حتى يفيد عدم تناول الصيغة إياهن و بحمله على عدم الذكر) لهن (استقلالاً) وفيه أنه على تقدير كون الصيغة للرجال والنساء يكون ذكرهن مع الرجال، فإن أريد بالذكر الاستقلالي ذكرهن ،وحدهن فليس ذكر الرجال استقلالياً أيضاً، فلا يصح الشكوى بذكر الرجال استقلالاً ،دونهن، وإن أريد ذكرهن مقصوداً وإن كان مع أغيارهن أيضاً استقلالي، فلا يصح الشكوى أصلاً إلا أن يقال: تناول الصيغة للمختلط ليس إلا لأنهن كالتوابع للرجال، فأردن ذكرهن استقلالاً من غير تبعية فتأمل فيه والأولى أن يحمل قولهن : ما نرى الله ذكر إلا الرجال على الذكر استقلالاً بصيغ أخرى غير صيغ الجموع السالمة نحو: الرجال والعباد، فأردن أن يذكرن كذلك فتدبر قيل الشكاة به حينئذ أي حين إرادة الذكر الاستقلالي (بعيد، فإن الرجال قوامون على النساء فهن من توابعهم أقول : لعل مرادهن التماس الذكر كذلك أي من غير تبعية (تحصيلاً للشرافة قال أمير المؤمنين عمر رضي الله عنه : والله كنا في الجاهلية ما نعد للنساء أمراً حتى أنزل الله فيهن ما أنزل وقسم لهن ما قسم رواه الشيخان في حديث طويل وكون الرجال قوامين عليهن لا ينافي قصد تحصيل الشرافة، وأيضاً الصيغة متناولة لهن قطعاً لعموم الشرعية ولو مجازاً، ومحال أن لا يكن عارفات بتناول الصيغة إياهنَّ فالشكاة بعدم ذكر هن مطلقاً لا يصح إنما الشكاة للذكر الاستقلالي تحصيلاً للشرافة فافهم (و) استدل (ثالثاً بأنه جمع المذكر إجماعاً وهو) أي : الجمع لتضعيف المفرد فيكون هذا الجمع لتضعيف المذكر وفيه أنه استدلال بالتسمية فإن النحاة يسمون هذا الجمع جمع المذكر، ولا يلزم منه أن يكون مفرده ،مذكراً ، ألا ترى أنهم يقولون لنحو السنين المذكر مع أن مفرده مؤنث عندهم هذا وربما يقرر هكذا إن هذا الجمع جمع المذكر باتفاق النحاة، والجمع لتضعيف مفرده فيكون مدلوله آحاداً من المذكر الذي هو مفرده، وهذا ليس استدلالاً بالتسمية وفيه أن المذكر عندهم ما كان مجرداً عن التاء ونحوه، وإن كان الإناث داخلة فيه، ألا ترى أنهم قالوا : الإنسان مذكر مع أن أفراده الإناث، فلا يلزم من كونه جمعاً للمذكر أن لا يكون آحاد مفرده أنثى، كما لا يخفى على من له أدنى مساس (و) قال في «التحرير» : فإن قيل) لو دخل فيه الإناث ويكون جمع المذكر تسمية محضة فمفرده مؤنث أيضاً فأين تذهب تاء مسلمة) هذا
Issue: The sound masculine plural and similar (where males predominate over females), meaning its singular can be applied to a mixed group of men and women by way of predominance (taghlib), and it is the plural that distinguishes in its singular between masculine and feminine by the presence or absence of taa. This excludes the plural whose singular cannot be applied to women at all, like rijal (men), for it is for men by agreement. Also excludes the plural whose singular includes both by language and usage like an-nas (people), for it includes both by agreement. Also excludes the plural whose singular is specific to women, for it is specific to women by agreement if it exists. So consider: does it include women by usage (wad') just as it includes men? (The majority deny it) among Shafi'is and Malikis, contrary to Hanbalis, for they said it includes men and women by usage. The author chose the first and said: For us, the immediate understanding (tabadur) when used absolutely (without a clarifying context) is (men alone), and that is a sign of being literal (haqiqah). The proof of tabadur is induction (istiqra'), but the opponent does not accept it. And he argued first with His statement, the Exalted: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women, the believing men and believing women, the obedient men and obedient women, the truthful men and truthful women, the patient men and patient women, the humble men and humble women, the charitable men and charitable women, the fasting men and fasting women, the men who guard their private parts and the women who guard, and the men who remember Allah much and the women who remember – Allah has prepared for them forgiveness and a great reward.' [Al-Ahzab:35]. He (Quran) has placed the women in apposition to what was mentioned as masculine plural forms. So if women were included in those (masculine plurals), that would be an emphasis (ta'kid). And if they were not included but the forms were specific to men, it would be an independent mention (ta'sis), and ta'sis is preferable to ta'kid. So the form is specific to men in usage, and the principle is literalness (haqiqah), so it is specific to them. I say there is a problem because in Sharh al-Mukhtasar it says there is no dispute that it is for men alone, i.e., used for them literally. So on this, ta'kid is not necessary because it is permissible that the plural in the verse is used for men, so no ta'kid and no metaphor (majaz). So the claim that it is for them alone is not established, as is obvious. The author of Matla' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: 'The meaning of it being literal for them is not that it is a homonym (mushtarak lafzi) between them and the mixed group as the author will clearly state, but rather that it is for the common trait (al-qadr al-mushtarak), so it is a shared meaning (mushtarak ma'nawi), and applying it to them (men) is literal because they are among its individuals. This does not harm the argument. For these plurals are definite and indicate the totality of what they are applicable to. So if they included women, women would be covered by the forms, and mentioning them afterwards would be ta'kid. And since ta'sis is preferable, it must be interpreted as specific to men. And the principle is literalness.' There is a point in this argument: that a particular example cannot establish a general rule. Indeed, just as it is used for men alone, it is also used for mixed groups frequently. So why isn't the mixed usage taken as literal, and the exclusive usage became literal? The principle of literalness does not indicate that this usage is literal. Also, particularizing an individual from a general term for specify (nassiyya) is common, as in His statement: 'Guard the prayers and the middle prayer' [Al-Baqarah:238] and the like. This is not conventional ta'kid. For if it is meant that if it were literal for mixed, it would be conventional ta'kid, the implication (mulazama) is denied. If it is meant the mere strengthening of the ruling even in some individuals, then the claim that ta'sis is preferable is denied, otherwise the likes of sentences beginning with 'inna' would be contrary to the principle. So ponder. And he argued secondly with the report: i.e., the report of the Messenger of Allah and the negation: i.e., the believing women's negation of being mentioned, as narrated by Umm al-Mu'minin Umm Salama that she said: 'O Messenger of Allah, the women said: We do not see Allah mentioning except men.' Then the verse 'Indeed, the Muslim men and Muslim women' was revealed. Reported by Ahmad as in al-Tahrir. And al-Tirmidhi narrated from Umm 'Amara: 'I came to the Prophet and said: I see everything is for men, and I do not see women mentioned in anything.' Then it was revealed. Al-Tirmidhi said: hasan gharib hadith. So women, despite being among the people of language, eloquent, denied that the masculine (form) includes them. Then the report of the Messenger gives certainty. And it was objected by denying that they negated their own mention, such that it would indicate that the form does not include them, and by interpreting it as not mentioning them independently. For if the form is for both men and women, their mention would be with men. If by independent mention is meant mentioning them alone, then the mention of men is not independent either, so the complaint about men being mentioned independently is not valid. If it is meant mentioning them intentionally even with others, then the complaint is not valid at all unless it is said that the form includes the mixed only because they are like followers; so they wanted independent mention without subordination. So ponder. The better approach is to interpret their statement 'We do not see Allah mentioning except men' as independent mention by other forms besides the sound plurals, like 'ar-rijal' and 'al-'ibad', so they wanted to be mentioned likewise. So consider. It was said: the complaint at that time, i.e., when wanting independent mention, is far-fetched because men are in charge (qawwamun) over women, so women are their followers. I say: Perhaps they wanted request of mention likewise, i.e., without subordination, to obtain honor. Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar said: 'By Allah, we were in Jahiliyya not considering women as anything until Allah revealed what He revealed about them and apportioned them what He apportioned.' Narrated by Bukhari and Muslim in a long hadith. And men being in charge over women does not contradict the aim of obtaining honor. Also, the form includes them definitely in the legal sense even if metaphorically, and it is impossible that they were unaware that the form included them. So the complaint about not being mentioned at all is not valid; the complaint is about the independent mention to obtain honor. So understand. And he argued thirdly: that it is the plural of masculine by consensus, and it is the plural to multiply the singular. So this plural is to multiply the masculine. It is objected that this is argument by terminology, for grammarians call it the plural of masculine, but that does not require that its singular be masculine. Don't you see that they say of words like as-sinun (years) that it is masculine while its singular is feminine according to them? This, and perhaps it is reasoned thus: This plural is the plural of masculine by agreement of grammarians, and the plural multiplies its singular, so its meaning is individuals from the masculine that is its singular. And this is not argument by terminology. And it is also said: the masculine according to them is what is free from taa and the like, even if females are included in it. Don't you see they say al-insan (human) is masculine while its individuals include females. So from it being a plural of masculine, it does not follow that its singular individuals cannot be female, as is clear to anyone with the least exposure. And al-Tahrir said: If it is said: If females enter into it, then the term 'plural of masculine' is a mere label, and its singular would also be feminine, so where does the taa of 'Muslima' go? This...
Source pages p. 262

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the scope of the sound masculine plural. The author presents the issue: does this plural include women along with men? The majority of Shafi'is and Malikis (and most Hanafis as implied) say no, it is specific to men; the Hanbalis say it includes both. The author (likely the commentator) selects the first position. The key evidence: the Quranic verse in Surah al-Ahzab (35) where men and women are listed separately using both masculine and feminine plurals. If the masculine plural included women, mentioning women afterward would be mere emphasis (ta'kid), which is weaker than giving independent mention (ta'sis). The principle of preferring ta'sis over ta'kid supports that the masculine plural is for men alone. A second argument: the hadith of Umm Salama/Umm 'Amara where women complained about not being mentioned, and then the verse was revealed. This shows that women themselves understood the masculine plural to exclude them. The Prophet's tacit approval affirms this understanding. Counterarguments are addressed: the complaint could be about independent mention, but that is far-fetched; women sought honor. The third argument: grammarians call it 'plural of masculine', implying it multiplies masculine singulars, but this is countered: grammarians call humans 'masculine' even though females are included, so the label doesn't prove exclusion of females. The passage ends with a quote from al-Tahrir questioning: if females are included, the term becomes a mere label, and feminine singulars would have no distinct form.

Key Terms
جمع المذكر السالم (jam' al-mudhakkar al-salim): A plural form that adds waw and noon or ya and noon to the masculine singular, and it distinguishes the singular from feminine by the presence or absence of taa marbuta.
تأسيس (ta'sis): An independent mention that adds a new meaning, as opposed to ta'kid (emphasis). In this context, mentioning women separately after the masculine plural is ta'sis.
تأكيد (ta'kid): Emphasis; repeating something already included. If the masculine plural included women, mentioning women afterward would be ta'kid, which is weaker.
تبادر (tabadur): The immediate understanding that comes to mind when a word is used without context. Here, the tabadur of the sound masculine plural is men.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what does the separate mention of 'muslimat' after 'muslimin' in Al-Ahzab 35 indicate?
A) That the sound masculine plural includes both men and women, and the mention is ta'kid.
B) That the sound masculine plural is specific to men, and the mention is ta'sis.
C) That the verse is ambiguous and requires further context.
D) That the sound masculine plural is metaphorical for men only.

Key Takeaway
The sound masculine plural is prima facie specific to men; separate mention of women is interpreted as ta'sis not ta'kid, and this is supported by both Quran and hadith.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
8 min
Do Sound Masculine Plurals Include Females? A Usul Dispute
Debating three proofs for excluding females from sound masculine plural

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the three arguments for excluding females from the sound masculine plural and derive the Hanafi position that 'Muslimun' includes both genders based on linguistic and textual evidence.

Core Idea
The sound masculine plural includes females when the singular denotes a common attribute, as in 'Muslimun' from 'Muslim' meaning any Muslim.

This passage tackles a classic usul question: does the sound masculine plural (e.g., al-Muslimun) refer only to men, or does it include women? The author presents three proofs that opponents use to restrict the plural to males, then systematically rebuts each using linguistic principles, Qur'anic verses, and the concept of taghlib (predominant treatment). By the end, you will see how Hanafi usul defends the inclusive scope of such plurals in Shari'a texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three proofs for exclusion
Read the passage to extract the three arguments: (1) the hadith of Umm Salama where women complained of not being mentioned, implying the plural excludes them; (2) the consensus of grammarians that it is 'masculine plural'; (3) the rule that the plural multiplies its singular, so if the singular is masculine the plural must be masculine.
The author responds to each in order.
2
Analyse the Hanafi response to each proof
For the hadith, the author argues the women wanted independent mention, not that the plural excluded them. For grammatical naming, he shows that 'masculine' refers to form, not gender (e.g., 'insan' is masculine but includes females). For the plural-rule, he demonstrates that the singular 'Muslim' in 'Muslima' means the absolute Muslim, not a male, so its plural includes all Muslims.
Pay attention to the distinction between independent mention and inclusion.
3
Understand the role of taghlib
The author introduces taghlib to explain how 'Muslimun' can include females without being metaphorical. Just as 'al-'Umarayn' includes Abu Bakr and 'Umar by predominant treatment, 'Muslimun' uses the masculine form as the lighter word to encompass both genders.
Taghlib does not turn the plural into a metaphor; it is a rule-based linguistic practice.
4
Examine the final linguistic analysis
The author proposes that 'Muslim' in compounds like 'Muslima' denotes the common meaning (absolute Islam), posited by a type-positing. The plural 'Muslimun' is built on that common meaning, so it literally refers to all who bear the attribute of Islam, male or female.
This avoids metaphor entirely, both in stem and structure.
5
Apply the ruling to a fiqh context
Consider a fiqh text: 'Whoever from the Muslims (man kana min al-Muslimin) must perform Jumu'ah.' According to the Hanafi position, the obligation applies to both men and women because 'Muslimin' includes females. Contrast with a school that restricts it to men.
This illustrates the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the usul dispute.

الحاصلة بعد التغليب بعد ثبوت القاعدة، ولا يلزم من التجوز في المادة انفراد التجوز فيها مع الهيئة بخلاف الأسود الرماة، فإن هذا الجمع من المجوز لا من الواضع وليس موضوعاً للشجعان لا نوعاً في ضمن قاعدة ولا شخصاً، ولك أن تمهد أولاً قاعدة هي أنه قد اتفق النحاة على أن مثل مسلمة لفظ مركب من المسلم والتاء، وكل منهما يدل على معناه فمعنى المسلم المفهوم منه حال مقارنته مع التاء ليس الرجل بخصوصه وإلا لزم اتصافه بالذكورة والأنوثة في حالة واحدة بل معناه مطلق الذات الموصوفة بالإسلام أعم من أن يكون مذكراً أو مؤنثاً، ونقول ثانياً : إن هذا المعنى قد وضع له لفظ مسلم البتة، وإلا لزم أن يكون مسلمة مجازاً لكون بعض مفرداته كذلك، إلا أن شرط الاستعمال فيه مقارنة اللفظ التاء، وهذا لا يخرج كونه حقيقة، ألا ترى أن الضمائر المتصلة حقائق استعمالها مقارنة العوامل بقي أن إطلاقه على الرجل خاصة حال انفراده إما لأنه موضوع له بوضع على حدة فيكون مشتركاً، أو لأنه وضع للقدر المشترك ليستعمل مجرداً في الذكر ومقارناً مع التاء في معناه المقيد بما يدل عليه التاء من الأنوثة كما أن لفظ هذا عند البعض موضوع لمعنى كليّ ليستعمل في الجزئيات، وحينئذٍ نقول : المسلمون جمع المسلم الذي وضع للقدر المشترك المستعمل في معناه في تركيب مسلمة، وعلى هذا ليس فيه تجوّز أصلاً، لا في المادة ولا في الهيئة، وإطلاقه على الذكور خاصة أما على الأول وكأنه بعيد فلاشتراك مفرده في المعنيين، أو لأنه كما يجوز استعمال مفرده في بعض الأفراد كذلك يجوز استعماله أيضاً، ويكون حقيقة لكونه استعمالاً في المفرد، ولا يجوز استعماله في الإناث المفردات، لأن مفرده كان لا يدل عليهم مجرداً عن التاء فتأمل فيه (و) بناء على هذا الذي ذكر من حديث عن مع
If it is intended that if mixing had a reality, it would be a terminological emphasis, then the entailment is denied. If it is intended the strengthening of the ruling itself, even in some individuals, then the priority of establishing (a new meaning) over it is denied, otherwise examples of sentences introduced by 'contrary to the principle' would be considered, so ponder. (And) he adduced as a second proof the tacit approval, i.e., the tacit approval of the Messenger of God, and the negation, i.e., the negation of believing women being mentioned, as narrated by Umm al-Mu'minin Umm Salama: she said, 'O Messenger of God, the women said: We see God mention only men.' So the verse was revealed: 'Indeed, the Muslim men and the Muslim women...' [33:35]. It was narrated by Ahmad, as in al-Tahrir. Al-Tirmidhi narrated from Umm 'Amara: she said, 'I came to the Prophet and said, I see everything goes to men, and I do not see women mentioned in anything.' So the verse was revealed. Al-Tirmidhi said: hasan gharib. So the women, despite being among the eloquent people of the language, when they denied their mention, they knew that the masculine word does not include them. Then the tacit approval of the Messenger indicates certainty. (And it was objected) by denying that they denied their own mention such that it would indicate the expression's non-inclusion of them, and by interpreting it as [denial of] independent mention. And therein: if the expression is for both men and women, then they are mentioned with the men. If by independent mention is meant their mention alone, then the men's mention is not independent either, so the complaint of men being mentioned independently without them is invalid. If by independent mention is meant their mention with intent, even if with others, then the complaint is baseless unless it is said that the expression's inclusion of the mixed group is only because they are followers of men, so they wanted to be mentioned independently without subordination. Reflect on it. The best is to interpret their statement 'We see God mention only men' as independent mention by other forms besides sound plurals, like 'al-rijal' and 'al-ibad', so they wanted to be mentioned likewise. So ponder. It was said that the complaint at that time, i.e., when desiring independent mention, is far-fetched, for men are maintainers over women, so they are followers. I say: perhaps their intent was to seek mention in that manner without subordination to attain honor. Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar said: By God, we in Jahiliyya did not count women as having any affair until God revealed what He revealed concerning them and apportioned for them what He apportioned. Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim in a long hadith. And men being maintainers over them does not negate the intention to attain honor. Also, the expression includes them definitely due to the general [scope] of the Shari'a, even if metaphorically, and it is impossible that they were unaware of the expression's inclusion of them. So the complaint of absolute non-mention is invalid; the complaint is only about independent mention to attain honor. So understand. (And) he adduced as a third proof that it is the masculine plural by consensus, and it is the plural to multiply the singular, so this plural multiplies the masculine. Therein: that it is an argument by naming, for grammarians call this plural 'masculine plural', and it does not necessitate that its singular be masculine. Do you not see that they call words like 'sinin' masculine while its singular is feminine according to them. This may be formulated as: this plural is the masculine plural by agreement of grammarians, and the plural multiplies its singular, so its meaning is individuals of the masculine that is its singular. This is not an argument by naming. Therein: that 'masculine' according to them is what is free from the ta' and the like, even if females are included in it. Do you not see that they said 'al-insan' is masculine while its individuals include females. So from its being a masculine plural it does not necessitate that its singular individuals cannot be female, as is not hidden from anyone with even a little exposure. (And) he said in al-Tahrir: If it is said: if females enter it and it becomes a masculine plural by mere naming, then its singular is also feminine, so where does the ta' of 'Muslima' go? This plural is one where the letters of the singular remain obligatorily. (It was said) in answer: the position of the ta' here is its position in 'Talhun' according to the opinion of the Kufan scholars. The conclusion is that the retention of the ta' in the plural is not necessary, as per the Kufan grammarians, like in 'Talhin' plural of 'Talha'. (I say: the question does not arise such that an answer is needed. It would only arise if it were said that it is the plural of 'Muslima', necessitating that the plural has two singulars, for it includes males too, so its singular would also be masculine. Thus it is not the plural of 'Muslima' but the plural of 'Muslim' stripped of the ta' that was inserted into 'Muslima' when intending to form this plural by way of predominant treatment (taghlib), like 'Umarayn' meaning the Caliph of the Messenger of God, Abu Bakr, and Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar, or Amir al-Mu'minin 'Umar ibn 'Abd al-Aziz. For when intending to express the two, the Caliph of the Messenger is included in the meaning of the word 'Umar by way of taghlib due to similarity in praiseworthy traits. It is as if one intended one who possesses these praiseworthy traits. In taghlib, the lighter word is chosen. If it is said: then the plural becomes metaphorical. He said: metaphor in 'Muslim' its singular does not necessitate metaphor in its plural when it is known that it is a rule, for then it is posited by a type-positing for the individuals resulting after taghlib. And the objection was settled in Sharh Matla' al-Asrar al-Rabbaniyya that the stem of the plural then is definitely metaphorical even if the form is literal, for the stem of the singular is metaphorical due to taghlib even if the form and structure are literal. Do you not see that the word 'al-aswad al-rumat' is metaphorical in terms of stem even if literal in terms of structure. But this is nothing, for the stem and structure are posited by type-positing for the individuals resulting after taghlib after establishment of the rule. And metaphor in the stem alone does not entail metaphor in it together with the structure, unlike 'al-aswad al-rumat', for this plural is from the non-posited speaker, not from the positer, and it is not posited for the brave neither as a type under a rule nor individually. You may first lay down a rule: that grammarians agree that a word like 'Muslima' is composed of 'al-Muslim' and the ta', each indicating its meaning. So the meaning of 'al-Muslim' understood from it when accompanied by the ta' is not specifically a man, otherwise it would be described with masculinity and femininity simultaneously. Rather, its meaning is the absolute entity described with Islam, whether masculine or feminine. Second, we say: this meaning has certainly been posited for the word 'Muslim'; otherwise, 'Muslima' would be metaphorical because some of its parts are metaphorical, except that the condition of its usage is the accompaniment of the ta'. This does not negate its being literal. Do you not see that attached pronouns are literal despite their usage being conditional upon the governing words? It remains that its application to specifically a man when alone is either because it is posited for it by a separate positing, making it a homonym, or because it is posited for the common meaning to be used alone for the male and accompanied by the ta' for its meaning restricted by what the ta' indicates of femininity, just as the word 'hadha' according to some is posited for a general meaning to be used for particulars. Then we say: 'al-Muslimun' is the plural of 'Muslim' which is posited for the common meaning used in its meaning in the composition of 'Muslima'. On this, there is no metaphor at all, neither in stem nor in structure. Its application to males specifically, as for the first view, it is unlikely due to the homonymy of its singular in two meanings, or because just as its singular may be used for some individuals, so may its plural also be used, and it is literal because it is usage in the plural, and it is not permissible to use it for female individuals because its singular did not indicate them when stripped of the ta'. So reflect on it. (And) based on what was mentioned from the hadith about 'with'.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive usul disputation regarding the linguistic scope of the sound masculine plural (جمع المذكر السالم). The author — likely 'Abd al-'Ali al-Ansari, commentator on Musallam al-Thubut — presents and rebuts three arguments for excluding females from the plural, then provides a detailed linguistic analysis based on the Hanafi usul framework.

The first proof is from the hadith of Umm Salama and Umm 'Amara. Women complained that only men were mentioned in the Qur'an, so the verse including 'Muslimin and Muslimat' was revealed. The opponent argues that this shows women understood the masculine plural to exclude them. The author counters: the women's complaint was about independent mention, not about non-inclusion in the plural. They wanted to be mentioned specifically, not just as followers. The word 'Muslimin' actually includes them, even if only metaphorically. The Prophet's tacit approval (تقرير) of their complaint was not about the plural's non-inclusion but about their desire for explicit mention.

The second proof: grammarians call it 'masculine plural' by consensus, implying it only refers to males. The author replies that 'masculine' here means the word form free of the feminine marker (ta'), not that all referents are male. For example, 'sinin' (years) is grammatically masculine though its singular 'sana' is feminine, and 'insan' (human) is masculine though it includes females. So the naming does not prove exclusion.

The third proof is the grammatical rule that the sound plural multiplies its singular; if the singular is masculine, the plural must be masculine. The author delves into a detailed analysis of words like 'Muslima' and 'Muslimun'. He clarifies that 'Muslim' in 'Muslima' is not specifically male but a common meaning (absolute Muslim). The plural 'Muslimun' is built on this common meaning, not on the specific male. The insertion of ta' in 'Muslima' is for femininity, and the plural is formed from the base 'Muslim' stripped of ta'. In 'Muslimun', the ta' is dropped and the waw-nun suffix is added. This is like 'Talhun' from 'Talha' (Kufan view). Thus there is no metaphor: the plural is literal and includes both genders.

The author then discusses the principle of taghlib (predominant treatment), where a lighter word is used to include others, as in 'al-'Umarayn' for Abu Bakr and 'Umar. Applying taghlib to 'Muslim' when forming the plural does not make the plural metaphorical; rather, the stem 'Muslim' in the plural is posited by type-positing for the mixed group. The correct view is that 'Muslim' in 'Muslima' means the absolute entity, so its plural includes all who bear that description, male or female. 

Throughout, the author defends the Hanafi position that the sound masculine plural is not restricted to males but includes females when the context allows, based on Shari'a usage and linguistic analysis. The commentary guides the student to understand the subtle interplay between grammar, usul, and revealed texts.

Key Terms
جمع المذكر السالم (jam' al-mudhakkar al-salim): A plural form that adds waw and nun to the masculine singular, or ya' and nun in oblique cases, and is called 'masculine' because its singular is grammatically masculine.
تغليب (taghlib): A linguistic principle where a word denoting one gender is used to include the other when the context requires, e.g., 'al-Muslimun' including women, or 'al-'Umarayn' for two persons sharing the name 'Umar' and a non- 'Umar'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, does the sound masculine plural 'Muslimun' include Muslim women?
A) Yes, always, as the singular 'Muslim' means any Muslim regardless of gender.
B) No, it only includes men; women require the feminine plural.
C) It includes them only when there is a separate mention (like 'wa al-Muslimat').
D) It includes them metaphorically, but not literally.

Key Takeaway
The sound masculine plural, when built on a common attribute, literally includes both genders, and this is supported by Qur'anic usage and Hanafi linguistic analysis.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Taghlib in Collective Masculine Plural: Literalness by Type-Derivation
Analysis of taghlib rule for plural forms and its legal application.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi argument that the plural 'Muslimūn' remains literal even when including females by taghlib, and evaluate the Hanbali counter-argument.

Core Idea
Taghlib creates a new literal form via type-coinage, not metaphor.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into a subtle but crucial linguistic principle in usul al-fiqh: taghlib (dominance/overwhelming). When the masculine plural includes females, is it literal or metaphorical? The Hanafi school, relying on the concept of type-derivation (al-wad' al-naw'i), argues that such plurals are literal. The Hanbalis counter with Quranic evidence and the principle of literalness. Understanding this debate shapes how we interpret general addresses like 'Muslimūn' in legal texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
Determine the exact question: When the collective masculine plural (e.g., Muslimūn) includes females by taghlib, is it literal (haqīqah) or metaphorical (majāz)?
This is a linguistic-legal question with practical consequences for who is addressed by general commands.
2
Analyze the Hanafi defense
Examine the argument that after taghlib, the plural is subject to type-coinage (al-wad' al-naw'i), making it a new literal form for the individuals resulting after taghlib.
The author uses the example of 'Umarayn' and compares with the problematic 'al-aswad al-ramāh'.
3
Examine the Hanbali proof
Look at the Quranic verses cited by the Hanbalis (2:36, 2:61) where the masculine plural is used for mixed groups, and their reliance on the principle of literalness as default.
They argue that if it is used for both, it must be literal for the combination.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal: the first understanding (tabādura) of the plural is males alone; inclusion of females is metaphorical via a general Sharia indication, not through the word itself.
The author stresses that taghlib does not make the plural metaphorical because a new type-coinage applies.
5
Note the practical outcome
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf: legal addresses like 'Muslimūn' default to males unless a general Sharia indication extends to females, as in known rulings, but for specific rulings like Friday prayer and jihad, the lack of such indication keeps them male-specific.
This shows how the linguistic debate affects fiqh rulings.

الجمع مما يبقى فيه حروف المفرد وجوباً (قيل) في الجواب مذهب التاء ههنا (مذهبها في طلحون على رأي أئمة (الكوفة والحاصل أن بقاء التاء في الجمع غير لازم كما عليه أئمة الكوفة النحاة، كما في طلحين جمع طلحة (أقول السؤال غير وارد حتى يحتاج إلى الجواب و(إنما يرد لو قيل إنه جمع مسلمة، ويلزم أن يكون للجمع (مفردان فإنه شامل للذكور أيضاً، فيكون مفرده مذكراً أيضاً، فليس هو جمع مسلمة (بل هو جمع مسلم مجرم مجرداً التاء (أدخلت عن فيه مسلمة عند إرادة تأليف هذا الجمع تغليباً (كعمرين المراد منه خليفة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أبو بكر وأمير المؤمنين عمر ، أو أمير المؤمنين عمر بن عبد العزيز فإنه عند إرادة التعبير عنهما أدخل خليفة رسول الله في مدلول لفظ عمر تغليباً للمشابهة في الأخلاق الحميدة، فكأنه أريد من به هذه الأوصاف الحميدة، وفي التغليب يختار اللفظ الأخف، فإن قيل : فعلى هذا يصير الجمع مجازاً قال ولا يلزم
