وإن كان الصيغة حقيقة، فإن مادة المفرد، وهو مجاز للتغليب وإن كان الصيغة والهيئة حقيقة، ألا ترى أن لفظ المادة هو ، مع شرط الأسود الرماة مجاز باعتبار المادة وإن كان حقيقة باعتبار الهيئة وهذا ليس بشيء فإن المادة الهيئة موضوعة بالوضع النوعي للآحاد الحاصلة بعد التغليب بعد ثبوت القاعدة، ولا يلزم من التجوز في المادة انفراد التجوز فيها مع الهيئة بخلاف الأسود الرماة، فإن هذا الجمع من المجوز لا من الواضع وليس موضوعاً للشجعان لا نوعاً في ضمن قاعدة ولا شخصاً، ولك أن تمهد أولاً قاعدة هي أنه قد اتفق النحاة على أن مثل مسلمة لفظ مركب من المسلم والتاء، وكل منهما يدل على معناه فمعنى المسلم المفهوم منه حال مقارنته مع التاء ليس الرجل بخصوصه وإلا لزم اتصافه بالذكورة والأنوثة في حالة واحدة بل معناه مطلق الذات الموصوفة بالإسلام أعم من أن يكون مذكراً أو مؤنثاً، ونقول ثانياً : إن هذا المعنى قد وضع له لفظ مسلم البتة، وإلا لزم أن يكون مسلمة مجازاً لكون بعض مفرداته كذلك، إلا أن شرط الاستعمال فيه مقارنة اللفظ التاء، وهذا لا يخرج كونه حقيقة، ألا ترى أن الضمائر المتصلة حقائق استعمالها مقارنة العوامل بقي أن إطلاقه على الرجل خاصة حال انفراده إما لأنه موضوع له بوضع على حدة فيكون مشتركاً، أو لأنه وضع للقدر المشترك ليستعمل مجرداً في الذكر ومقارناً مع التاء في معناه المقيد بما يدل عليه التاء من الأنوثة كما أن لفظ هذا عند البعض موضوع لمعنى كليّ ليستعمل في الجزئيات، وحينئذٍ نقول : المسلمون جمع المسلم الذي وضع للقدر المشترك المستعمل في معناه في تركيب مسلمة، وعلى هذا ليس فيه تجوّز أصلاً، لا في المادة ولا في الهيئة، وإطلاقه على الذكور خاصة أما على الأول وكأنه بعيد فلاشتراك مفرده في المعنيين، أو لأنه كما يجوز استعمال مفرده في بعض الأفراد كذلك يجوز استعماله أيضاً، ويكون حقيقة لكونه استعمالاً في المفرد، ولا يجوز استعماله في الإناث المفردات، لأن مفرده كان لا يدل عليهم مجرداً عن التاء فتأمل فيه (و) بناء على هذا الذي ذكر من حديث عن 6 مع اندفع) ما قيل : يلزم أن يكون الجموع كلها مما لا واحد له من لفظه) وذلك لأن مفرده مسلم لكن مغلباً، وقد يقال : يلزم أن يكون الجموع كلها مما لم يكن له مفرد مستعمل أصلاً، وفيه أنه لا استحالة فيه إن أريد أن لا يكون له مفرد مستعمل حقيقة بل هو أول المسألة، وإن أريد أن لا يكون مستعملاً أصلاً لا حقيقة ولا مجازاً ، فاللزوم ،ممنوع كيف المجاز بالتغليب شائع فتدبر، وهو لو سلك السبيل الذي بينا لا يرد هذا السؤال من أصله لأن مفرده مستعمل في ضمن استعمال مسلمة، ثم قيل : لا يصح حديث التغليب فإنه لو كان مفرده مسلماً أدخل فيه المسلمة تغليباً لصح: نساء مسلمون، إذ حينئذ النساء والرجال سواسية في الفردية، ولو كان الاختلاط معتبراً لما صح الإطلاق على الرجال وحدهم، وهذا ليس بشيء، فإن المقصود أن المسلمين جمع مسلم أدخل فيه المسلمات المختلطة مع المسلمين تغليباً فالإناث من أفراده مقارنة مع الرجال لا وحدهن فتدبر الحنابلة (قالوا :أولاً : صح الجمع المذكر لهما نحو) قوله تعالى : اهْبِطُوا بَعْضُكُمْ لِبَعْضٍ عَدُوٌّ ﴾ [البقرة : ٣٦] وقوله تعالى : اهْبِطُوا مِصْرًا فَإِنَّ لَكُم مَّا ﴿ سَأَلْتُهُ ﴾ [البقرة : ٦١] (كما يصح (للمذكر فقط والأصل) في الاستعمال (الحقيقة أقول ذلك) أي كون الأصل الحقيقة إذا لم يكن لأحدهما بخصوصه حقيقة، وهو ممنوع) فإنه قد تقدم أن المتبادر منه الرجال وحدهم فيكون حقيقة فيهم وهذا إنما يتم لو سلم الخصم الاستقراء (وأجيب أيضاً بلزوم (الاشتراك اللفظي إذ لا نزاع لأحد في أنه للرجال وحدهم حقيقة) فلو كان للمختلط أيضاً حقيقة يلزم الاشتراك، وهو خلاف الأصل (قيل : عدم النزاع في أن إطلاقه للرجال وحدهم بخصوصهم حقيقة (ممنوع فإنهم يقولون بالاشتراك المعنوي) فوضعه للقدر المشترك بين الرجال وحدهم والمختلطين مع النساء وإطلاقه عليهم وحدهم حقيقة من حيث إنه من أفراد الموضوع (له لا من حيث خصوصهم فحينئذ لا يلزم الاشتراك اللفظي (و) قالوا (ثانياً : لو لم يدخلن في هذا الجمع لما شمل الأحكام لهن إذ حينئذٍ أمثال ﴿وَأَقِيمُوا الصَّلَوةَ [البقرة : ٤٣] من الخطابات مختصة بالرجال (أقول) الملازمة ممنوعة فإن دخولهن في الخطاب بطريق المجاز بقرينة عموم الشريعة القاطعة كما قال : لما علم عموم الشريعة للنساء ضرورة) من الدين (وقد ثبت عموم الصيغة لهن (لغة ولو تجوزاً حملنا عليه) بهذه القرينة، والحال أنه يجوز أن يكون مجازاً من جهة التغليب بقرينة عموم الشريعة لكن للخصم أن يقول : التجوز خلاف الأصل، فلا يصار إليه، فيدفع بدعوى التبادر الذي مر إن سلم الخصم فافهم (ولذا) أي لأجل أن شمول الأحكام لهن لقرينة عموم الشريعة (لم يحمل عليه فيما لا يعلم) عمومه من الأحكام (كالجمعة والجهاد وغيرهما والخصم يقول إنما لم يحمل فيها لقرينة اختصاص هذه الأحكام بالرجال (ويجاب في المشهور) بمنع بطلان اللازم إن أريد عدم الشمول صيغة وبالتزام عدم الشمول نصاً بل الشمول (بالإجماع) أو بدليل آخر، كتنقيح المناط، وحكمي على الواحد حكمي على الجماعة، كما في المعدوم زمن الخطاب الشفاهي (وفيه ما فيه) فإن الإجماع متأخر عن زمن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم ، والكلام في ذلك الزمن بل الإجماع انعقد على الاستدلال بهذه الخطابات لشمول الأحكام لهن فالإجماع دل على شمول النص لهن من غير
The plural that retains the letters of the singular obligatorily (it was said) in the answer the view of the feminine tā' here (their view in Talhun according to the opinion of the Kufan grammarians) and the conclusion is that the retention of tā' in the plural is not necessary as per the Kufan grammarians, as in Talhin plural of Talhah. I say: the question is not valid so as to need an answer. It would only be valid if it were said that it is the plural of Muslimah, and then it would be necessary for the plural to have two singulars, since it includes males too, so its singular would also be masculine. So it is not the plural of Muslimah, but rather the plural of Muslim stripped of tā', with tā' introduced into its meaning of Muslimah when intending to form this plural by taghlīb (dominance), like Umarayn meaning the Caliph of the Messenger of God, Abu Bakr and Amir al-Mu'minin Umar, or Amir al-Mu'minin Umar ibn Abd al-Aziz; when intending to express both, the Caliph of the Messenger of God is included in the meaning of the word Umar by taghlīb due to similarity in praiseworthy morals, as if what is intended is whoever possesses these praiseworthy attributes. In taghlīb, the lighter word is chosen. If it is said: on this basis, the plural becomes metaphorical. He said: there is no necessary consequence from the metaphor in (Muslim) its singular to metaphor in its plural if it is known that it is a rule, for then it is stipulated by type-derivation for the individuals resulting after taghlīb. The doubt in Sharh Matla' al-Asrar al-Rabbaniyyah is stated that the material of the plural then is metaphorically certain, even if the form is literal. For the material of the singular, which is metaphorical due to taghlīb, even if the form and structure are literal. Do you not see that the word al-aswad al-ramāh is metaphorical with respect to material even if literal with respect to structure? And this is nothing, for the material and structure are stipulated by type-derivation for the individuals resulting after taghlīb after the rule is established. Metaphor in the material does not necessitate that it is metaphorical alone together with the structure, unlike al-aswad al-ramāh, for that plural is from (coiner) not from the legislator, and it is not stipulated for the brave, neither type-wise within a rule nor individually. You may first lay down a rule: that the grammarians have agreed that like Muslimah is a compound word of al-Muslim and tā', and each indicates its meaning. So the meaning of al-Muslim understood from it when accompanied by tā' is not specifically man, otherwise it would be described by masculinity and femininity at the same time. Rather its meaning is the absolute essence described by Islam, whether male or female. Secondly, we say: this meaning has certainly been stipulated for the word Muslim, otherwise Muslimah would be metaphorical because some of its parts are like that. However, the condition of its usage is the accompaniment of tā', and this does not remove it from being literal. Do you not see that attached pronouns are literal, their usage is accompanied by governors? Remaining: its application to man specifically when isolated is either because it is stipulated for him by a separate coinage, so it becomes homonymous, or because it is stipulated for the common meaning to be used bare for the male and accompanied by tā' in its meaning restricted to what tā' indicates of femininity, just as the word 'this' according to some is stipulated for a universal meaning to be used for particulars. Then we say: al-Muslimūn is the plural of al-Muslim which is stipulated for the common meaning used in its meaning in the construction of Muslimah. On this, there is no metaphor at all, neither in the material nor in the structure. Its application to males specifically: either according to the first, and it is far-fetched due to the homonymy of its singular in two meanings, or because just as it is permissible to use its singular for some individuals, so it is also permissible to use it, and it is literal because it is usage in the singular, and it is not permissible to use it for female singulars, because its singular does not indicate them without tā'. So contemplate. And based on this mentioned above, what was said is repelled: that it would be necessary that all plurals have no singular from their word. Because its singular is Muslim but with taghlīb. And it may be said: it would be necessary that all plurals have no singular used at all. And in it, there is no impossibility if what is meant is that it has no singular used literally, but that is the beginning of the issue. If what is meant is that it is not used at all, neither literally nor metaphorically, then the necessary consequence is denied. How is metaphor by taghlīb common? So contemplate. And if he took the path we have shown, this question does not arise at all, because its singular is used within the usage of Muslimah. Then it was said: the hadith of taghlīb is not valid, for if its singular were Muslim including Muslimah by taghlīb, it would be correct to say 'Muslim women (nisā' muslimūn)', because then women and men are equal in individuality. If mixing were considered, it would not be correct to use it for men alone. This is nothing, for the intent is that al-Muslimūn is the plural of Muslim, into which Muslim women mixed with Muslim men are included by taghlīb. So females are among its individuals when with men, not alone. So contemplate. The Hanbalis said: First: the masculine plural is valid for both, like His statement, exalted: 'Descend, some of you enemies of others' (Quran 2:36) and His statement 'Descend to Egypt, for you will have what you asked' (2:61), just as it is valid for males only. And the principle in usage is literalness. I say that: meaning that the principle is literalness when there is no literal meaning for one of them specifically, and it is denied. For it has preceded that the first understanding from it is males alone, so it is literal for them. This is only complete if the opponent concedes the induction. And it was also answered by the necessity of lexical homonymy, for no one disputes that it is literally for males alone. So if it were also literal for the mixed, homonymy would result, which is against principle. It was said: the lack of dispute that its usage for males alone specifically is literal is denied, for they say it is by common meaning. So it is stipulated for the common meaning between males alone and those mixed with women, and its usage for them alone is literal in that they are individuals of the subject matter, not in terms of their specificity. So then lexical homonymy does not follow. And they said: Secondly: if they (women) were not included in this plural, then rulings would not include them, for then like 'Establish prayer' (2:43) among addresses would be specific to men. I say: the necessary consequence is denied, for their inclusion in the address is by way of metaphor with the indication of the categorical generality of the Sharia, as he said: because the generality of the Sharia for women is known necessarily from religion, and the generality of the formula for them has been established linguistically, even if metaphorically, we apply it to them with this indication. And the situation is that it is permissible for it to be metaphorical by way of taghlīb with the indication of the generality of the Sharia, but the opponent can say: metaphor is against principle, so it should not be resorted to. So it is refuted by claiming the first understanding which has passed, if the opponent concedes. So understand. And for this reason, i.e., because the inclusion of rulings for them is due to the indication of the generality of Sharia, it is not applied where its generality is not known, like Friday prayer, jihad, etc. And the opponent says that it was not applied there due to the indication that these rulings are specific to men. And the answer in the well-known view is by denying the nullity of the necessary consequence if what is meant is non-inclusion in terms of wording, and by accepting non-inclusion textually, but inclusion by consensus or by another proof, like refinement of the ascertainment, and 'my ruling on one is my ruling on the group', as in the case of those non-existent at the time of oral address. And there is something in it, for consensus is later than the time of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, and the discussion is about that time. Rather, consensus was established on using these addresses to include rulings for them, so consensus indicated the inclusion of the text for them without...
Source pages p. 264

Commentary
This passage is an analysis of the linguistic principle of 'taghlib' (dominance/overwhelming) as applied to the collective masculine plural form in Arabic, and its implications for legal interpretation in usul al-fiqh. The author begins by discussing the plural of 'Muslimah' (a female Muslim), arguing that the plural 'Muslimūn' is not directly the plural of 'Muslimah' but rather of 'Muslim' (the male), with the tā' (feminine marker) being introduced by taghlib when females are included. This is analogous to the dual form 'Umarayn' which is used to refer to two great caliphs by including one under the name of the other due to shared praiseworthy attributes. The author defends that this does not make the plural metaphorical; rather, the plural is a new type-derived form that is literal because it is coined for the individuals resulting after taghlib. The doubt from Sharh Matla' al-Asrar is raised that the material (the root meaning) is metaphorical, but the author refutes this by distinguishing between material and structure and by asserting that the plural is coined by type-derivation after the rule is established. A foundational rule is laid down: the word 'Muslim' when combined with tā' indicates the absolute essence described by Islam, not specifically male or female. The plural 'Muslimūn' is then the plural of 'Muslim' which means the common meaning, and when applied to males alone it is still literal because it is an instance of the common meaning. The Hanbali argument is presented: they cite Quranic verses like 'Descend' (2:36, 2:61) where the masculine plural includes both genders. They argue that the principle is literalness, so if the plural is used for both, it must be literal for that. The author responds by denying that the first understanding is only for males, and that homonymy would result if it were literal for both, but the Hanbalis avoid that by claiming a common (ambiguous) meaning. The Hanbalis also argue that without such inclusion, commands like 'Establish prayer' would only apply to men. The author replies that women are included by way of metaphor with the indication of the generality of Sharia, but the opponent objects that metaphor is not the default. The author explains that for known rulings this is accepted, but for rulings like Friday prayer and jihad, which are specific to men, the plural is not applied to women. Ultimately, the debate hinges on whether the plural is literal or metaphorical when applied to mixed groups, with the Hanafi position asserting that it is literal by type-derivation, and the Hanbalis arguing for a common meaning that includes both.

Key Terms
التغليب (al-taghlīb): The linguistic principle of including a secondary element under a primary element in a compound due to shared attributes.
الحقيقة (al-ḥaqīqah): Literal meaning; usage of a word for what it was originally coined for.
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical or figurative meaning; usage for something other than its original coinage.
الوضع النوعي (al-waḍʿ al-nawʿī): Type-derivation coinage; a linguistic rule where patterns derive words for specific types of entities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the plural 'Muslimūn' remain literal even when it includes females by taghlib?
A) Because females are not actually included in the meaning.
B) Because the plural is metaphorically extended but taken as literal for legal purposes.
C) Because the plural is coined by type-derivation for the individuals resulting after taghlib, making it a new literal form.
D) Because the Quran uses it metaphorically and we follow that.

Key Takeaway
Taghlib, when established as a rule, produces a new literal form via type-coinage, so the collective masculine plural remains literal even when including females.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Taghlib: Does the Masculine Plural Literally Include Women? A Hanafi-Hanbali Usul Dispute
Examines the principle of taghlib and the dispute over literal inclusion of women in masculine plural forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of tabāddur (immediate understanding) to determine the literal reality of a term and evaluate the Hanbali counter-argument regarding taghlib.

Core Idea
The masculine plural is literally for men only; inclusion of women is metaphorical via taghlib.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a classic usul dispute: when the Quran addresses 'believers' (muslimūn), does the word itself include women? The Hanafis argue that the immediate understanding (tabāddur) of 'muslimūn' is men, so inclusion of women requires a metaphorical step. The Hanbalis counter that the word is literally general. This lesson unpacks the proof and the practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested term
Recognize that the dispute centers on the masculine plural (جمع مذكر) and whether it is literally general or specific to males.
Hanafis rely on tabāddur (immediate understanding) to assert specificity.
2
Apply the Hanafi tool of tabāddur
Consider what a native speaker immediately understands from 'muslimūn' absent context. Hanafis argue it is men alone.
Tabāddur is a key Hanafi method for determining haqīqah.
3
Examine the Hanbali proof
The Hanbalis cite Quranic verses (2:36, 2:61) where the masculine plural (e.g., 'ihbitū') addresses both men and women, claiming literal generality.
They treat these as evidence that the form is amm (general) for both genders.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis argue that inclusion in these verses is metaphorical (tajawuz) due to a contextual indication (qarīna), not literal. The immediate understanding remains men.
They maintain that shifting from haqīqah to majāz requires a strong qarīna.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In rulings like Jumu'ah and jihad, Hanafis exclude women because the specific qarīna (evidence) shows these are for men; Hanbalis may include them if they take the form as general.
The dispute affects many fiqh issues where masculine plurals occur.

The third article on linguistic principles: Taghlib. It is refuted what was said: 'It would necessitate that all plurals have no singular from their own word form' — because its singular is conceded but overwhelmed. It is said: It would necessitate that all plurals have no singular used originally at all. In it there is no impossibility if what is meant is that they have no singular used in reality, that is the beginning of the issue. If what is meant is that it is not used at all, neither reality nor metaphor, then the implication is denied; how can it be that metaphor by taghlib is widespread? So ponder. If he had taken the path we showed, this question would not arise at all because its singular is used within the usage of musallama. Then it was said: The concept of taghlib is invalid, because if its singular were musallam, then including the feminine by taghlib would yield 'women are Muslims' being correct, since then women and men are equal in individuality. If mixing were considered, application to men alone would not be valid. This is nothing, for the point is that 'Muslimin' is the plural of 'Muslim' into which Muslim women mixed with Muslim men are included by taghlib; females are among its individuals alongside men, not alone. So ponder. The Hanbalis said: First, the masculine plural is correct for both genders as in His statement: 'Descend, some of you as enemies to others' (2:36) and His statement: 'Descend to Egypt, for you will have what you asked for' (2:61) just as it is correct for males only. The principle in usage is reality. I say that: meaning that reality is original when neither has a specific reality, and it is rejected. For it has preceded that the immediate understanding is men alone, so it is reality in them. This is only complete if the opponent concedes the induction. And it was also answered by necessitating lexical homonymy, since no one disputes that it is reality for men alone. If it were also reality for the mixed, then homonymy would be necessitated, which opposes the principle. It was said: The claim of no dispute that its application to men alone specifically is reality is rejected, for they hold to conceptual homonymy. So it is placed for the common core between men alone and those mixed with women, and applying it to them alone is reality in that it is from individuals of the subject matter, not from their specificity. Then homonymy is not necessary. And they said: Secondly, if women were not included in this plural, then rulings would not apply to them, since then verses like 'Establish prayer' (2:43) would be exclusive to men. I say: The implication is rejected, for their inclusion in the address is through metaphor by the indication of the pervasive generalness of the Shari'ah, as it is said: When the generalness of the Shari'ah for women is known necessarily from religion, and the generalness of the formula for them has been established linguistically even if metaphorically, we apply it by this indication. And the case is that it is permissible to be metaphorical by taghlib through the indication of the generalness of the Shari'ah, but the opponent may say: Metaphorical usage is contrary to the principle, so it is not resorted to. This is repelled by the claim of immediate understanding which passed, if the opponent concedes. So understand. Therefore, i.e., because the inclusion of rulings for them is due to the indication of the generalness of the Shari'ah, it is not applied in what is not known to be general from the rulings, like Friday prayer, jihad, and others. The opponent says: They were not applied therein due to the indication of the specificity of these rulings to men. And in the well-known view it is answered by denying the invalidity of the concomitant if what is meant is non-inclusion in the formula, and by affirming non-inclusion in the text, rather inclusion by consensus or by another proof, like tanqih al-manat, and my ruling on the single is my ruling on the group, as in the case of the non-existent at the time of direct address. It has some problem, for consensus came after the time of the Messenger of Allah, and the discussion is at that time. Rather consensus was established on using these addresses for inclusion of rulings for them, so consensus indicated the inclusion of the text for them without needing a separate proof. So ponder. Note: It was said in the books of most shaykhs that the statement of the Hanbalis is exactly the statement of the Hanafis, and it is argued by their statement in the case of the warring person: 'Give me assurance for my sons and I will give assurance' that his daughters enter into the assurance. If the formula were not encompassing, they would not enter. The more apparent is that their entry is because assurance is something to be cautious about, so it was interpreted as general metaphorically. This does not indicate that their entry is by way of reality. And it is not far-fetched to say: If this were a metaphor for the sake of caution, it would necessitate establishing assurance by metaphorical interpretation in every word occurring in assurance, and that is not so. It may be objected that the establishment of assurance is by indication of the text, and there is no need for their entry in the formula. And know that if this attribution to the Hanafis is due to the mentioned branch, then what was objected would apply, but the narrators transmitted from the Hanafis in this way, and the author of al-Badi' said: Most of our companions held this view. And if their statement is established from another angle, then there is no harm in building the mentioned branch upon it, rather it is then the determined view. And Allah knows best His rulings.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage from the third article on linguistic principles deals with the concept of 'taghlib' (overwhelming) in Arabic. Taghlib refers to using a masculine plural form to include females when they are mixed with males, even though the form is primarily for males. The author begins by addressing an objection: if taghlib is allowed, then every plural would have no singular derived from the same root? No, because the singular exists but is overwhelmed. Then the author rejects the Hanbali argument that the masculine plural is equally valid for both genders, showing that the immediate understanding is men alone. The Hanafis argue that when women are included in a command like 'Establish prayer', it is via metaphorical extension (tajawuz) by the indication (qarīna) of the general comprehensiveness of the Shari'ah, not because the word itself is literally inclusive. This is supported by the fact that in rulings like Jumu'ah and jihad, women are not included because there is a specific indication that those are for men. The author concludes with a note that some Hanafi scholars actually agree with the Hanbali view, citing the example of granting aman (security) where daughters are included under 'bani' (sons) by caution. However, this is a metaphorical expansion, not literal. The passage demonstrates the Hanafi approach to usul: the default is reality (haqiqah), and shifting to metaphor requires a strong indication.

Key Terms
تغليب (taghlīb): The principle where a term primarily for one gender (masculine) is applied to include the other gender when they are mixed.
جمع مذكر (jam' mudhakkar): The masculine plural form typically used for rational beings; its literal reality is for males only.
تبادر (tabāddur): The immediate mental understanding that arises from a word when heard without context.
قرينة (qarīna): An indication or contextual clue that modifies the literal meaning, allowing metaphorical interpretation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, when the Quran commands 'Establish prayer' (أَقِيمُوا الصَّلَاةَ), are women included literally?
A) Yes, because the masculine plural literally includes both genders.
B) No, they are included only by metaphorical extension due to the indication of the Shari'ah's general comprehensiveness.
C) They are not included at all; the command is only for men.
D) They are included legally but excluded linguistically.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school maintains that the masculine plural is literally for men only; inclusion of women requires a qarīna and is metaphorical (taghlib), not literal.

Default Inclusion of Slaves in General Legal Addresses
Analyzing default inclusion of slaves in general addresses across rights of Allah and rights of servants

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the default rule for including slaves in general legal addresses and apply the Hanafi principle of linguistic generality versus customary exclusion.

Core Idea
General addresses include slaves by default; any exclusion requires proof from evidence or established custom.

This lesson tackles a foundational usul question: do general legal commands (like 'O believers' or 'whoever steals') automatically include slaves? The Hanafi majority holds that they do, based on linguistic generality. However, some scholars argue for default exclusion, citing the master's ownership of the slave's benefits. We will examine the arguments, the key distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants, and the role of custom ('urf) in shaping legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core positions
First, locate the three main positions: majority (default inclusion), minority (default exclusion), and Abu Bakr al-Jassas's distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants. Note the evidence each side uses.
The text explicitly groups these positions at the start.
2
Analyze the majority's proof
The majority argues that linguistic expressions are for generality ('umum). Any change due to custom ('urf) must be proven. Examine how they refute the claim that exclusion of slaves from some rulings (like jihad) establishes a general custom.
The key phrase: 'al-sighah kanat lil-‘umum'.
3
Examine the opposing proof
The minority argues that since a slave's benefits are owned by the master, commanding the slave would contradict that ownership. Therefore, the legislator's usage never intended slaves. Assess this argument and the Hanafi response that ownership is not absolute.
Consider the example of prohibitions like zina or stealing.
4
Apply the distinction between rights of Allah and rights of servants
Al-Jassas argues that in rights of Allah (e.g., prayer, fasting), defaults include slaves, except where clear harm to the master (like Friday prayer). In rights of servants (e.g., contracts), the default is exclusion because custom points to freemen. Evaluate whether this distinction is justified.
The text uses inductive survey (istiqra') to support this.
5
Determine the operative rule and its practical consequence
The text favors the majority position but acknowledges al-Jassas's nuance. For fatwa, a slave committing theft would face hadd punishment. Understand how the burden of proof shifts between positions.
This illustrates the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference).

الخطاب الذي يعم العبيد لغة هل يتناولهم شرعاً أو لا، قال الأكثر : نعم يتناولهم فيعم الحكم لهم وقيل : لا يتناولهم فلا يعمهم الحكم وقال الشيخ أبو بكر الجصاص الرازي الحنفي رحمه الله تعالى : يتناولهم في حقوق الله تعالى فقط لا في حقوق العباد، تحرير محل النزاع أنه لا شك أن من الخطابات ما يتناول الكل من الأحرار والعبيد بالاتفاق، ومنها ما يختص الأحرار فقط بالإجماع، وإنما النزاع في أن الظاهر شرعاً ما هو، فعند الأكثر الظاهر التناول كما كان لغة فيحتاج في عدمه إلى دليل آخر، وقيل: الظاهر عدم التناول، فيحتاج في شمول الحكم والتناول إلى دليل زائد وعند الشيخ أبي بكر التفصيل بحقوق الله تعالى وحقوق العباد (لنا) الصيغة كانت للعموم والتناول وما عرف عرف طار على اللغة يخرجها عن مقتضاها لغة وإن دل دليل على الخروج أي خروج العبيد عن بعض الخطابات كالجهاد والحج إلى غير ذلك ولا يلزم منه العرف هذا وفيه إشارة إلى رد استدلال النافي بأن حكم الجهاد ونحوه لا يتناول العبيد، فلو لم يكن خارجاً عن الخطاب لزم النسخ، وجه الرد أنها خارجة فلا نسخ، لكن لدليل فلا يلزم العرف فافهم، ولك أن تقول : استقرى الأحكام الشرعية فوجد أكثرها المتعلق بحقوق الله تعالى شاملة لهم إلا ما فيه ضرر بين للمولى كالجمعة والحج والجهاد قبل النفير العام، وأما سائر النواهي كالزنا والشتم والكفر والقتل والغصب فشاملة قطعاً، فلا يمكن فيها ادعاء العرف من أحد، وأما حقوق العباد فأكثرها مختصة بالأحرار، وقلما يدخل فيها العبيد فلا يبعد أن يدعي فيها العرف ويقال : إن عرف الشارع حاكم بعدم دخولهم فيها إلا بالدليل فإن الأكثر عدم الدخول والظن تابع للأغلب فكلما ورد الخطاب الشرعي المتعلق بحقوق العباد يتسارع الذهن إلى اختصاصه بالأحرار، وهذا معنى العرف فقوله : وما عرف عرف طار مطلقاً ممنوع ، النافون للدخول قالوا : منافع العبد مملوك لسیده شرعاً، والخطاب لهم ينافيه أي ينافي ملك السيد منافعه فلم يكن مراداً في الاستعمال أي استعمال الشارع قط فيتسارع الذهن إلى الأحرار وهو معنى الاختصاص بالأحرار عرفاً، أقول إذا زيد فلم يكن مراداً في الاستعمال قط فلا يرد ما قيل إن الخروج لأجل لزوم محال على تقدير الدخول وهو المنافاة بين تعلق الخطاب بهم ومملوكية المنافع لا يمنع التناول صيغة إذ يجوز أن يكون لزوم المحال قرينة صارفة عن مقتضاها، وجه عدم الورود أن المحال لما كان لازماً في الاستعمالات الشرعية كلها لم يكن العبد مراداً قط فلزم العرف والجواب : لا نسلم عموم مملوكية المنافع في الأوقات كلها بل خص منها البعض وهو المنافع التي تمنع امتثال أوامر الله تعالى فلم يثبت العرف وهذا تام في حقوق الله تعالى. وأما المنافع المانعة عن امتثال أوامر الشرع في حقوق العباد فأكثرها مملوكة للسيد، وله أن يمنعه عن العمل بالأوامر ويشغله بخدمته، ففيها للمناقشة مجال، وقد يقرر بأن مملوكية المنافع دلت على الخروج، ولو في بعض الأحكام فاحتمل كل خطاب خروجهم، واحتمال المخصص يوجب الوقف كما مر من أنه لا يجوز العمل بالعام قبل البحث عن المخصص، فوجب التوقف إلى قيام الدليل على الدخول وجوابه أن الاحتمال العقلي مسلم، والاحتمال العرفي ممنوع، فلا يجب التوقف وقد مر أيضاً الخروج في بعض الأحكام لا يوجب الاشتمال ثم التوقف قبل البحث عن المخصص لو تم لدل على التوقف في الدخول وعدمه وكان عدم الدخول عرفاً والظهور فيه فتدبر الشيخ أبو بكر المفصل بين الحقوق الإلهية والعبدية ادعي حدوث العرف فيما ليس من حقوقه تعالى وفيها باق كما كان في اللغة، قال المصنف ومن ادعى فعليه البيان أي هذه دعوى من غير دليل لكن دليل الاستقراء الحاكم بأكثرية الخروج إن تم تم الكلام .
Issue: Does the address which includes slaves linguistically include them legally or not? The majority said: Yes, it includes them, so the ruling applies to them. And it was said: It does not include them, so the ruling does not apply to them. And Abu Bakr al-Jassas al-Razi al-Hanafi (may Allah have mercy on him) said: It includes them only in the rights of Allah (huquq Allah), not in the rights of servants (huquq al-‘ibad). Determination of the point of dispute: There is no doubt that among the addresses, some include all, free and slave, by agreement, and some are specific to freemen only by consensus. The dispute is only over what is the apparent (zahir) meaning legally. According to the majority, the apparent is inclusion as in language, so non-inclusion requires another evidence. And according to the other view, the apparent is non-inclusion, so inclusion and application require additional evidence. And according to Abu Bakr, there is a distinction between the rights of Allah and the rights of servants. (Our proof): The expression was for generality and inclusion, and whatever customary usage (‘urf) emerges afterward that removes it from its linguistic implication, unless evidence indicates exclusion (i.e., exclusion of slaves from some addresses like jihad, hajj, etc.). And this does not necessitate that ‘urf is operative. This contains a refutation of the deniers’ argument: 'The ruling of jihad and similar matters does not include slaves. If they were not excluded from the address, it would require abrogation (naskh).' The refutation: They are excluded but thanks to evidence, so no abrogation, and ‘urf is not necessitated. Understand. And you may say: One may survey the legal rulings and find that most related to the rights of Allah include them except where there is clear harm to the master like Friday prayer, Hajj, and jihad before general mobilization. As for other prohibitions like adultery, insult, disbelief, murder, and usurpation, they include them definitively, so no one can claim ‘urf in them. As for rights of servants, most are specific to freemen, and slaves rarely enter into them. So it is not far-fetched to claim ‘urf there and say: 'The legislator’s custom (urf al-shari‘) rules that they are not included except with evidence, because most cases show non-inclusion, and probability follows the majority.' So whenever a legal address concerning rights of servants appears, the mind hastens to its specific application to freemen. This is the meaning of ‘urf. So his statement: 'Whatever custom emerges (wa ma ‘urifa ‘urfun tarun)’ is absolutely denied. The deniers of inclusion said: The benefits (manafi‘) of the slave are owned by his master legally, and the address to slaves contradicts that (i.e., contradicts the master’s ownership of his benefits). Therefore, the slave was never intended in the legislator’s usage, so the mind hastens to free persons. This is the meaning of specification to freemen customarily. I say: If one adds 'was never intended in usage at all', then the objection does not arise: 'The exclusion is because of the impossibility that would follow from inclusion (i.e., contradiction between addressing them and ownership of benefits) does not prevent the linguistic inclusion, because it is possible that the impossibility is a context that diverts from its implication.' The reason the objection does not arise is that since the impossibility was necessary in all legal usages, the slave was never intended, so ‘urf becomes established. The answer: We do not concede the generality of ownership of benefits in all times. Rather, some of them are excepted—those benefits that prevent compliance with the commands of Allah. Thus, ‘urf is not established. This is complete for the rights of Allah. As for the benefits preventing compliance with legal commands in the rights of servants, most are owned by the master, and he may prevent the slave from doing the commanded acts and occupy him with his service. So there is room for discussion there. And it may be argued that ownership of benefits indicates exclusion, even in some rulings, so every address is probable of their exclusion. And the probability of a specification requires suspension (waqf), as previously that one cannot act upon the general before investigating for a specification. So suspension is necessary until evidence of inclusion appears. Its answer: The rational probability is conceded, but the customary probability is denied, so suspension is not necessary. It has also passed that exclusion in some rulings does not necessitate inclusion. Then suspension before searching for a specification, if it were valid, would indicate suspension regarding inclusion and non-inclusion, and non-inclusion would be the custom and the apparent therein. So reflect. Abu Bakr made a distinction between divine rights and servile rights. He claimed that ‘urf developed regarding what is not from the rights of Allah, whereas in those rights it remained as in language. The author said: 'Whoever claims something must provide proof (al-bayan).' That is, this is a claim without evidence, but if the evidence from inductive survey (istiqra‘) ruling with majority exclusion is complete, then the discussion is complete.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage addresses a core usul question: Do general legal addresses (like 'O believers' or 'whoever steals') include slaves by default? The majority of Hanafis hold that they do, because the linguistic generality carries over unless a specific evidence excludes them. A minority says the opposite: the default is that slaves are not included, requiring evidence for inclusion. Abu Bakr al-Jassas distinguishes between rights of Allah (e.g., prayer, zakat) and rights of servants (e.g., contracts, compensation). The Hanafi arguments rely on the principle that language is general and that any change due to custom (‘urf) must be proven. The opponents argue that since a slave's benefits are owned by his master, commands that require his own effort are contradictory; thus, the legislator never intended slaves in such addresses. The Hanafi response is that ownership of benefits is not absolute; the slave must still obey divine commands. The discussion includes a detailed refutation and ends with the author stating that a claimant must provide proof, and the inductive argument is considered.

Key Terms
حقوق الله تعالى (huquq Allah ta'ala): Rights of Allah: duties primarily toward God, like prayer, fasting, zakat, and hudud.
حقوق العباد (huquq al-‘ibad): Rights of servants: duties owed to other humans, like contracts, torts, and financial obligations.
عرف (‘urf): Customary usage of the lawgiver (shar‘) that may override linguistic generality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the default status of slaves regarding general legal addresses?
A) Slaves are not included unless there is evidence for inclusion.
B) Slaves are included unless there is evidence for exclusion.
C) Slaves are included in rights of God but not in rights of servants.
D) Slaves are never included in any legal address.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the default presumption is that general legal expressions include all individuals, including slaves, unless a specific evidence or established custom excludes them.

Inclusion of the Prophet in General Commands
Dispute over inclusion of the Prophet in general commands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the three positions on whether the Prophet is included in general commands, apply the Hanafi argument from combination of roles, and analyze the opposing argument regarding commanders not being commanded.

Core Idea
The Prophet is included in general commands unless excluded — roles can combine from different perspectives.

This lesson addresses a fundamental usul al-fiqh dispute: whether the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) is included in general commands addressed to the ummah. Three views exist: inclusion, absolute exclusion, and a detailed view based on the word 'qul'. The Hanafi school adopts inclusion, arguing from the generality of language and the possibility of combining roles. We will trace the arguments and counterarguments in the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
List the three views on inclusion: (1) The Prophet is included customarily (Hanafi preferred view). (2) He is not included at all. (3) Al-Halimi's differentiation: if the command is prefaced by 'qul', the Prophet is not included; otherwise, he is.
Note that the Hanafi position is called 'al-mukhtar'.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Identify the two main proofs: (1) Generality of language – the command's form is general. (2) The Prophet is obligated by the Sharia like everyone else. Also, the practice of the Companions who understood inclusion and the Prophet's confirmation.
The proof from practice is strong: the Companions asked the Prophet when he did not act on a general command, implying they assumed inclusion.
3
Examine the opposing objection
The objector says: The Prophet is a commander (amir) and a deliverer (muballigh), so he cannot be commanded or delivered to. This would be contradictory if he were included in general commands.
This objection relies on the logical incompatibility of the roles.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response uses three arguments: (1) Roles can combine from different perspectives (like a doctor treating himself). (2) The true commander is Allah; the Prophet is only a narrator. (3) The Prophet in relation to himself is neither commander nor deliverer; these apply only to others.
The key is distinguishing perspectives: relative to others he is a commander; relative to himself he is commanded.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: for a general command like 'Establish prayer', the Hanafi view holds that the Prophet was included and obligated, while the opposing view might exclude him. This affects how we understand the Prophet's actions and the scope of the command.
The Hanafi view aligns with the fact that the Prophet did pray and follow all commands.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية

لسیده شرعاً، والخطاب لهم (ينافيه) أي ينافي ملك السيد منافعه (فلم يكن مراداً في الاستعمال أي استعمال الشارع (قط) فيتسارع الذهن إلى الأحرار (وهو معنى الاختصاص بالأحرار عرفاً، أقول إذا زيد فلم يكن مراداً في الاستعمال قط فلا يرد ما قيل إن الخروج لأجل لزوم محال على تقدير الدخول) وهو المنافاة بین تعلق الخطاب بهم ومملوكية المنافع (لا يمنع التناول صيغة إذ يجوز أن يكون لزوم المحال قرينة صارفة عن مقتضاها، وجه عدم الورود أن المحال لما كان لازماً في الاستعمالات الشرعية كلها لم يكن العبد مراداً قط فلزم العرف (والجواب : لا نسلم عموم مملوكية المنافع في الأوقات كلها (بل خص منها البعض) وهو المنافع التي تمنع امتثال أوامر الله تعالى (فلم يثبت العرف وهذا تام في حقوق الله تعالى. وأما المنافع المانعة عن امتثال أوامر الشرع في حقوق العباد فأكثرها مملوكة للسيد، وله أن يمنعه عن العمل بالأوامر ويشغله بخدمته، ففيها للمناقشة مجال، وقد يقرر بأن مملوكية المنافع دلت على الخروج، ولو في بعض الأحكام فاحتمل كل خطاب خروجهم، واحتمال المخصص يوجب الوقف كما مر من أنه لا يجوز العمل بالعام قبل البحث عن المخصص، فوجب التوقف إلى قيام الدليل على الدخول وجوابه أن الاحتمال العقلي مسلم، والاحتمال العرفي ممنوع، فلا يجب التوقف وقد مر أيضاً الخروج في بعض الأحكام لا يوجب الاشتمال ثم التوقف قبل البحث عن المخصص لو تم لدل على التوقف في الدخول وعدمه وكان عدم الدخول عرفاً والظهور فيه فتدبر الشيخ أبو بكر المفصل) بين الحقوق الإلهية والعبدية ادعي حدوث العرف فيما ليس من حقوقه تعالى وفيها باق كما كان في اللغة، قال المصنف ومن ادعى فعليه (البيان أي هذه دعوى من غير دليل لكن دليل الاستقراء الحاكم بأكثرية الخروج إن تم تم الكلام .
مطلقاً،
مدعاه
مسألة
النبي صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم داخل عرفاً (في العمومات) الشاملة له (وقيل: لا يدخل هو صلى الله عليه وسلم مطلقاً ( وفصل) أبو عبد الله الحسين (الحليمي الشافعي، وقال: (إن كان) الخطاب (مصدّراً بالقول كقل يا عبادي لم يشمله ، وإلا شمله صلى الله عليه وسلم لنا وجود المقتضى وهو عموم اللغة) فإن المفروض الكلام في الخطاب الشامل لغة مع عموم الشريعة) فإن الرسول الله مكلف بالشرائع أيضاً وعدم المانع وهو إباء التركيب) فإن التركيب غير آب عنه، قيل : المفصل لا يساعد عليه أي على عدم إباء التركيب إذ المتبادر بلفظ : قل لبني تميم افعلوا كذا خروج المخاطب وإن كان داخلاً في بني تميم (أقول : الفرق بينه وبين : يا بني تميم (افعلوا بدون كلمة : قل (تحكم فإن كليهما نداء لبني تميم، والمنادي لا ينادي نفسه، فلو اقتضى هذا عدم دخول المتكلم لم يدخل في الصورتين وإن كان الدخول في: يا بني تميم، لأجل كون المتكلم حاكياً والمنادى غيره، ففي قل: يا بني تميم، أيضاً كذلك، فإن المخاطب ههنا أيضاً حاك، والخطابات الإلهية كلها سواء كانت مصدرة بقل أولا الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم

عنهم
فواتح الرحموت شرح مسلم الثبوت الجزء الأول
حاك لها، ثم إن المصدّر بقل يحتمل معنيين : أحدهما أن يكون المقصود الأمر للمخاطب بالأمر لبني تميم، وحينئذ يكون المخاطب آمراً ،حقيقة وحينئذ لا يتناول المخاطب بقل البتة والثاني أن يكون المقصود الآمر بالحكاية والكلام لغيره حقيقة فحينئذ يتناول قطعاً، فإنه ليس آمراً حقيقة، بل هو مأمور من الآمر مع غيره، لكن مع هذا مأمور بحكاية هذا الأمر، فإن أراد الحليمي بالمصدر بكلمة قل ما يكون المقصود منه الأول، فمنع القائل يتجه، وإلا فلا فتدبر (واستدل) على المختار بأن (الصحابة رضوان الله تعالى عليهم فهموه) أي التناول، وقرره رسول الله لا لا لأنه إذا لم يعمل بمقتضاه سألوه عن الموجب للترك (فذكره) ولو لم يفهموه لم يكن لسؤالهم وجه (أقول) لا يلزم من سؤالهم وفهمهم العموم تناول الصيغة (بل يكفي بعموم الشريعة دليلا على فهم التناول (وأيضاً) ما قالوا (منقوض بالمسألة الآتية من عدم دخول المعدومين، فإن الصحابة رضي الله فهموا تناول الحكم إياهم (فتدبر) المخصصون بالأمة (قالوا: (أولاً) رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم (آمر ، فلا يكون مأموراً) للمنافاة، وتناول الخطاب يقتضي المأمورية (و) رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم (مبلغ فليس مبلغاً إليه والدخول في الخطاب يلزمه كونه مبلغاً إليه، لأنه له المبلغ إلى كل مكلف ويجاب أولاً بأنه يجوز) اجتماع الآمرية والمأمورية، وكونه مبلغاً ومبلغاً إليه من جهتين كالطبيب إذا عالج نفسه) فهو معالج من حيث هو طبيب ومعالج من حيث هو مريض إن قيل : الآمر أعلى مرتبة من المأمور) فهما متنافيان لتنافي اللوازم فلا يجتمعان في ذات والمبلغ يعلم الخطاب قبل المبلغ إليه فلو اجتمعا ذات يلزم علمه بالخطاب قبل علمه (قلنا لا نسلم علو الأمر، فإن العلو ليس بشرط في الأمر، بل يكفي الاستعلاء، ولما لم يكن هذا المنع مفيداً في المقام أعرض عنه، وقال: (لو سلم فبحيثية الآمرية والمبلغية أعلى وأقدم على نفسه من حيث المأمورية وكونه مبلغاً إليه، قال مطلع الأسرار الإلهية»: إنه لا فائدة في التبليغ إلى نفسه لأن المقصود من التبليغ علم المبلغ إليه، والعلم لما كان حاصلاً لا حاجة إلى التبليغ وكذا لا فائدة في أمر نفسه (و) يجاب (ثانياً بأن الآمر هو الله تعالى لا غيره (والمبلغ جبريل والرسول صلوات الله عليه وآله وأصحابه (حاك) فحينئذ يمنع الآمرية وكونه مبلغاً (أقول : يرده قوله تعالى: ﴿وَأُولِي الأَمرِ ﴾ [النساء : ٥٩] هذا سهو من الناسخ والصواب وأولي الأمر (منكم فإنه ) أعلى منا وإذا كان الأدنى آمراً فالأعلى بالطريق الأولى (و) يرده أيضاً قوله تعالى : بَلَغَ مَا أُنزِلَ إِلَيْكَ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦٧] الآية فإن الخطاب العام للنبي الله منه ) أي مما أنزل فهو عليه السلام مبلغ قطعاً فلا مجال للمنع، وتخصيص الخطابات العامة بعيد كل البعد (و) يجاب (ثالثاً : بأنه عليه وآله وأصحابه وأزواجه الصلاة والسلام بالقيام إلى نفسه ليس ،آمراً ولا مبلغاً وإن كان بالقياس إلى غيره آمراً له ومبلغاً، فلا يلزم اجتماع الآمرية والمأمورية فيه الله ولا كونه مبلغاً ومبلغاً إليه، ولو أرجع الجواب الثاني إليه لم يبعد، فإن المجيب به شارح المختصر، ولم يجب هو بهذا الجواب، يرد عليه ما أورد أقول يرد عليه) قوله تعالى : ( بَلّغ مَا أُنزِلَ [المائدة: ٦٧] فإن) كلمة ما عامة والخطابات العامة منه فيكون هو صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم مبلغاً لهذه

The third article on linguistic principles.

... (the slave) is owned by the master legally, and the address to them (contradicts it) i.e. contradicts the master's ownership of his benefits (so he was never intended in the usage i.e. the usage of the Lawgiver (ever) so the mind races to free persons (and that is the meaning of specificity to free persons customarily). I say: if it is added then he was never intended in usage ever, so what is said that exclusion is because of impossibility upon the assumption of inclusion is not objectionable) and it is the contradiction between attaching the address to them and the ownership of benefits (does not prevent the form from encompassing, for it is permissible that the impossibility is a contextual indicator diverting from its requirement. The reason for non-objection is that since impossibility was necessary in all legal usages, the slave was never intended, so custom became established (and the answer: we do not concede the generality of ownership of benefits in all times (rather some of them are excepted) i.e. the benefits that prevent compliance with the commands of Allah (so custom is not established and this is complete regarding the rights of Allah. As for benefits preventing compliance with the commands of the Law in the rights of people, most of them are owned by the master, and he can prevent him from acting upon commands and occupy him with his service, so in these there is room for discussion. And it may be established that ownership of benefits indicates exclusion, even in some rulings, so every address carries the possibility of their exclusion, and the possibility of a specifier necessitates suspension as previously mentioned that one cannot act upon the general before seeking the specifier, so suspension is necessary until evidence for inclusion is established. Its answer is that rational possibility is conceded, but customary possibility is denied, so suspension is not necessary. And it has also passed that exclusion in some rulings does not necessitate inclusion. Then suspension before seeking the specifier, if valid, would indicate suspension in both inclusion and non-inclusion, and non-inclusion would be customary and apparent, so reflect. Sheikh Abu Bakr Al-Mufassal differentiated between divine and servitude rights; he claimed that custom occurred in what is not of His rights, and in them it remained as in language. The author said: whoever claims, upon him is (the explanation i.e. this is a claim without evidence. But the evidence of induction ruling by the majority of exclusion, if valid, then the matter is settled absolutely.

His claim

Issue: The Prophet (peace be upon him and his family and companions) is included customarily (in the general commands) encompassing him (and it is said: he (peace be upon him) is not included absolutely (and Abu Abdullah Al-Husayn Al-Halimi Al-Shafi'i) differentiated, and said: (If) the address (is prefaced by 'say' like 'Say: O My servants' it does not include him, otherwise it includes him). For us is the existence of the effective cause which is the generality of language) for the assumed speech is about the address that is general linguistically along with the generality of the Sharia) for the Messenger of Allah is also obligated by the Shari'ah and the absence of a preventer which is the composition's refusal) for composition is not averse to it. It was said: the differentiated view is not supported i.e. not on the lack of composition's aversion, for what is immediately understood from the phrase 'Say: O Banu Tamim, do such-and-such' is the exclusion of the addressee even if he is included in Banu Tamim. (I say: the difference between it and 'O Banu Tamim' (do) without the word 'Say' is arbitrary, for both are calls to Banu Tamim, and the caller does not call himself. If this necessitated the non-inclusion of the speaker, he would not be included in both forms, although inclusion in 'O Banu Tamim' is due to the speaker being a narrator and the called is other. So in 'Say: O Banu Tamim' it is also like that, for the addressee here is also a narrator, and all divine addresses, whether prefaced by 'say' or not, the Prophet (peace be upon him) is a narrator for them. Then that which is prefaced by 'say' carries two meanings: one is that the intended is to command the addressee to command Banu Tamim, and then the addressee is a commander truly, and then it does not include the addressee of 'say' at all. The second is that the intended is the commander to narrate and the speech is truly for others, then it includes him definitely, for he is not truly a commander, but is commanded by the commander along with others, but with this he is commanded to narrate this command. If Al-Halimi meant by the prefacing with 'say' the first meaning, then the objector's objection is valid, otherwise not, so reflect. (And he argued) for the chosen view that (the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) understood it) i.e. inclusion, and the Messenger of Allah confirmed it, because if he did not act upon its requirement they asked him about the reason for leaving (and he mentioned it) and if they did not understand it, their question would have no basis. (I say: their understanding and question does not necessitate the form's inclusion, but rather the generality of the Sharia suffices as evidence for understanding inclusion (and also) what they said (is refuted by the following issue of non-inclusion of the non-existent, for the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) understood the inclusion of the ruling for themselves, so reflect) the specifiers of the Ummah. (They said: (first) the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him and his family) is a commander, so he cannot be commanded) due to contradiction, and inclusion of the address necessitates being commanded (and) the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is a deliverer, so he is not the one to whom something is delivered, and inclusion in the address necessitates him being one to whom something is delivered, because he is the deliverer to every obligated person. And it is answered first: that (it is permissible) to combine commanding and being commanded, and being a deliverer and being delivered to from two perspectives, like a doctor when he treats himself) he is a treater from the perspective of being a doctor and is treated from the perspective of being a patient. If it is said: the commander is of a higher rank than the commanded) so they are contradictory due to the contradiction of their implications and cannot combine in one essence, and the deliverer knows the address before the one delivered to, so if they combine in one essence, it would necessitate his knowledge of the address before his knowledge. (We say: we do not concede the higher rank of the commander, for higher rank is not a condition in commanding; rather self-elevation suffices. And since this prevention was not useful in this context, he turned away from it and said: (if conceded, then from the perspective of commanding and delivering, he is higher and precedes himself from the perspective of being commanded and delivered to. The author of 'Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya' said: there is no benefit in delivering to oneself because the purpose of delivery is the knowledge of the one delivered to, and since knowledge is already present there is no need for delivery, and likewise there is no benefit in commanding oneself (and) it is answered (secondly: that the commander is Allah alone, not other, and the deliverer is Jibril, and the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and companions) is a narrator, so then commanding and his being a deliverer are prevented. (I say: this is refuted by His statement: 'and those in authority among you' [4:59] this is a mistake from the copyist, the correct is 'and those in authority among you' for he is higher than us, and if the lower is a commander, then the higher is all the more so) and also refuted by His statement: 'Convey what has been revealed to you' [5:67] for the general address to the Prophet from it i.e. from what was revealed, so he (peace be upon him) is definitely a deliverer, so there is no room for prevention, and specifying general addresses is very far (and) it is answered (thirdly: that he (peace and blessings be upon him and his family and wives) in relation to himself is not a commander nor a deliverer, although in relation to others he is a commander and deliverer, so there is no combination of commanding and being commanded nor his being a deliverer and delivered to. And if one were to refer the second answer to it, it would not be far, for the answerer is the commentator of Al-Mukhtasar, and he did not answer with this answer. It is objected to him what was presented. I say it is objected to him) by His statement: 'Convey what has been revealed' [5:67] for the word 'what' is general and the general addresses from it, so he (peace be upon him and his family and companions) is a deliverer of these

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul discussion about whether the Prophet Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم) is included in general commands addressed to the Muslim community. The author presents multiple views and engages in a scholarly debate.

The passage begins by discussing a related issue about slaves not being addressed due to ownership of benefits, which establishes a custom that slaves are excluded from general commands. This is used to illustrate how custom can restrict general language. The discussion then moves to the main issue: is the Prophet included in general commands?

Three positions are presented:
1. The Prophet is included customarily in general commands (al-mukhtar, the chosen view).
2. The Prophet is not included at all.
3. A detailed view by Al-Halimi: if the command is prefaced by 'qul' (say), the Prophet is not included; otherwise he is.

The defenders of the chosen view argue based on the generality of language and the fact that the Prophet is obligated by the Sharia like everyone else. They also cite the practice of the Companions who understood the Prophet to be included and asked him about commands when he did not act upon them, which the Prophet confirmed.

Objections are raised: the Prophet is a commander and a deliverer, so he cannot be commanded or delivered to. These objections are answered in three ways. First, it is possible to combine these roles from different perspectives, like a doctor treating himself. Second, the true commander is Allah, and the Prophet is only a narrator; the roles are distinct. Third, the Prophet in relation to himself is neither commander nor deliverer; these roles apply only to others.

The passage also critiques the second answer using Quranic verses (4:59 and 5:67) to show that the Prophet is indeed a commander and a deliverer, reinforcing the point that he is included in general commands.

The commentary emphasizes that the preferred view in the Hanafi school is the inclusion of the Prophet in general commands unless there is specific evidence to exclude him. This is a matter of usul al-fiqh that affects how we understand the scope of Quranic and Prophetic commands.

Key Terms
العام (al-'amm): A general term that includes all individuals to whom it applies, unless there is evidence for specification.
المخصص (al-mukhassis): Evidence that restricts the scope of a general term.
الآمرية والمأمورية (al-amiriyyah wa al-ma'muriyyah): The quality of commanding and being commanded. The text argues they can combine in one person from different perspectives.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in this passage, is the Prophet Muhammad (صلى الله عليه وسلم) included in general commands addressed to the ummah?
A) Yes, he is included customarily.
B) No, he is never included.
C) Only if the command is not prefaced by 'qul'.
D) Only if the command is about divine rights.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the Prophet is included in general commands customarily, based on the generality of language and the possibility of combining roles from different perspectives.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
15 min
Inclusion of the Prophet in General Commands
Dispute on whether the Prophet is included in general addresses to the ummah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi argument for the Prophet's inclusion in general commands and analyze the counterarguments regarding his special role.

Core Idea
The Prophet is included in general commands unless proven otherwise; partial exclusion does not negate overall inclusion.

A key question in usul al-fiqh is whether the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) is included in general Quranic commands addressed to the believers. The Hanafi school holds that he is included, while some scholars argue that his unique roles as commander and conveyer preclude his being commanded. This lesson unpacks the arguments on both sides and traces how the Hanafis defend their position, using analogies and textual proofs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
The Hanafi position: the Prophet is included in general commands unless specific evidence excludes him. The opposing position: the Prophet is not included because he is a commander and conveyer, and has special rulings.
The dispute centers on whether the Prophet's roles prevent his inclusion.
2
Examine the opponents' dalil
Opponents argue that the Prophet as āmir (commander) and muballigh (conveyer) cannot be ma'mūr (commanded) or muballagh ilayhi (conveyed to) – these would conflict in one person.
They also point to special rulings like prohibition of charity as evidence of exclusion.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to the role objection
The Hanafis respond that roles can combine from different aspects, like a doctor treating himself. Also, the true commander is Allah, and the Prophet is merely a conveyer. The Prophet's standing to himself is not that of a commander.
This refutes the incompatibility claim.
4
Evaluate the special rulings objection
The Hanafis argue that partial exclusion from some rulings (e.g., prohibition of charity) does not prove total exclusion from all general commands. Analogies: the sick, traveler, and menstruating woman are excepted from some obligations but not all.
This preserves the general inclusion unless proven otherwise.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: whether the Prophet was obligated by general commands like fasting Ramadan. Hanafis say yes, opponents say no. This affects how we understand the Prophet's actions and their legal implications.
The Hanafi view supports the Prophet's full participation in communal obligations.

ولم يجب هو بهذا الجواب، يرد عليه ما أورد أقول يرد عليه) قوله تعالى : ( بَلّغ مَا أُنزِلَ [المائدة: ٦٧] فإن) كلمة ما عامة والخطابات العامة منه فيكون هو صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم مبلغاً لهذه الخطابات أيضاً وفيه أن بلغ ما أنزل إنما يستدعي أن يكون مبلغاً للكل لا أن يكون مبلغاً بالنسبة إلى كل مكلف إذ المتبادر منه بلغ ما أنزل إليك من الخطابات العامة والخاصة إلى غيرك من المكلفين والخطابات العامة يجوز أن يكون الرسول داخلاً فيها ويكون مبلغاً لها بالنسبة إلى أغياره هذا (و) قالوا (ثانياً) رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مخصوص بأحكام كوجوب ركعتي الفجر) غير الفرض على ما حققه المتأخرون (و) وجوب صلاة الضحى) وهذا غير صحيح، فإنه قد ثبت تركها من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم (و) وجوب صلاة (الأضحى) وهذا غير صحيح على رأينا، فإن صلاة الأضحى واجبة عندنا على الكل (وحرمة أخذ الصدقة فإن قلت حرمة أخذ الصدقة غير مخصوصة به عليه السلام بل متناولة لكل بني هاشم قلت المراد بها صدقة التطوع، وليست هي محرمة على بني هاشم إلا على رسول الله ، كذا في «الكشف»، على أن حرمة التصدق المفروض على سائر بني هاشم بالتبع لا بالأصالة (و) حرمة (خائنة الأعين) وفسرت بالإشارة إلى الإيلام المباح من القتل والضرب على خلاف ما يظهر روي في التواريخ بسند متصل أنه جاء أمير المؤمنين عثمان بعبد الله بن سرح إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فأجرى عبد الله كلمة الشهادة على اللسان وقد كان أهدر دمه قبل فقال بعد ذهابه : «هلا قتلتموه قبل أن يقول كلمة الشهادة فقالوا : لو أشرت بعينك فقال : لا يحل» أو «لا ينبغي للنبي خائنة الأعين» (وإباحة النكاح من غير شهود ومهر وولي) هذا لا يصح عندنا، فإن النكاح بلا ولي صحيح عندنا، على أنه إن أراد نکاحه من غير ولي له فهذا عام في نكاح كل رجل، وإن أراد أن نكاحه من المرأة من غير ولي لها ففيه أنه عليه السلام ولي كل مسلم ومسلمة (و) إباحة (الزيادة على أربع بل على تسع كما قالت أم المؤمنين عائشة رضي الله عنها إنه ما خرج رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدنيا إلا وقد أباح له الله تعالى النساء كلها إلى غير ذلك مما اختص به عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (فدل) هذا الاختصاص على عدم المشاركة في العموم (والجواب أن دلالته على عدم المشاركة في العموم ممنوعة و أن الخروج من البعض بدليل لا يوجب الخروج) من كل عام (مطلقاً، كالمريض والمسافر والحائض) خرجوا عن بعض الخطابات ولا يلزم الخروج منه مطلقاً.
He narrated it. Then the one initiated by 'qul' (say) has two meanings: one is that the intended meaning is the command to the addressee to command the Banu Tamim, and then the addressee is a commander in reality, and then it does not include the addressee of 'qul' at all. The second is that the intended meaning is the commander, by way of narration, and the speech is for others in reality, so then it includes him definitely, for he is not a commander in reality, but rather he is commanded by the commander along with others, but nevertheless he is commanded to narrate this command. If al-Halimi intended by the infinitive with the word 'qul' the first meaning, then the proponent's objection is directed, otherwise no. Reflect. And he adduced evidence for the chosen view that the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) understood it, i.e., inclusion, and the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) confirmed it? No, because when he did not act upon its implication, they asked him about the reason for leaving, and he mentioned it. Had they not understood it, their question would have no basis. I say: It does not follow from their question and understanding the general inclusion of the formula; rather, the generality of the Shariah suffices as evidence for understanding inclusion. Also, what they said is refuted by the coming issue of the non-inclusion of the non-existent, for the Companions understood the inclusion of the ruling for them. Reflect on those who specify for the ummah. They said: First, the Messenger of Allah is a commander, so he cannot be commanded, due to incompatibility, and inclusion in the address necessitates being commanded. And the Messenger of Allah is a conveyer, so he is not one to whom conveyed, and entering into the address entails him being one to whom conveyed, because he is the conveyer to every obligated person. The answer first: It is allowed for commanding and being commanded, and being a conveyer and being conveyed to, from two aspects, like a doctor when he treats himself; he is a treater in that he is a doctor and a patient in that he is a sick person. If it is said: The commander is higher in rank than the commanded, so they are incompatible due to incompatibility of implications, so they cannot combine in one essence, and the conveyer knows the address before the one conveyed to, so if they combine in one essence, it necessitates his knowledge of the address before his knowledge. We say: We do not accept the highness of the commander, for highness is not a condition in commanding; rather, superiority suffices. And since this objection was not useful in this context, he turned away from it and said: Even if we accept it, by virtue of being a commander and a conveyer he is higher and advances upon himself in terms of being commanded and being conveyed to. The author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: There is no benefit in conveying to oneself because the purpose of conveying is the knowledge of the one conveyed to, and since knowledge is already present, there is no need for conveying, and likewise there is no benefit in commanding oneself. And the second answer: The commander is Allah alone, no other, and the conveyer is Jibril, and the Messenger and his Companions are narrators, so then he is prevented from being a commander and being a conveyer. I say: This is refuted by His saying, may He be exalted, 'And those in authority among you' (4:59) - this is a scribal error, the correct is 'and those in authority among you', for he is higher than us, and if the lower is a commander, then the higher is a fortiori. And it is also refuted by His saying, 'Convey what has been revealed to you' (5:67) - the verse, for the general address is to the Prophet from Him, i.e., from what was revealed, so he is definitely a conveyer, no room for denial, and specifying general addresses is very far-fetched. And the third answer: By his standing to himself, he is not a commander nor a conveyer, although by relation to others he is a commander to him and a conveyer, so the combination of commanding and being commanded does not occur in him, nor him being a conveyer and conveyed to. And if the second answer is referred back to him, it is not far-fetched, for the one who answered with it is the commentator of al-Mukhtasar, and he did not answer with this answer; what was brought against him is brought. I say, it is refuted by His saying, 'Convey what has been revealed' (5:67), for the word 'ma' is general, and the general addresses are from Him, so he is a conveyer of these addresses as well. And in it, that 'convey what has been revealed' only requires that he be a conveyer of all, not that he be a conveyer with respect to every obligated person, since the apparent meaning of it is 'convey what has been revealed to you of general and specific addresses to others of the obligated ones', and general addresses may include the Messenger and he be a conveyer of them with respect to others. This. And they said, secondly: The Messenger of Allah is specified with rulings such as the obligation of two rak'ats of Fajr (non-obligatory according to later scholars), and the obligation of Duha prayer (this is incorrect, for he is reported to have left it), and the obligation of Adha prayer (this is incorrect in our view, for Adha prayer is obligatory upon all in our school), and the prohibition of accepting charity (if you say: the prohibition of accepting charity is not specific to him but includes all Banu Hashim, I say: what is meant is voluntary charity, which is not prohibited for Banu Hashim except for the Messenger of Allah, as in al-Kashf; moreover, the prohibition of obligatory charity upon other Banu Hashim is by following, not primarily), and the prohibition of 'kha'inat al-a'yun' (treachery of the eyes) which is interpreted as a sign to permissible pain such as killing and beating contrary to what appears. It is narrated in histories with an uninterrupted chain that Uthman brought Abdullah ibn Sarh to the Messenger of Allah, and Abdullah uttered the word of testimony by the tongue, and the Prophet had previously declared his blood permissible, then after he left he said, 'Why did you not kill him before he said the word of testimony?' They said, 'If you had gestured with your eye?' He said, 'It is not permissible' or 'It is not befitting for a prophet to have treachery of the eyes.' And the permissibility of marriage without witnesses, dowry, and guardian: This is not correct in our view, for marriage without a guardian is valid in our view; if he intended his marriage without a guardian for him, this is general in the marriage of every man; and if he intended his marriage from a woman without a guardian for her, then in it, he is the guardian of every Muslim man and woman. And the permissibility of more than four, even more than nine, as A'isha said that the Prophet did not leave this world except that Allah had permitted to him all women, and other things specific to him. So this specification indicates non-participation in the general. The answer: Its indication of non-participation in the general is prevented, and that exclusion from some by evidence does not necessitate exclusion from every general absolutely, like the sick, the traveler, and the menstruating woman; they are excluded from some addresses and it does not necessitate exclusion from it absolutely.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed dispute on whether the Prophet (ﷺ) is included in general commands addressed to the Muslim community. The author supports the view that he is included. The opponents argue that being a commander and a conveyer precludes being commanded or conveyed to. The author responds with multiple arguments: (1) It is possible to combine these roles from different aspects, like a doctor treating himself. (2) The true commander is Allah, and the Prophet is merely a conveyer. (3) The Prophet's standing to himself is not as a commander but as a follower. The text then addresses the claim that the Prophet's special rulings (e.g., obligation of Fajr sunnah, Duha, Adha prayers, prohibition of charity, etc.) prove his exclusion from general rules. The author refutes these by showing they are either incorrect or apply generally. The conclusion: exception of some individuals from general commands does not necessitate absolute exclusion, analogous to sick, traveler, and menstruating women being excepted from certain obligations but not from all.

Key Terms
الخطاب العام (al-khitāb al-‘āmm): A general address that includes all addressees without specification.
التناول (al-tanāwul): Inclusion of a person in the scope of an address or ruling.
الآمرية والمأمورية (al-āmiriyya wa al-ma’mūriyya): The quality of being a commander and being commanded, respectively.
الاستعلاء (al-isti‘lā’): Superiority or high status; a condition for commanding according to some, but the author says it is not necessary.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in the text, what is the primary reason the Prophet is included in general commands despite his role as a commander?
A) Because the Companions asked him about the commands
B) Because commanding and being commanded can combine from different aspects, like a doctor treating himself
C) Because the Prophet never actually followed any specific command
D) Because the Quran explicitly states the Prophet is included in every verse

Key Takeaway
The Prophet is included in general commands unless specific evidence excludes him; partial exception is not total exclusion.
