Inclusion of Non-Existent Persons in Immediate Oral Addresses
Dispute on whether non-existent persons are included in immediate oral addresses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that immediate oral addresses exclude non-existent persons and distinguish it from the Hanbali view.

Core Idea
An immediate oral address cannot include a non-existent person because a demand requires an existing subject.

This lesson examines a key usul dispute: Do general commands like 'O you who believe' include people not yet born? The Hanafi school argues they do not, while the Hanbalis and some Hanafis disagree. Understanding this distinction clarifies how Quranic rulings apply across generations.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the Hanafi ruling: immediate oral addresses do not include non-existent persons. Note the two types of address: immediate (tanjīzī) and conditional (ta‘līqī).
The source states 'al-khitāb al-tanjīzī' does not include non-existent persons.
2
Identify the opponent and their proof
The Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr (from the Hanafi school) hold that such addresses are general and include all believers, present or future. Their likely proof is the apparent generality of the wording.
Opposing schools: Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response to the absent-person objection
The Hanafi text anticipates the objection: absent people also cannot be called, yet they are included in some addresses (e.g., conditional ones). The response distinguishes absent (existing but not present) from non-existent (not existing at all). Absent persons are capable of receiving the demand because they exist, unlike the non-existent who are 'nothing'.
The key distinction: existence vs. non-existence.
4
Interpret the formula 'O you who believe'
The text offers two explanations: either it does not include the absent, or it uses predominance (taghlīb) — addressing the present but extending to others figuratively. This resolves the apparent conflict with the general wording.
Taghlīb allows a term for one category to include another by predominance.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: whether Quranic rulings like fasting ('O you who believe, fasting is prescribed...') apply to later generations via this immediate address. Hanafis say they apply via other mechanisms (e.g., conditional or non-oral addresses), not via this oral address.
The dispute affects how we understand the binding force of commands for subsequent generations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: يعم الخطاب التنجيزي لا التعليقي فإنه قد تقدم أنه يعم المعدومين (الشفاهي) وأما غير الشفاهي فيتناول المعدومين (نحو: ﴿يَاهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا [البقرة: ۱۷۸] لا المعدومين في زمن الوحي) أي نزول الخطاب (خلافاً للحنابلة وأبي اليسر منا لنا :أولاً: أن المعدوم لا ينادى ولا يطلب منه الفعل والخطاب التنجيزي الشفاهي يقتضي تعلق الطلب ،به، فإن قلت: فعلى هذا يلزم أن يكون الموجودون الغائبون لم يتناولهم الخطاب، فإن الغائب لا ينادى، قلت : بعض الخطابات نحو فَمَن شَهِدَ مِنكُمُ الشَّهْرَ فَلْيَصُمْهُ ﴾ [البقرة : ۱۸٥] أعني بصيغة يمكن تعلقها بالغائبين فإنهم صالحون لتعلق الطلب بخلاف المعدوم، فإنه ليس بشيء حتى يتوجه إليه الطلب، وأما نحو يتأَيُّهَا الَّذِينَ ءَامَنُوا) [البقرة : ۱۷۸] فقد التزم عدم تناوله للغيب، أو يقال : إنه جعل بالتغليب
Issue: The immediate address is general, not the conditional address, for it has already been stated that it includes non-existent persons. The oral address does not include non-existent persons at the time of revelation, contrary to the Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr from us. Our evidence: First, the non-existent cannot be called nor can an action be demanded from him, and the immediate oral address necessitates the attachment of the demand to him. If you say: On this basis, it would necessitate that the existing but absent persons are not included in the address, for the absent is not called. I say: Some addresses, like 'Whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185), meaning by a formula that can be attached to the absent, for they are suitable for the attachment of the demand, unlike the non-existent, for he is nothing such that the demand can be directed to him. As for 'O you who believe' (2:178), it is acknowledged that it does not include the absent, or it is said that it is made by way of predominance.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage addresses whether general commands given orally and immediately (like 'O you who believe') include non-existent persons (those not yet born) at the time of revelation. The Hanafi position is that such addresses do not include the non-existent, contrary to the Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr. The proof is that a non-existent person cannot be called or required to act, because he is not a subject capable of receiving a demand. A possible objection is that absent persons also cannot be called, yet they are included in some addresses. The answer distinguishes between absent persons, who are suitable for the demand (since they can potentially be reached), and non-existent persons, who are nothing. As for the formula 'O you who believe,' it is said to be by way of predominance (taghlib) meaning it addresses the present but can be extended to others later.

Key Terms
الخطاب التنجيزي (al-khitāb al-tanjīzī): Immediate address that is directed to the audience at the time of revelation.
الخطاب التعليقي (al-khitāb al-ta‘līqī): Conditional address that applies to future individuals based on a condition.
الشفاهي (al-shifāhī): Oral address, as opposed to written or other forms.
المعدوم (al-ma‘dūm): Non-existent; a person who does not exist at the time of the address.
التغليب (al-taghlīb): Predominance; using a term that applies to one category to include another by way of predominance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does the immediate oral address 'O you who believe' not include non-existent persons?
A) Because the Quran was only revealed for the Arabs.
B) Because the non-existent cannot be called or required to act.
C) Because the Prophet limited the address to the Companions.
D) Because the address is conditional.

Key Takeaway
Immediate oral addresses exclude non-existent persons because a demand requires an existing subject; conditional or non-oral addresses can include them.

The Prophet's Special Rulings and General Commands: A Usul Dispute
Response to argument that the Prophet's exemptions invalidate general commands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that specific exceptions do not negate general commands and evaluate the opponent's argument using analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Exclusion from some general commands by specific evidence does not nullify generality for others.

This passage addresses a critical usul question: Do the Prophet's special rulings undermine the principle that general commands are truly inclusive? The opponent argues yes; the Hanafi school responds with a robust defense, using analogy to show that exceptions confirm the rule, just as the sick and traveler are exempted from fasting without abolishing the general obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Opponent's Challenge
Recognize the claim: the Prophet's special rulings (khususiyyat) prove he is not included in general commands, thus questioning the universality of umum.
The opponent uses multiple examples from the Prophet's life.
2
List the Opponent's Examples
Enumerate the examples: Duha prayer, prohibition of charity, marriage without witnesses, 'betrayal of the eyes,' etc. Each is presented as evidence that the Prophet is exempted from general obligations.
These examples are the core of the opponent's inductive argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Refutation
Go through each example: some are refuted as not actually specific (e.g., Eid prayer is obligatory on all in Hanafi school); others are genuine exceptions but only apply to that specific command, not all general commands.
The Hanafi response dismantles the examples one by one.
4
Extract the Core Analogy
The Hanafi school uses the analogy of the sick, traveler, and menstruating woman: they are excluded from some obligations but the general obligation remains for others. This shows that exceptions do not negate generality.
This qiyas is the key to understanding the principle.
5
Formulate the Principle
Conclude that specific evidence of exclusion (takhsis) from some commands does not invalidate the general applicability of other commands. The general command remains operative for all not excluded.
This preserves the universal reach of divine commands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الخطابات أيضاً وفيه أن بلغ ما أنزل إنما يستدعي أن يكون مبلغاً للكل لا أن يكون مبلغاً بالنسبة إلى كل مكلف إذ المتبادر منه بلغ ما أنزل إليك من الخطابات العامة والخاصة إلى غيرك من المكلفين والخطابات العامة يجوز أن يكون الرسول داخلاً فيها ويكون مبلغاً لها بالنسبة إلى أغياره هذا (و) قالوا (ثانياً) رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم مخصوص بأحكام كوجوب ركعتي الفجر غير الفرض على ما حققه المتأخرون (و) وجوب صلاة الضحى وهذا غير صحيح، فإنه قد ثبت تركها من رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم (و) وجوب صلاة الأضحى وهذا غير صحيح على رأينا، فإن صلاة الأضحى واجبة عندنا على الكل وحرمة أخذ الصدقة فإن قلت حرمة أخذ الصدقة غير مخصوصة به عليه السلام بل متناولة لكل بني هاشم قلت المراد بها صدقة التطوع، وليست هي محرمة على بني هاشم إلا على رسول الله ، كذا في «الكشف»، على أن حرمة التصدق المفروض على سائر بني هاشم بالتبع لا بالأصالة (و) حرمة خائنة الأعين وفسرت بالإشارة إلى الإيلام المباح من القتل والضرب على خلاف ما يظهر روي في التواريخ بسند متصل أنه جاء أمير المؤمنين عثمان بعبد الله بن سرح إلى رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم ، فأجرى عبد الله كلمة الشهادة على اللسان وقد كان أهدر دمه قبل فقال بعد ذهابه : «هلا قتلتموه قبل أن يقول كلمة الشهادة فقالوا : لو أشرت بعينك فقال : لا يحل» أو «لا ينبغي للنبي خائنة الأعين» وإباحة النكاح من غير شهود ومهر وولي هذا لا يصح عندنا، فإن النكاح بلا ولي صحيح عندنا، على أنه إن أراد نکاحه من غير ولي له فهذا عام في نكاح كل رجل، وإن أراد أن نكاحه من المرأة من غير ولي لها ففيه أنه عليه السلام ولي كل مسلم ومسلمة (و) إباحة الزيادة على أربع بل على تسع كما قالت أم المؤمنين عائشة رضي الله عنها إنه ما خرج رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم من الدنيا إلا وقد أباح له الله تعالى النساء كلها إلى غير ذلك مما اختص به عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (فدل) هذا الاختصاص على عدم المشاركة في العموم (والجواب أن دلالته على عدم المشاركة في العموم ممنوعة و أن الخروج من البعض بدليل لا يوجب الخروج من كل عام مطلقاً، كالمريض والمسافر والحائض خرجوا عن بعض الخطابات ولا يلزم الخروج منه مطلقاً.
The third chapter on linguistic principles: also regarding addresses. It includes that 'convey what is revealed' requires that he be a conveyor to all, not that he be a conveyor to each individual, because the apparent meaning is 'convey what has been revealed to you of general and specific addresses to other obligated persons.' It is permissible for the general addresses to include the Messenger himself and he conveys them to others. This (and) they said, secondly: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) is specified with rulings such as the obligation of two rak'ats of Fajr (supererogatory) according to the later scholars, and the obligation of Salat al-Duha (mid-morning prayer) - but this is incorrect because it is established that he omitted it. And the obligation of Salat al-Adha (Eid prayer) - this is incorrect in our view, for Eid prayer is obligatory upon all in our school. And the prohibition of taking charity (sadaqah). If you say: the prohibition of taking charity is not specific to him, but includes all Banu Hashim, I say: what is meant is voluntary charity, and it is not prohibited for Banu Hashim except for the Messenger of Allah. This is in Al-Kashf. Moreover, the prohibition of obligatory charity upon the rest of Banu Hashim is by way of subordination, not fundamentally. And the prohibition of 'betrayal of the eyes' which is interpreted as signaling to permitted harm such as killing or beating, contrary to what appears. It is narrated in histories with a connected chain that the Commander of the Faithful Uthman brought 'Abdullah ibn Sa'd to the Messenger of Allah, and 'Abdullah uttered the word of testimony, though his blood had been declared lawful, so he (the Prophet) said after his departure: 'Why did you not kill him before he said the word of testimony?' They said: 'If you had signaled with your eye?' He said: 'It is not lawful' or 'It is not befitting for a prophet to have betrayal of the eyes.' And the permissibility of marriage without witnesses, dowry, or guardian - this is not correct in our view, for marriage without a guardian is valid in our school. If he means his marriage without a guardian for himself, then this is general for every man's marriage; if he means his marriage to a woman without a guardian for her, then he is the guardian of every Muslim man and woman. And the permissibility of marrying more than four, even up to nine, as the Mother of the Believers Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) said: 'The Messenger of Allah did not leave this world except that Allah had permitted him all women,' and other things specific to him, peace and blessings be upon him and his family and Companions. (So this specificity) indicates non-participation in generality. (The answer is that its indication of non-participation in generality is denied, and that exclusion from some by evidence does not necessitate exclusion from every general (command) absolutely, like the sick, the traveler, and the menstruating woman - they are excluded from some addresses but it does not necessitate exclusion from it absolutely.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a dispute concerning the effect of the Prophet's ﷺ special rulings on the principle of general applicability (umum) of commands. The opponents argue that because the Prophet ﷺ is exempted from certain general obligations (e.g., he was commanded to pray Duha, forbidden from taking charity, allowed marriage without witnesses, etc.), this shows that general commands do not necessarily include everyone. The Hanafi response, introduced by 'Wal-Jawab', denies that this inference is valid. The correct principle is that exclusion of some individuals from a general command by a specific evidence (dalil) does not negate the generality for others. Analogous examples are the sick, traveler, and menstruating woman: they are exempted from certain obligations (e.g., fasting) but the general obligation of fasting remains for others. Therefore, the Prophet's special rulings do not prove that general commands are not meant to be universal. The passage also clarifies each alleged special ruling, showing that some are not actually specific (e.g., Eid prayer is obligatory on all, marriage without guardian is not exclusive to him).

Key Terms
العموم (al-umum): The general applicability of a command to all individuals who meet the conditions, unless a specific evidence excludes some.
الخصوص (al-khusus): The specification of certain individuals or cases by evidence that excludes them from a general command.
التخصيص (al-takhsis): The act of specifying a general term to exclude some of its individuals by a separate evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi response, why do the special rulings of the Prophet ﷺ not negate the general applicability of commands?
A) Because the Prophet ﷺ is not included in any general commands to begin with.
B) Because the examples given are not actually specific to him upon investigation.
C) Because the exclusion of some individuals by specific evidence does not negate the generality for others, like the sick and traveler.
D) Because the general commands only apply to non-Prophets.

Key Takeaway
Specific exceptions (takhsis) to a general command do not negate its generality; the command remains binding on all not excluded by explicit evidence.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific

Immediate Oral Addresses Do Not Include Non-Existent Persons
Rule distinguishing immediate and conditional addresses regarding non-existent persons

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between immediate (tanjizi) and conditional (ta'liqi) addresses, and apply the Hanafi rule that immediate addresses do not intend non-existent persons.

Core Idea
Immediate oral addresses do not intend non-existent persons; only conditional or non-oral addresses do.

In Hanafi usul, understanding the scope of divine addresses is crucial. This passage tackles a key question: when Allah says 'O you who believe,' does this include believers not yet born? The Hanafi answer is no, and this lesson unpacks the reasoning, including two proofs and a refutation of philosophical objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the Hanafi position: immediate (tanjizi) oral addresses (like 'O you who believe') do not include non-existent persons; only conditional (ta'liqi) addresses or non-oral addresses include them.
2
Examine the First Proof
Understand why the non-existent cannot be called or commanded immediately. The addressee must exist and be capable of compliance at the time of address. Unlike absent persons (who can be addressed via conditional formulas), the non-existent is nothing and cannot be demanded an act.
3
Analyze the Qiyas to Child and Insane
Second proof: just as children and the insane are not intended by general addresses due to lack of understanding, the non-existent is even more deserving of exclusion. The analogy is based on the common attribute of inability to understand the immediate demand.
4
Evaluate the Opposing View and Philosophical Objection
Contrast with Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr who hold that general addresses include all believers. Also examine the philosophical claim that all times are present to Allah; the Hanafi response dismisses this as sophistry, clarifying that Allah’s knowledge does not equate to temporal presence for immediate address.
5
Understand the Role of Taghlib
The rhetorical device of taghlib (preponderance) treats a composite of existent and non-existent as existent in expression, but it does not create real obligation for the non-existent. The passage concludes that taghlib is not needed because addresses use words like 'people' that apply to both existent and non-existent linguistically.

Source Text

الله ، رحمه الله تعالى وهو الأمر والناهي والكل ممن في الأزمنة المختلفة أن الخطاب من هو من الماضي والمستقبل والحاضر حاضر عنده تعالى ونسبته تعالى إلى الموجودين والمعدومين نسبة واحدة، يصح :لكونه تعالى منزهاً عن الزمانية، فحينئذ يصح الطلب والنداء، والجواب عنه أن معنى حضورهم عنده تعالى أنه يعلمهم لا يغرب شيء منهم عنه تعالى بالوقوع فى أزمنتهم، فلو تعلق بهم الطلب لتعلق بإيقاعهم في أزمنة وقوعهم بصفة التكليف وهذا هو التكليف التعليقي ولا كلام فيه، وإن أريد بالحضور عنده ما أرادت الفلاسفة من أن الزمان مع ما فيه موجود في الواقع حاضر عنده معه سبحانه وسموا هذا الوجود وجوداً ،دهرياً، ومعيته تعالى سبحانه لهم في هذا الوجود الواقعي معية دهرية وأن أعدامهم ليست أعداماً حقيقية في الواقع بل غيبوبة زمانية، فمشايخنا الكرام يرونه سفسطة غير صالحة لابتناء الحقائق العلمية فضلاً عن الأمور الشرعية فتدبر (قيل) في شرح الشرح (ذلك) أي عدم صحة نداء المعدومين (حق في المعدومين فقط، وأما المركب من الموجودين والمعدومين فجائز النداء (فيه تغليباً) للموجودين على المعدومين والتغليب استعمال فصيح شائع (أقول : المركب من الموجود والمعدوم معدوم والمعدوم لا نداؤه وطلبه (فلا يجوز النداء والطلب تنجيزاً حقيقة) وإن صح تعليقاً وصورة (وإنما الكلام فيه أي في الطلب الحقيقي تنجيزاً وفيه نوع مسامحة فإن في التغليب لا ينادى المركب ولا يطلب منه الفعل، بل ينادى كل واحد ويطلب من كل لكن بتنزيل المعدوم موجوداً، فالأولى أن يقال : التغليب لا يجعل المعدوم موجوداً فهو لا شيء محض لا يصح نداؤه ولا الطلب منه تنجيزاً على أن التغليب في التعبير بلفظ الموجود) وليس الكلام فيه، ولا حاجة إليه أيضاً فإن الخطابات الشفاهية ليست بلفظ الموجود بل بلفظ الناس وأمثاله، وهو كما يطلق على الموجود يطلق على المعدوم فلا حاجة في التعبير إلى التغليب لا (ثانياً) أنه لم يعم الصبي والمجنون) وذلك لعدم الفهم والتمييز (فالمعدوم أجدر) بعد تناول الخطابات إياه، وحاصله قياس المعدوم على الصبي والمجنون بجامع عدم الفهم قيل : عدم توجه التكليف إلى البعض وهو الصبي والمجنون بناء على دليل وهو رفع القلم عنهما لا ينافي عموم الخطاب وتناوله (لفظاً لبعض آخر، والحاصل عدم الاشتراك في الجامع (أقول خطاب المجنون ونحوه مستحيل الإرادة من الطالب لانتفاء شرطه الذي هو الفهم والتمييز، ولعله أراد بالصبي والمجنون اللذين لا يعقلان فلا يرد أن الصبي غير مستحيل الإرادة لأنه ربما الخطاب ويفهمه، كيف وقد تقدم ما روى البيهقي من إناطة الأحكام يسمع بالعقل قبل الخندق وبعده نسخ عنه فإذن دخوله قطعاً (فلا صح يعمهم إرادة وإذا لم يعمهم لانتفاء الفهم والتمييز وهو موجود في المعدوم فلا يعمهم أيضاً وإن أريد مطلق التناول لفظاً والشمول وضعاً، يقال ومطلق التناول لفظاً غير محل النزاع بل النزاع في عمومهم إرادة
Issue: The immediate (tanjizi) address is general, not the conditional (ta'liqi). For it has preceded that it includes the non-existent (ma'dumin). (Oral address) As for non-oral address, it includes the non-existent (like: 'O you who have believed' [al-Baqarah 2:178] not including the non-existent at the time of revelation). That is, the descent of the address, contrary to the Hanbalis and Abu al-Yusr from among us. Our evidence: First, the non-existent cannot be called nor can an act be demanded from him, and the immediate oral address necessitates the attachment of the demand to him. If you say: on this basis, it would follow that present but absent persons (gha'ibin) are not included by the address, for the absent cannot be called. I say: Some addresses like 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast' [al-Baqarah 2:185] I mean by a formula that can attach to the absent, for they are fit for the attachment of the demand, unlike the non-existent, for he is nothing such that the demand can turn to him. As for like 'O you who have believed' [al-Baqarah 2:178], it is conceded that it does not include the absent, or it is said: it is made, by way of preponderance (taghlib), a caller addressed with the formula of the present, unlike the non-existent. As the author said. Then there is another discussion here, may Allah have mercy on him: that the Commander and Prohibitor, and all who are in different times, the address from whoever is of the past, future, and present is present with Him, and His relation to the existent and non-existent is one relation. It would be valid because He is transcendent above temporality. Then at that time, the demand and call would be valid. The answer to that is that the meaning of their presence with Him is that He knows them; nothing of them is absent from Him in the occurrence in their times. So if the demand attached to them, it would attach to their occurrence in the times of their occurrence with the attribute of obligation, and this is the conditional obligation (taklif ta'liqi), and there is no dispute about it. If one intends by presence with Him what the philosophers intended, that time and what is in it exist in reality, present with Him, and they call this existence 'temporal existence' (wujud dahrī), and His accompaniment with them in this real existence is a temporal accompaniment, and that their non-existence is not real non-existence but temporal concealment, then our honorable scholars consider it sophistry, not suitable for the foundation of scientific realities, let alone religious matters. So ponder. It was said in Sharh al-Sharh: that (i.e., the invalidity of calling the non-existent) is true only for non-existent persons. As for a composite of existent and non-existent, calling is permissible through preponderance (taghlib) of the existent over the non-existent, and taghlib is a common eloquent usage. I say: the composite of existent and non-existent is non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be called or demanded from him. So calling and demanding are not valid immediately in reality, even if valid conditionally and formally. The discussion is only about real demand immediately. And there is some allowance for it, because in taghlib, the composite is not called nor is action demanded from it. Rather, each individual is called and action is demanded from each, but by treating the non-existent as existent. So it is better to say: taghlib does not make the non-existent existent; it is pure nothing, not valid to call or demand from immediately. Moreover, taghlib in expression with the word 'existent' is not the issue, nor is there a need for it because oral addresses are not with the word 'existent' but with words like 'people' and the like, and just as it applies to the existent, it applies to the non-existent. So there is no need for taghlib in expression, nor in obligation (taklif). That is, taghlib is not in obligation, nor does it validate it. For if every one of the non-existent were then obligated truly and immediately, taghlib would not help. So what needs taghlib is not needed, and what needs it is not helped by it. So let one reflect, for it is more worthy of acceptance. And second evidence for us: that it does not include the child and the insane, due to lack of understanding and discernment, so the non-existent is more deserving of not being included. The gist is a comparison (qiyas) of the non-existent to the child and insane on the basis of lack of understanding. It was said: the non-address of obligation to some, i.e., the child and insane, based on evidence (the lifting of the pen from them) does not negate the generality of the address and its inclusion verbally for others. The conclusion is that there is no common factor. I say: addressing the insane and similar is impossible in intention from the demander due to the absence of its condition which is understanding and discernment. Perhaps he meant by the child and insane those who do not reason, so it is not objected that the child is not impossible in intention because he may be addressed and understand. How so? It has preceded what al-Bayhaqi narrated about the attachment of rulings to hearing with reason before al-Khandaq and its abrogation after that. So his inclusion is certain. So it is not correct that they are included in intention. And if they are not included due to the absence of understanding and discernment, and this exists in the non-existent, then it is not included either. If one intends absolute verbal inclusion and conceptual coverage, it is said: absolute verbal inclusion is not the subject of dispute; rather, the dispute is about their inclusion in intention.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a rule stating that immediate (tanjizi) oral addresses (like 'O you who believe') do not include non-existent persons (ma'dumin) at the time of revelation, unlike conditional addresses (ta'liqi) which do include them. This is a Hanafi position in opposition to the Hanabilah and Abu al-Yusr (a Hanafi figure). The author provides two main proofs. First, a non-existent person cannot be called or demanded an act, and immediate oral address requires the addressee to be present for the demand to attach. The objection that absent persons (gha'ibin) are also not present is countered by noting that some formulas (e.g., conditional clauses) can address the absent, whereas non-existent persons are nothing. Second, a qiyas (analogy) is made: just as children and the insane are not included in general addresses due to lack of understanding, the non-existent are even more deserving of exclusion. The text also refutes a philosophical argument that all times are present to Allah, making address to non-existent possible. The scholars dismiss this as sophistry because it confuses knowledge with temporal presence. The concept of taghlib (preponderance) is discussed but ultimately rejected for real obligation; it only works formally. The key point is that the dispute is about real intention (irada) of the address, not mere verbal coverage (shumul lafzi).

Key Terms
الخطاب التنجيزي (al-khitab al-tanjizi): An immediate address or command that expects immediate compliance from those actually present and capable.
الخطاب التعليقي (al-khitab al-ta'liqi): A conditional address that applies to future persons when they come into existence and meet the conditions.
المعدوم (al-ma'dum): A non-existent person at the time of the address, one who does not yet exist.
التغليب (al-taghlib): The rhetorical device of giving preponderance to the majority or present group in a composite expression, treating the non-existent as if existent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the main reason immediate oral addresses do not include non-existent persons?
A) Because non-existent persons are not believers.
B) Because they cannot be called or demanded an act at the time of the address.
C) Because the address was only for the Companions.
D) Because the text is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
Immediate oral addresses only intend those who exist and can understand at the time of address; non-existent persons are included only through conditional or non-oral addresses.

Addressing the Non-Existent: Hanafi vs. Hanbali on Direct Obligation
Analyzing whether the Shari'ah address includes those not present at revelation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the Hanafi position that the non-existent are not directly addressed and identify the role of taghlib and qiyas in the dispute with the Hanbalis.

Core Idea
The non-existent cannot be directly addressed; later generations are obligated through the generality of Shari'ah, not the original spoken address.

Does the Qur'anic command 'O you who believe' include Muslims born centuries later? The Hanafi school argues that the original spoken address (khitab shifahi) only obligates those present at revelation. Later generations are bound through the generality of Shari'ah (umum al-shari'ah) and established evidence (nash al-dalil). This lesson unpacks the textual, rhetorical, and qiyas-based arguments that support this view, and contrasts them with the Hanbali counter-arguments.

Guided Path
1
Frame the core issue
Identify the question: does the Shari'ah address (khitab) in the Qur'an and Sunnah directly include those who were non-existent at the time of revelation? The Hanafis say no; the Hanbalis say yes.
The term al-ma'dum refers to persons not yet born.
2
Analyze the taghlib (subsumption) argument
Some propose that a compound of existent and non-existent can be addressed by subsuming the non-existent under the existent. Examine why the Hanafis reject this: the compound itself is non-existent, and taghlib is only a rhetorical figure, not a legal reality.
Taghlib does not make the non-existent existent; it only treats it as such in wording.
3
Examine the qiyas on the child and insane
The Hanafis analogize the non-existent to the child and insane (who lack understanding and discernment). Since the address does not directly obligate them, the non-existent is more deserving of exclusion. Trace the analogy: asl (child/insane), hukm (not addressed), illa (lack of understanding), far' (non-existent), hukm (not addressed).
The Hanbalis object that the lifting of the pen is a specific evidence, not a general principle.
4
Evaluate the Hanbali objections
Hanbalis argue: (1) Scholars throughout history have used the original address as evidence for later generations, implying inclusion. (2) If the Prophet did not address them, he would not be sent to them. (3) The Quranic wording must correspond to inner speech, which includes the non-existent. The Hanafis respond to each point.
The Hanafi responses distinguish between evidence (istidlal) and direct address, and between actual and suspensive obligation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The difference is theoretical but affects how we understand the mechanism of obligation. For Hanafis, a Muslim today is obligated because the Shari'ah is general and evidence establishes the ruling applies to all, not because the original speech literally speaks to him.
This influences secondary issues like the authority of general commands for later generations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يبين منادى معبراً بصيغة الحاضر بخلاف المعدوم كما المصنف ثم ههنا بحث آخر الله ، رحمه الله تعالى وهو الأمر والناهي والكل ممن في الأزمنة المختلفة أن الخطاب من هو من الماضي والمستقبل والحاضر حاضر عنده تعالى ونسبته تعالى إلى الموجودين والمعدومين نسبة واحدة، يصح : لكونه تعالى منزهاً عن الزمانية، فحينئذ يصح الطلب والنداء، والجواب عنه أن معنى حضورهم عنده تعالى أنه يعلمهم لا يغرب شيء منهم عنه تعالى بالوقوع فى أزمنتهم، فلو تعلق بهم الطلب لتعلق بإيقاعهم في أزمنة وقوعهم بصفة التكليف وهذا هو التكليف التعليقي ولا كلام فيه، وإن أريد بالحضور عنده ما أرادت الفلاسفة من أن الزمان مع ما فيه موجود في الواقع حاضر عنده معه سبحانه وسموا هذا الوجود وجوداً ،دهرياً، ومعيته تعالى سبحانه لهم في هذا الوجود الواقعي معية دهرية وأن أعدامهم ليست أعداماً حقيقية في الواقع بل غيبوبة زمانية، فمشايخنا الكرام يرونه سفسطة غير صالحة لابتناء الحقائق العلمية فضلاً عن الأمور الشرعية فتدبر (قيل) في شرح الشرح (ذلك) أي عدم صحة نداء المعدومين (حق في المعدومين فقط، وأما المركب من الموجودين والمعدومين فجائز النداء (فيه تغليباً) للموجودين على المعدومين والتغليب استعمال فصيح شائع (أقول : المركب من الموجود والمعدوم معدوم والمعدوم لا نداؤه وطلبه (فلا يجوز النداء والطلب تنجيزاً حقيقة) وإن صح تعليقاً وصورة (وإنما الكلام فيه أي في الطلب الحقيقي تنجيزاً وفيه نوع مسامحة فإن في التغليب لا ينادى المركب ولا يطلب منه الفعل، بل ينادى كل واحد ويطلب من كل لكن بتنزيل المعدوم موجوداً، فالأولى أن يقال : التغليب لا يجعل المعدوم موجوداً فهو لا شيء محض لا يصح نداؤه ولا الطلب منه تنجيزاً على أن التغليب في التعبير بلفظ الموجود) وليس الكلام فيه، ولا حاجة إليه أيضاً فإن الخطابات الشفاهية ليست بلفظ الموجود بل بلفظ الناس وأمثاله، وهو كما يطلق على الموجود يطلق على المعدوم فلا حاجة في التعبير إلى التغليب لا في التكليف) أي ليس التغليب في التكليف ولا يصححه أيضاً فإن كل واحد من المعدومين حينئذ مكلف حقيقة وتنجيزاً فما ينفع فيه التغليب لا حاجة إليه وما فيه حاجة لا ينفع فيه (فليتأمل) فإنه أحق بالقبول (و) لنا (ثانياً) أنه لم يعم الصبي والمجنون) وذلك لعدم الفهم والتمييز (فالمعدوم أجدر) بعد تناول الخطابات إياه، وحاصله قياس المعدوم على الصبي والمجنون بجامع عدم الفهم قيل : عدم توجه التكليف إلى البعض وهو الصبي والمجنون بناء على دليل وهو رفع القلم عنهما لا ينافي عموم الخطاب وتناوله (لفظاً لبعض آخر، والحاصل عدم الاشتراك في الجامع (أقول خطاب المجنون ونحوه مستحيل الإرادة من الطالب لانتفاء شرطه الذي هو الفهم والتمييز، ولعله أراد بالصبي والمجنون اللذين لا يعقلان فلا يرد أن الصبي غير مستحيل الإرادة لأنه ربما الخطاب ويفهمه، كيف وقد تقدم ما روى البيهقي من إناطة الأحكام يسمع بالعقل قبل الخندق وبعده نسخ عنه فإذن دخوله قطعاً (فلا صح يعمهم إرادة وإذا لم يعمهم لانتفاء الفهم والتمييز وهو موجود في المعدوم فلا يعمهم أيضاً وإن أريد مطلق التناول لفظاً والشمول وضعاً، يقال ومطلق التناول) لفظاً (غير محل النزاع بل النزاع في عمومهم إرادة
المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية الحنابلة (قالوا: (أولاً) لو لم يكن المعدوم مشمول الخطاب لما صح الاحتجاج به على شمول الأحكام إياه ولم يزل العلماء يحتجون به على من هو في أعصارهم) وكان معدوماً زمن الخطاب (وذلك) أي الاحتجاج المذكور منهم (إجماع على العموم قلنا يجوز أن لا يكون الاحتجاج لأجل دخولهم في الخطاب إرادة بل ذلك لعلمهم بعموم الشريعة) لكل مكلف موجود من زمن الوحي إلى يوم القيامة (وهو لا يتوقف على عموم الخطاب الشفاهي، و) قالوا (ثانياً : لو لم يكن الرسول صلى الله عليه وسلم (مخاطباً لهم لم يكن مرسلاً إليهم إذ لا تبليغ إليهم إلا بهذه العمومات ولا إرسال إلا بتبليغ أحكام الله تعالى (قلنا) عدم التبليغ إلا بهذه العمومات (ممنوع بل الخطاب (للبعض (شفاهاً وهم الموجودون زمن الخطاب (وللباقي بنصب الدليل على أن حكمهم كحكمهم وبه يتحقق الإرسال قيل : النظم القرآني يحاذي الكلام النفسي وهذا أي الكلام النفسي (يعم المعدوم) كما تقدم فلزم تناول اللفظي أيضاً للمعدوم، وإلا بطل التحاذي قلنا : المحاذاة ليس) واجباً من كل وجه ضرورة الفرق بين التعلق منجزاً كما في الخطاب الشفاهي (والتعليق) كما في الكلام النفسي، وإذا كان فرق بالتعلق والتعليق فيجوز الافتراق بدخول المعدوم وعدمه هذا.
He explains the caller expressed with the form of the present tense unlike the non-existent, as the author. Then here is another research — may Allah have mercy on him — and it is the commander and prohibitor and all those in different times that the address is from the past, future, and present, and all are present with Him, may He be exalted, and His relation, may He be exalted, to the existents and non-existents is one relation. It is valid because He is transcendent above temporality. Then at that time the request and calling are valid. The reply about it is that the meaning of their presence with Him is that He knows them; nothing is hidden from Him in their occurrence in their times. So if the request were attached to them, it would attach to their occurrence in the times of their occurrence with the attribute of obligation, and this is suspensive obligation, and there is no discussion about it. If by presence with Him is meant what the philosophers meant — that time with what is in it is actually present with Him, and they call this existence temporal existence, and His coexistence with them in this actual existence is temporal coexistence, and their non-existences are not real non-existences in reality but temporal absence — then our noble teachers consider it sophistry, unfit for building scientific truths, let alone religious matters. So ponder. It was said in the commentary of the commentary: That (i.e., the invalidity of calling the non-existent) is true only for the non-existent. As for the compound of existents and non-existents, calling is permissible in it by way of subsuming the non-existent under the existents. Subsumption is a common eloquent usage. I say: The compound of existent and non-existent is non-existent, and the non-existent cannot be called or requested. So calling and requesting are not valid actually, even if valid suspensively and formally. The discussion is only about real request actually, and in it there is a kind of approximation, because in subsumption the compound is not called nor asked for action; rather each one is called and asked, but by treating the non-existent as existent. So it is better to say: Subsumption does not make the non-existent existent; it is pure nothing, so its calling and requesting are not valid actually. Moreover, subsumption is in expression with the word 'existent', and the discussion is not about it, and there is no need for it either, because spoken addresses are not with the word 'existent' but with the word 'people' and the like, and it is applied to the existent as it is applied to the non-existent. So there is no need in expression for subsumption, not in obligation, i.e., subsumption is not in obligation nor does it validate it. Because then each one of the non-existents would be obligated actually and really, so what subsumption benefits is not needed, and what is needed it does not benefit. So let it be reflected upon, for it is more deserving of acceptance. And we have a second argument: that it does not include the child and the insane due to lack of understanding and discernment, so the non-existent is more deserving (of exclusion) after the addresses encompass him. In summary, it is a qiyas of the non-existent on the child and the insane with the commonality of lack of understanding. It was said: The lack of address of obligation to some — the child and the insane — based on the evidence of the lifting of the pen from them does not negate the generality of the address and its coverage verbally of some others. The summary is the lack of commonality in the commonality. I say: Addressing the insane and the like is impossible of intention from the requester due to the absence of its condition, which is understanding and discernment. Perhaps he meant by the child and the insane those who do not understand, so it is not objected that the child is not impossible of intention because he might understand. How could it be, and there has preceded what al-Bayhaqi narrated about the attachment of rulings to hearing with reason before the trench and after it abrogated? So his inclusion is certain. So it does not include them in intention, and if it does not include them due to the absence of understanding and discernment which is present in the non-existent, then it does not include them either. If what is meant is absolute verbal coverage and sense inclusion, it is said: Absolute verbal coverage is not the point of dispute; rather the dispute is about their inclusion in intention. The third article: Linguistic Principles. The Hanbalis said: First, if the non-existent were not included in the address, then it would not be valid to use it as evidence for the inclusion of rulings upon them, and the scholars have never ceased using it as evidence against those in their own times who were non-existent at the time of the address. That (i.e., the aforementioned evidence) is a consensus on generality. We say: It is possible that the evidence is not because of their inclusion in the address in intention but rather due to their knowledge of the generality of the Shari'ah for every responsible being existing from the time of revelation to the Day of Resurrection, and it does not depend on the generality of the spoken address. And they said, second: If the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, were not addressing them, he would not have been sent to them, since there is no communication to them except through these general statements, and there is no sending except by communicating Allah's rulings. We say: The statement that there is no communication except through these general statements is denied. Rather the address is to some verbally — those existing at the time of the address — and for the rest by establishing evidence that their ruling is like their ruling, and thereby the sending is realized. It was said: The Quranic wording corresponds to the inner speech, and this inner speech includes the non-existent, as stated, so the verbal also must include the non-existent, otherwise the correspondence is invalid. We say: The correspondence is not necessary in every respect, due to the necessary difference between actual attachment as in spoken address and suspensive attachment as in inner speech. If there is a difference in attachment and suspension, then divergence in inclusion or non-inclusion of the non-existent is permissible. That is it.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of whether the Shari'ah address (khitab) in the Qur'an and Sunnah includes those who were not present at the time of revelation (al-ma'dum). The author presents multiple arguments and refutations. First, he discusses the claim that since Allah is beyond time, all times are present to Him, so addressing non-existent is possible. He replies that this refers to Allah's knowledge, not actual obligation. Then he addresses the argument of 'taghlib' (subsumption): some say a compound of existent and non-existent can be addressed by subsuming the non-existent under the existent. The author refutes this, stating that the compound itself is non-existent and cannot be addressed; subsumption does not make the non-existent existent and only applies to wording, not obligation. Second, the author uses qiyas: since the address does not include the child and the insane due to lack of understanding, the non-existent is even more deserving of exclusion. Third, the Hanbalis argue that scholars use the address as evidence for later generations, implying inclusion, and that if the Prophet didn't address them he wouldn't be sent to them. The author replies that the evidence is through the generality of Shari'ah and not the original address, and that the Prophet's sending is established by setting up evidence (nash al-dalil) that later generations share the same rulings. The passage ends with a rebuttal to the idea that Qur'anic wording must correspond to inner speech: the correspondence is not necessary in all respects, as actual and suspensive attachments differ. The author consistently defends the Hanafi position that only those present at revelation are directly addressed; later generations are included through the generality of Shari'ah, not the literal address.

Key Terms
تغليب (taghlib): Subsuming the non-existent under the existent in address or expression, treating the non-existent as present for rhetorical purposes.
خطاب شفاهي (khitab shifahi): Spoken address directed to those present at the time of revelation.
تكليف تعليقي (taklif ta'liqi): Suspensive obligation that becomes actual when conditions are met, unlike immediate obligation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, how are later generations obligated by the Qur'anic address?
A) They are directly addressed because Allah is timeless.
B) They are addressed through the rule of taghlib.
C) They are obligated through the generality of Shari'ah and evidence (nash al-dalil).
D) They are not obligated at all because they were not present.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the Shari'ah address (khitab) directly obligates only those present at revelation; later generations are obligated through the generality of Shari'ah and evidence, not through the literal address or rhetorical devices like taghlib.

Inclusion of the Speaker in a General Address (Hanafi Usul)
Determining whether the speaker (Allah or Prophet) is included in the address's scope

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the speaker is included in a general address by default, identify when rational impossibility excludes the speaker, and critique the claim of tabaddur.

Core Idea
The speaker is included in a general address by default; only rational impossibility can exclude.

This lesson examines a key Hanafi usul principle: when a Shari'ah address uses a general formula (e.g., 'all things'), does the speaker (Allah or the Prophet) fall within its scope? The majority of Hanafis affirm inclusion unless reason prevents. We will explore the linguistic basis, the rejection of tabaddur, and the exceptional case of rational impossibility, illustrated by Qur'anic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default rule
State the Hanafi position: the speaker is included if the wording grammatically includes him. The act of speaking is not a qarina (contextual indicator) of exclusion.
Refer to the phrase 'عند الأكثر من الحنفية'
2
Understand the linguistic basis
Analyze why inclusion is default: 'لنا التناول لغة' – the language itself covers the speaker. The burden of proof lies on those who claim exclusion.
Contrast with the opposing view that tabaddur (immediate understanding) suggests exclusion.
3
Rebutt the counterargument of tabaddur
Explain why the claim of tabaddur is rejected: it lacks evidence. The mutabadir is not authoritative without proof.
See passage: 'ودعوى التبادر بخروجه لا تسمع فإنها بلا دليل'
4
Recognize the rational exception
When the ruling is rationally impossible for the speaker, reason excludes him. Example: 'Allah is the Creator of all things' – Allah cannot be created.
This is a specification (takhsis) by reason, not by a linguistic qarina.
5
Examine the nuance of 'shay' (thing)
Note that the word 'shay' can mean either 'entity' or 'volition'. In 'Creator of all things', if 'shay' means 'volition', Allah is linguistically excluded, so no takhsis is needed.
Author says it is correct (فافهم فإنه الصواب).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المتكلم داخل في عموم متعلق الخطاب إن كان داخلاً في الصيغة (عند الأكثر) من الحنفية ،وغيرهم الحاصل أن التكلم ليس قرينة الخروج عن متعلق الخطاب (مثل) قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ [البقرة : ۲۹] وأكرم من أكرمك ولا تهنه وقيل لا يدخل (لنا التناول لغة) لأن الكلام فيما يتناول بحسب اللغة (والعرف المغير (لم يعرف) قالوا : المتبادر خروج المتكلم، أجاب بقوله : ( ودعوى التبادر بخروجه لا تسمع فإنها بلا دليل (نعم، قد يخصص) الخطاب بغير المتكلم (بالعقل) إذا لم يمكن تعلق الحكم به عقلاً (نحو : اللَّهُ خَلِقُ كُل شَيْءٍ [الزمر: (٦٢] بناء على أنه شيء لا كأشياء أخر مخلوقة، ويمكن أن يقرر بأنه شيء بمعنى شاء لا كالأشياء التي بمعنى مشيآت والمراد في الآية المعنى الثاني، فهو تعالى خارج عنه لغة، ولفظ الشيء يطلق على المعنيين فلا تخصيص (فافهم) فإنه الصواب.
Issue: The speaker is included in the generality of the object of the address if he is included in the formula, according to the majority of the Hanafis and others. The conclusion is that the speaker is not a contextual indicator (qarina) of exclusion from the object of the address, such as His statement, may He be exalted: 'And He is of all things Knowing' [Qur'an 2:29] and 'Honor the one who honors you and do not humiliate him.' It was said that he is not included. For us (Hanafis), the linguistic coverage is because the speech is about what is covered linguistically, and the altered custom is not known. They said: The immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) is the exclusion of the speaker. He answered with his statement: And the claim of immediate understanding of his exclusion is not heeded, for it is without evidence. Yes, sometimes the address is specified to other than the speaker by reason if the ruling cannot apply to Him rationally, such as 'Allah is the Creator of all things' [Qur'an 39:62], based on the view that He is a thing unlike other created things. It can be established that He is a thing in the sense of 'will' (sha') not like things in the sense of 'volitions' (mashiyyat), and the intended meaning in the verse is the second meaning. So He, may He be exalted, is linguistically excluded from it, and the word 'thing' is used for both meanings, so there is no specification. So understand, for it is correct.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a short usul rule: When a Shari'ah address uses a general formula (like 'everyone'), does it include the speaker (e.g., Allah in Qur'anic verses, or the Prophet in his commands)? The majority of Hanafis hold that the speaker is included in the scope of the address if the wording grammatically includes him. The mere fact that he is the speaker is not a contextual indicator (qarina) to exclude him. For example, Allah says 'And He is of all things Knowing' — He is included among those who know. Similarly, 'Honor the one who honors you' includes the speaker. Some opponents claim that the immediate understanding (al-mutabadir) is the speaker's exclusion, but the author rejects this as baseless. However, reason may exclude the speaker if the ruling is rationally impossible for Him. For instance, 'Allah is the Creator of all things' cannot include Allah because He is not created. But the author notes that the Arabic word 'shay'' (thing) can mean either 'entity' or 'volition'; in the verse it means volition, so Allah is not included. Thus, the default is inclusion, with exceptions only for rational impossibility.

Key Terms
قرينة (qarina): A contextual indicator that modifies the apparent meaning of an address.
التبادر (al-tabaddur): The immediate understanding that comes to mind upon hearing an utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafis, what is the default ruling regarding the inclusion of the speaker in a general address?
A) The speaker is always excluded.
B) The speaker is included unless reason (aql) prevents it.
C) The speaker is included only if the wording explicitly mentions him.
D) The speaker is never included because he is the authority.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default is that the speaker is included in a general address; only rational impossibility can exclude him.

The Address to One: Not Linguistically General
Establishing that the Shari'ah address to an individual does not extend to all by language

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that an address to one is not linguistically general and evaluate the Hanbali counterargument using the madhhab dispute map.

Core Idea
A command to one person does not obligate all by language alone; generality requires external evidence.

When the Lawgiver speaks to a single individual, does that speech obligate everyone? The Hanafi school holds that by pure linguistic and customary usage, it does not. This lesson unpacks that rule and its dispute with the Hanbalis, who rely on qiyas and a hadith to extend the ruling. Understanding this reveals how the Hanafis prioritize linguistic precision over analogical extension in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
The address to one is linguistically specific; it does not extend to others by language or custom. This is considered necessary and self-evident.
The source text asserts this is from the 'first principles' (al-awwaliyat).
2
Identify the opposing view
The Hanbalis hold that it does extend to all, based on qiyas (by negating individuality) and the hadith 'My ruling on the one is my ruling on the group.'
3
Follow the Hanbali proof
They argue by dropping specificity (ilgha' al-khususiyya) and citing the hadith as a direct statement of universality.
The hadith is quoted in the source text.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that generality, if present, comes from external evidence (dalil kharij), not from the linguistic form. The hadith indicates the ruling is the same, but does not make the address itself general.
The majority (jumhur) do not deny the possibility of external generality.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical effect: whether a command given to one companion obligates all others directly without additional evidence.
Hanafis require external evidence; Hanbalis consider it directly binding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة من أن خطاب الشارع لواحد من الأمة لا يعم غيره لغة وعرفاً، ونقل عن الحنابلة خلافه) الخطاب لواحد من المكلفين يعمهم كلهم، ولما كان القول المختار ضرورياً فإنه من الأوليات، فإنه لغة للواحد والعرف المغير لم يطرأ والمنع مكابرة أوّل كلامهم وقال : (ولعلهم يدعون عمومه للمكلفين (بالقياس) بإلغاء الخصوصية ونفي الفارق ( وبقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم : (حكمي على الواحد حكمي على الجماعة فالعموم بدليل خارجي والجمهور لا ينكرونه، وما استدلوا به من هذا الحديث وفهم الصحابة رضوان الله عليهم لا يفيد أزيد من
Issue: The address of the Lawgiver to one of the community does not include others linguistically and customarily. A contrary view is reported from the Hanbalis: the address to one responsible person applies to all of them. Since the chosen opinion is necessary, it is among the self-evident truths. For it is linguistic for the one, and the altered custom has not occurred, and denial is stubbornness. He interpreted their statement and said: Perhaps they claim its generality for all responsible persons by qiyas, by disregarding specificity and negating the difference, and by his statement, may Allah bless him and his family and grant them peace: 'My ruling upon the one is my ruling upon the group.' So the generality is by an external evidence, and the majority do not deny it. What they used as evidence from this hadith and the understanding of the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, does not give more than
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage states a clear usul rule: When the Lawgiver (e.g., the Prophet) addresses a specific individual (e.g., a companion), that address does not, by pure linguistic and customary usage, apply to all other individuals. The Hanbalis are reported to hold that it does apply to all. The author declares this necessary and self-evident, because the word form is singular and custom has not changed. The Hanbalis' argument for generality is based on qiyas (analogy) by dropping individuality and on the hadith 'My ruling upon the one is my ruling upon the group.' The author responds that such generality comes from an external evidence (dalil kharij), not from the linguistic form itself, and the majority (jumhur) do not deny that it can be general, but the point is that it is not linguistically general. The hadith and Companion understanding do not prove more than that it is general by evidence. The text breaks off but the meaning is clear: the default is that an address to one applies only to that one.

Key Terms
خطاب الشارع (khitab al-shari'): The speech of the Lawgiver (Allah or Prophet) directed to a specific individual.
العموم بدليل خارجي (al-umum bi dalil khariji): Generality that is established by an external evidence, not by the wording itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view, why does an address to one not automatically apply to all?
A) Because the Lawgiver intended only that person.
B) Because linguistically the singular form is exclusive.
C) Because the Companions did not apply it to others.
D) Because the hadith forbids generalization.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, an address to one individual is linguistically and customarily specific; generality requires external evidence.

15 min
Speaker Included in General Address
Rule on whether the speaker is included in the general address

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that the speaker is included in a general address unless rational impossibility prevents, and identify the exception using Quranic examples.

Core Idea
The speaker is included in the general meaning of their own words unless reason forbids it.

When Allah says 'He is Knowing of all things,' does that include Himself? The Hanafi school answers yes, based on the principle that being the speaker does not create an exclusion. This lesson unpacks the rule, its proof from language, and the rational exception, as presented in Fawatih al-Rahamut.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule
State the claim: the speaker is included in the general object of their address if the expression linguistically encompasses them. Note that this is the majority Hanafi view.
The rule opposes the notion that being a speaker automatically excludes one.
2
Examine the evidence
Look at the two examples: the Quranic verse 'And He is of all things Knowing' (2:29) and the command 'Honor the one who honors you.' Both show the general expression covering the speaker. Understand that linguistic inclusion is the proof.
The word 'كل' (all) in the verse is general and includes Allah.
3
Analyze the counterclaim
The text mentions an opposing view (قيل لا يدخل) and the argument that custom (عرف) makes the speaker's exclusion immediately understood. Note the Hanafi rebuttal: such a claim is baseless without proof.
Custom is not recognized as changing the linguistic meaning here.
4
Understand the exception
When reason prevents the application of the ruling to the speaker, the address is specified to exclude them. Example: 'Allah is the Creator of all things' – Allah cannot be His own creation, so He is not included. But the author clarifies this is due to a different meaning of 'thing', not a specification.
The exception is rational, not customary or linguistic.
5
Appreciate the author's conclusion
The author ends with 'فافهم' (understand), emphasizing the correctness of the Hanafi view. Note how the final analysis shows that the word 'thing' can cover both meanings, so no specification is needed.
The rule stands: speaker is included unless rationally impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة (المتكلم داخل في عموم متعلق الخطاب إن كان داخلاً في الصيغة (عند الأكثر) من الحنفية ،وغيرهم الحاصل أن التكلم ليس قرينة الخروج عن متعلق الخطاب (مثل) قوله تعالى : ﴿ وَهُوَ بِكُلِّ شَيْءٍ عَلِيمٌ [البقرة : ۲۹] وأكرم من أكرمك ولا تهنه وقيل لا يدخل (لنا التناول لغة) لأن الكلام فيما يتناول بحسب اللغة (والعرف المغير (لم يعرف) قالوا : المتبادر خروج المتكلم، أجاب بقوله : ( ودعوى التبادر بخروجه لا تسمع فإنها بلا دليل (نعم، قد يخصص) الخطاب بغير المتكلم (بالعقل) إذا لم يمكن تعلق الحكم به عقلاً (نحو : اللَّهُ خَلِقُ كُل شَيْءٍ (الزمر: ٦٢] بناء على أنه شيء لا كأشياء أخر مخلوقة، ويمكن أن يقرر بأنه شيء بمعنى شاء لا كالأشياء التي بمعنى مشيآت والمراد في الآية المعنى الثاني، فهو تعالى خارج عنه لغة، ولفظ الشيء يطلق على المعنيين فلا تخصيص (فافهم) فإنه الصواب.
Issue: The speaker is included in the generality of the object of the address if he is included in the expression, according to the majority of the Hanafis and others. The conclusion is that being the speaker is not a contextual indicator of exclusion from the object of the address. Example: His statement the Exalted: 'And He is of all things Knowing' (Surah al-Baqarah: 29) and 'Honor the one who honors you and do not dishonor him.' It was said that he is not included. For us: the linguistic inclusion, because speech pertains to what it encompasses according to language, and the changed custom is not known. They said: the immediate understanding is the speaker's exclusion. He replied: 'The claim of immediate understanding of his exclusion is not to be heard, for it is without proof. Yes, the address may be specified to exclude the speaker by reason, if the ruling cannot apply to him rationally, like: 'Allah is the Creator of all things' (Surah az-Zumar: 62), on the basis that He is a thing unlike other created things. And it can be established that 'thing' here means 'willed thing' not like things that mean 'volitions'; the intended meaning in the verse is the second, so He is excluded linguistically. The word 'thing' applies to both meanings, so there is no specification. Understand, for it is correct.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a usul ruling: When a general expression is used by a speaker (like Allah or the Prophet), does it include the speaker? The majority of Hanafis hold that the speaker is included if the word form includes him, because being the speaker does not automatically exclude. Example: 'And He is of all things Knowing' includes Allah as knowing Himself. The claim that custom excludes the speaker is rejected as baseless. Exception: if reason prevents inclusion, such as 'Allah is the Creator of all things' where He cannot be His own creation. The author clarifies that the word 'thing' in that verse may mean 'willed thing', so Allah is not included, but this is a linguistic nuance, not a specification. The comment 'فافهم' emphasizes the correctness.

Key Terms
المتكلم (al-mutakallim): The speaker delivering the address (Allah, Prophet, etc.)
قرينة الخروج (qarinat al-khuruj): Contextual indicator that excludes the speaker from the general meaning.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: the speaker is included in the general scope of their address unless rational impossibility prevents, with no automatic exclusion.

Address to One Is Not General: Hanafi Usul on Specificity
Hanafi position that address to one does not include all

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the generality of an address to one individual, identify the opposing school's argument, and apply the principle of tanqih al-manat to distinguish inherent vs. external generality.

Core Idea
A command to one person does not bind all; generality requires external evidence, not linguistic extension.

This lesson tackles a fundamental usul question: when the Prophet (ﷺ) addresses a specific individual, does that ruling apply automatically to all similar cases? The Hanafi school answers with a decisive no, grounding its position in linguistic principles and custom. This lesson walks through the key arguments and the concept of tanqih al-manat, which helps us refine the legal cause to determine if a ruling is individual-specific or generalizable.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
Exactly what is contested? The issue: whether an address (khitab) from the Lawgiver to one specific person of the Ummah includes all other mukallafs linguistically and customarily.
The dispute is about inherent generality, not about whether external evidence can extend it.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
The Hanafi position is that such an address is for that individual only, both linguistically and by custom. Generality (umum) can only come from external evidence (dalil khariji), not from the wording itself.
The author calls this position 'self-evident' (min al-awwaliyyat).
3
Analyze the opposing argument and the Hanafi response
The Hanbalis (apparently) argue by analogy (qiyas) disregarding specificity, and cite the hadith 'My judgment upon one is my judgment upon the congregation' and the practice of the Companions on Ma'iz. The Hanafi response: these only prove generality by external evidence; the Companions may have used general texts, not the specific address.
The concept of tanqih al-manat (refining the rationale) is key: the specific cause in Ma'iz's case (e.g., confession) may not apply to all.
4
Apply tanqih al-manat to the Ma'iz case
The story of Ma'iz shows the Prophet's specific judgment, but Companions like 'Umar applied general stoning texts, not the specific address. Thus, using the specific address as a direct precedent (without checking the cause) is unsound; one must first refine the legal cause to see if it is generalizable.
This demonstrates the Hanafi method: a ruling given to an individual is not automatically precedent for all.

Issue: That the address of the Lawgiver to one of the Ummah does not include others linguistically and customarily, and it has been transmitted from the Hanbalis the opposite: that the address to one of the responsible persons includes them all. But the chosen saying is necessary, for it is among the primary principles: indeed it is linguistically for one, and the changed custom has not occurred; denial is stubbornness. They began their speech and said: Perchance they claim its generality for the responsible persons by analogy (qiyas) by disregarding specificity and negating difference, and by his ﷺ saying: 'My judgment upon one is my judgment upon the congregation.' So generality is by external evidence, and the majority do not deny it. What they adduce from this hadith and the understanding of the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) does not give more than this. And from here, i.e., for the sake of refining the rationale (tanqih al-manat) and this hadith: The Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) judged upon others than Ma'iz what the Prophet ﷺ judged upon him of stoning for adultery. Its story as related by Muslim from Buraydah (may Allah be pleased with him): Ma'iz ibn Malik came to the Prophet and said: O Messenger of Allah, purify me. He said: Woe to you, go back and ask forgiveness of Allah and repent to Him. He said: He went back not far, then came and said: O Messenger of Allah, purify me. The Messenger of Allah said likewise until the fourth time. The Messenger of Allah said: From what shall I purify you? He said: From adultery. The Messenger of Allah asked: Is he mad? He was told he was not mad. He said: Has he drunk wine? A man stood and smelled him and found no smell of wine. He said: Have you committed adultery? He said: Yes. So he ordered him to be stoned. Then indeed here are general texts indicating stoning, like his ﷺ saying: 'Take from me, take from me; Allah has made for them a way: the virgin with the virgin: one hundred lashes and exile for a year; the married with the married: one hundred lashes and stoning.' Related by Muslim. The Commander of the Faithful, 'Umar, said: Allah sent Muhammad with the truth and revealed the Book to him, and among what was revealed was the verse of stoning. The Messenger of Allah stoned, and we stoned after him. Stoning is in the Book of Allah, true upon those who commit adultery and are married from men and women, if there is evidence, pregnancy, or confession. Related by al-Bukhari and Muslim. Many such examples. So then it is not certain that the Companions judged by stoning on the basis of Ma'iz's stoning; rather, it is possible they judged by these general texts, as the Commander of the Faithful, 'Umar, did. But the matter is easy, for this is a discussion in the example. The deduction by the Prophet's judgment upon one to others is reported from the Companions in many places and is well-known among people, and there is no need for explanation. As for their deduction by his saying: 'I have been sent to the black' i.e., the non-Arabs, 'and the red' i.e., the Arabs, related by Ahmad and Ibn Hibban as in al-Taysir, and His saying: 'And We have not sent you except comprehensively to mankind' (Saba: 28), the aspect of deduction is that since the sending was general, his addresses were also general. It is weak, because it does not indicate that all addresses are for everyone. What the hadith and verse indicate is that his sending was to all, and from that it follows that some of his addresses are general to all, not that every address is general to them. This is apparent, so they do not indicate generality at all, neither linguistically, nor customarily, nor by analogy. So reflect.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This is a usul dispute about whether an address to one individual of the Ummah is general to all. The Hanafi position is that it is not general linguistically or customarily; any generality must come from external evidence. The Hanbalis allegedly hold it is general by analogy using the hadith 'My judgment upon one is my judgment upon the congregation.' The author argues that such generality is by external evidence, not inherent. The story of Ma'iz shows that the Companions applied the same ruling to others, but that may have been due to general texts, not the specific address. The hadith about sending to black and red and the verse 'وما أرسلناك إلا كافة للناس' only show that some addresses are general, not all. Thus, the Hanafi view stands: the address to one does not linguistically include others.

Key Terms
تنقيح المناط (tanqih al-manat): Refining the rationale or identifying the precise legal cause.
خطاب (khitab): Address or speech act, particularly from the Lawgiver.
عموم (umum): Generality; inclusion of all individuals or cases under a ruling.

An address to one individual does not linguistically or customarily include all; any generality must be proven by external evidence.

Do Specific Prophetic Addresses Include the Ummah? A Hanafi Usul Dispute
Dispute on whether addresses specific to the Prophet apply to the Ummah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi usul holds that addresses specific to the Prophet (e.g., 'O Prophet') include the Ummah, apply the concept of 'urf, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i linguistic argument.

Core Idea
Custom can override literal specificity: 'O Prophet' addresses the Ummah unless evidence excludes them.

This passage plunges us into a classic usul al-fiqh dispute: does a Quranic address to the Prophet alone, such as 'O Prophet', legally bind the entire Ummah? The Hanafis and Hanbalis answer with a confident 'yes', while the Shafi'is and Malikis say 'no'. The disagreement hinges on whether language's literal specificity can be overridden by customary usage (urf). The author presents the opposing argument and prepares to defend the Hanafi view with evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal question
State the core dispute: do addresses specific to the Prophet include the Ummah? Recognize that this affects whether commands like 'O Prophet, divorce your wives' apply to all Muslims.
2
Contrast the two positions
Note that Hanafis (and Hanbalis) say yes, while Shafi'is (and Malikis) say no. Each bases its view on a different interpretive principle.
Schools are divided along usul lines: Hanafi uses custom; Shafi'i uses literal word meaning.
3
Examine the opponents' dalil
The opponents argue: the word 'Prophet' is a singular noun, so it refers only to one person. Linguistically, an address to one does not include others unless evidence shows otherwise.
This is a strong linguistic argument (dalil lughawi).
4
Understand the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that the intended meaning is not the literal one but the customary one. In Arabic usage, addressing a leader often includes his followers. The word 'prophet' is a description, not a proper name, so it can be used generically.
Custom (urf) is a recognized source of legal interpretation in Hanafi usul.
5
Assess the need for evidence
The opponents object that custom cannot be assumed without proof. The author agrees but then hints that evidence (in later passages) establishes this custom. For now, we note that the debate is about evidential burden.
This is an epistemological issue: what is the default assumption about custom?

This is a usul dispute about whether addresses that are specifically directed to the Prophet (e.g., 'O Prophet') include the entire Ummah. Does an Address to the Prophet Include the Ummah? Hanafi Argument from 'Urf
Hanafi defense of 'urfi generality for Divine addresses to the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that a divine address naming the Prophet customarily includes the ummah, and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i/Maliki evidence.

Core Idea
An address to one who is followed (qudwa) customarily includes the followers.

This lesson explores a key dispute in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: whether Divine addresses that specifically name the Prophet (e.g., 'O Prophet, when you divorce women') apply to the entire ummah. The Hanafis and Hanbalis argue that they do, based on customary understanding ('urf), while the Shafi'is and Malikis restrict them to the Prophet. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi reliance on 'urf shapes legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
State the question: does a Divine address that names the Prophet (e.g., 'O Prophet') include the ummah? The Hanafis say yes, Shafi'is say no.
The passage centers on verses like 65:1 and 33:37.
2
Analyze the Hanafi proof from 'urf
The Hanafis argue that addressing someone with the rank of being followed (like the Prophet) customarily implies inclusion of followers. They compare it to saying 'one like you does not stingy.' This is not a linguistic generality but a 'urfi one.
The key is the construction, not the word 'Prophet' itself.
3
Examine the Shafi'i objections
The Shafi'is object that linguistically a word for one cannot include others, and that such addresses are specific. They interpret the verses as honorific (taḥrīf) or requiring analogy to apply.
They deny that 'urf can override linguistic specificity.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond that custom is established by evidence (Quranic practice), and that specifying only the Prophet is a form of takhṣīṣ of a 'urfi general, which is valid. They further argue that if the addresses were not general, the Quranic verses would lose their benefit.
The thamarah al-khilaf: direct application of verses like talaq to the ummah without additional proof.
