⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة
(خطابه تعالى (للرسول صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم (بخصوصه) نحو: ﴿يَاأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ ﴾ [الأنفال: ٦٤] هل يعم الأمة) أم لا فالحنفية والحنابلة) قالوا (نعم) يعمهم والشافعية والمالكية قالوا (لا) تمسك النفاة أولاً بأن ما للواحد لا يتناول غيره لغة فالخطاب له لا يعم غيره ويجاب بأن المراد تناوله عرفاً ولا ينفعه ما ذكروا (قيل : الأصل عدم طريان العرف فدعوى العرف خلاف الأصل : فلا يثبت إلا بالدليل (أقول : دلت الأدلة الآتية على ثبوته أي ثبوت العرف ثم قيل إنه من الضروريات أن لفظ النبي ليس مستعملاً في العموم قطعاً، وتحقيق كلامنا أن المقصود أن خطاب من له رتبة الاقتداء يدل عرفاً على شمول الحكم لمن يقتدي به لا بأن اللفظ الموضوع بإزاء من له رتبة الاقتداء مستعمل فيه وفي أغياره مقتديه حق يكون خلاف البديهة، بل نقول إن هذا التركيب أي تعلق الخطاب بمن له رتبة الاقتداء عرفاً لطلب الحكم منه ومن أتباعه كما أن قولك : مثلك لا يبخل فإنه يدل على الحكم على المثل بعدم البخل لغة لكن أمثال هذا التركيب في العرف لنفي البخل عن المخاطب، كذا هذا (و) تمسكوا :(ثانياً) : لو كان الخطاب المذكور عاماً (يلزم أن يكون التنصيص على أنه المراد فقط تخصيصاً وليس كذلك إجماعاً (ويجاب) في «شرح المختصر» بمنع بطلان اللازم ولا نسلم الاتفاق عليه فإنا قائلون بكونه تخصيصاً فإنه كما يرد على العام يرد على العام عرفاً وهذا عام عرفي قد يخصص بالبعض، والتحقيق أنك قد عرفت أن هذا التركيب عرفاً لتناول الحكم للمقتدى به وأتباعه، فإذا أريد الاختصاص به فقد تغير مما له في العرف إلى ما ليس له من الحكم على البعض قطعاً، فإن هذا التغير تخصيصاً فتخصيص للتركيب والتخصيص كما يرد على المفرد يرد على المركب كسائر المجازات فإنها كما ترد على المفردات ترد على المركبات كما بين في علم البيان ، فإن أراد شارح المختصر بالتزام التخصيص، هذا فهو حق، وإن أراد التخصيص في المفرد فهو إحسان إلى من لا يقبله، فإنا لا المفرد الذي وضع بإزاء من له رتبة الاقتداء به للإتباع حتى يكون معنى لفظ النبي هو صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم وأتباعه حتى يكون إرادته صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم فقط منه تخصيصاً له واحتج المعممون أولاً بأن الرسول له منصب الاقتداء به في كل شيء) فإنه بعث لذلك (إلا بدليل) صارف وكل من هو كذلك يفهم من أمره شمول أتباعه عرفاً) لا بجامع مشترك حتى يكون عمومه كعموم ما للواحد للكل فإنه مع قطع النظر عن الجامع يدل هذا التركيب في العرف على العموم ( ومنع الشيخ ابن الحاجب) هذا الفهم (مكابرة) وإنكار للضروري، وقد يقرر المنع بأن الشمول للإتباع بواسطة وجوب الاقتداء مسلم والفهم عن نفس اللفظ ممنوع والكلام فيه وجوابه أن المقصود أن هذا التركيب يدل عرفاً على الشمول، وإن كان حدوث ذلك العرف بواسطة الاقتداء، لا أن الشمول يفهم بأن حكم الإتباع والمتبوع واحد بدلالة نص أو قياس، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، وأما منع دلالة المفرد الموضوع بإزاء المتبوع على الشمول فليس بمكابرة لكنه في غير محل النزاع فتدبر (و) احتجوا (ثانياً) بقوله: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ إِذَا طَلَقْتُمُ النساء [الطلاق: ( الخطاب للنبي لله والمراد هو وأتباعه (و) بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا قَضَى زَيْدُ منها وَطَراً زَوَحْنَكَهَا لِكَيْ لَا يَكُونَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ حَرَجٌ فِي أَزْوَج أَدْعِيَابِهِمْ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٣٧] فإنه لو لم يكن الخطاب له متناولاً للأتباع لما تحصل هذه الفائدة وتزويجه صلى الله عليه وسلم زوجة زيد (و) بقوله وَامْرَأَةٌ مُؤْمِنَةٌ إِن وَهَبَتْ نَفْسَهَا لِلنَّبِي إِنْ أَرَادَ النَّبِيُّ أَن يَسْتَنكِحَهَا خَالِصَةً لَكَ مِن دُونِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٠] فإنه لو لم يكن الحكم له عليه وآله السلام عاماً للأتباع لما كان لهذا القول فائدة، وأجاب الشافعية عن الأول بأن ذكر النبي الا للتشريف، والمقصود ذكر الخطاب العام، وعن الثاني بأنه تنصيص على ثبوت الأتباع وإشارة إلى الإلحاق بالقياس، وعن الثالث
Issue: Does His address to the Messenger specifically, such as 'O Prophet', include the ummah or not? The Hanafis and Hanbalis said yes, it includes them, and the Shafi'is and Malikis said no. The negators hold first that what is for one does not include others linguistically, so the address to him does not include others. The response is that what is meant is inclusion by custom ('urf). They said: the default is absence of custom, so claiming custom is contrary to the default and cannot be established without evidence. I say: the following proofs establish custom. Then it is said that it is self-evident that the word 'Prophet' is not used for absolute generality. The actual argument: an address to one who has the rank of being followed indicates customarily the inclusion of those who follow him, not that the word itself is used for him and his followers, which would be against intuition. Rather, this construction of addressing one with the rank of being followed customarily demands the ruling for him and his followers, just as saying 'one like you does not stingy' linguistically indicates a ruling of non-stinginess for a like, but customarily such constructions are used to negate stinginess from the addressee. Similarly this. Second, they hold: if the mentioned address were general, then specifying that only the Prophet is meant would be a specification, which is not agreed upon. The response in Sharh al-Mukhtasar denies the necessity of the consequent and we do not concede agreement, for we consider it specification; specification can apply to a 'urfi general just as it applies to a linguistic general. The reality: this construction customarily includes the followed and his followers; if only the followed is intended, that is a change from what is customary, which is specification. Specification can apply to a compound as it applies to a single word, like other metaphors. If the commentator meant specification of the compound, that is correct; if he meant specification of the single word, that is unjustified. The generalizers (mu'amminun) argue first: the Messenger has the rank of being followed in everything, for he was sent for that, unless there is evidence to the contrary. Anyone in such a position, his command is customarily understood to include his followers, not by a common factor (jami' mushtarak) so that it would be like the generality of a word for all. Rather, this construction customarily indicates generality regardless of the common factor. Ibn al-Hajib's denial of this understanding is obstinacy and denial of the obvious. The denial is sometimes formulated as: inclusion of followers via obligation of following is conceded, but understanding from the very word is denied. The response: the point is that this construction customarily indicates inclusion, even if that custom arose due to the obligation to follow, not that inclusion is understood by a proof that the ruling of follower and followed is the same. Denying this is obstinacy. Second, they argued with His saying: 'O Prophet, when you divorce women'—the address is to the Prophet but it is intended for him and his followers. And with His saying: 'When Zayd had fulfilled from her his need, We married her to you so that there be no blame upon the believers in their adopted sons' wives'—if the address were not inclusive of followers, this benefit would not be obtained. And with His saying: 'And a believing woman if she gives herself to the Prophet, if the Prophet desires to marry her, exclusively for you, not for the believers'—if the ruling were not general for the followers, this statement would have no benefit. The Shafi'is respond to the first: mentioning the Prophet is for honor, and the intended is the general address. To the second: it is a specification of the followers' ruling and an indication of annexation by analogy. And to the third: ...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a dispute (usul al-fiqh) about whether Divine addresses that specifically name the Prophet (e.g., 'O Prophet') include the entire ummah. The Hanafis and Hanbalis hold that they do include the ummah, while the Shafi'is and Malikis hold that they do not. The author first presents the arguments of the negators: linguistically, a word for one cannot cover others; custom (urf) cannot be claimed against the default. The author responds that custom is established by evidence, and the key insight is that the construction of addressing a person of exemplary rank (like the Prophet) customarily connotes inclusion of his followers. He compares it to the phrase 'one like you does not stingy,' which customarily negates stinginess from the addressee. Then he addresses the second objection: if it were general, specifying only the Prophet would be an invalid specification. He argues that this is actually a valid specification of a 'urfi general, which is permissible. He then presents the Hanafi arguments: first, the Prophet is sent to be followed in everything, so his commands are customarily understood as including his followers. Denying this is obstinacy. Second, Quranic verses like 'O Prophet, when you divorce women' (65:1) are understood to address the ummah, as the plural verb 'you divorce' shows. Also, verses about marrying Zayd's ex-wife and the exclusive marriage of a believing woman to the Prophet (33:37, 33:50) would be pointless if they did not imply a ruling for the ummah. The Shafi'i counter-arguments are briefly presented. The passage is a good example of Hanafi reliance on 'urf (custom) as a source of legal understanding, and the debate revolves around whether the understanding comes from the word itself or from contextual custom.

Key Terms
خطاب (khiṭāb): An address or communication directed at someone, in this context a Divine address naming the Prophet.
عرف (ʿurf): Customary usage of language; the way people naturally understand certain constructions.
عموم عرفي (ʿumūm ʿurfī): A general meaning that is understood by custom, not by the literal word itself.
اقتداء (iqtidā'): Following, emulation; specifically the status of the Prophet as one to be followed (qudwa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafis, why does the verse 'O Prophet, when you divorce women' (65:1) apply to the entire ummah?
A) Because the word 'Prophet' linguistically includes all people.
B) Because the plural verb 'you divorce' shows multiple actors, and custom dictates that an address to a leader includes his followers.
C) Because the verse was revealed to abrogate a previous ruling.
D) Because all Quranic verses are general unless specified otherwise.

Key Takeaway
A Divine address to the Prophet can be a 'urfi general that includes the ummah, based on the Prophet's role as one to be followed and customary understanding.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
12 min
Customary Generality: Addressing the Prophet Includes the Ummah
Stating the argument that addressing the Prophet implies followers are included.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi concept of 'urfi 'amm to explain why an address to the Prophet includes the followers, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
The Prophet's address is customarily general—his commands include the ummah unless evidence specifies otherwise.

This lesson explores a key usul dispute: when the Qur'an addresses the Prophet directly, does the ruling extend to the entire ummah? The Hanafis argue that customary usage ('urf) makes such constructions inherently general, while the Shafi'is see them as specific to the Prophet unless proven otherwise. Through Quranic examples and linguistic reasoning, we will unpack the Hanafi position and its practical implications for deriving rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi claim
Understand that Hanafis argue addressing the one with iqtida' (status of being followed) customarily includes the followers. This is not literal word meaning but a construction-based custom.
Key term: 'umum 'urfi (customary generality).
2
Examine the linguistic analogy
The author draws an analogy to sayings like 'Mithluka la yabkhalu' (The likes of you are not stingy), which customarily apply to the addressee, not literally to all 'likes'. This shows how constructions acquire customary scope.
The analogy clarifies that the ruling is on the addressee but customarily includes all relevant parties.
3
Analyze the Quranic proofs
Look at verses 65:1, 33:37, and 33:50. In each, the address is to the Prophet but the ruling necessarily applies to the ummah (e.g., divorce rules, marriage of Zaynab, hiba of a believing woman). The benefit (fā'idah) would not be achieved if limited to the Prophet alone.
The verses are cited as evidence (dalīl) for customary inclusion.
4
Understand the Shafi'i objections and Hanafi response
Shafi'is respond: (1) the mention of 'Prophet' is for honor (tashrīf), not to indicate inclusion; (2) the benefit in 33:37 comes from analogy (qiyās); (3) 33:50 prevents analogy. Hanafis refute that these objections miss the point—the custom is established and the examples illustrate that custom (amārāt mulhimah).
The author dismisses the objections as futile (tā'iha), emphasizing that the custom is evident from contextual indicators (qarā'in).
5
Apply the principle in istinbat
When a ruling is addressed to the Prophet, the default Hanafi approach is to include the ummah unless a specific evidence (dalīl) restricts it. This affects how we derive fiqh rulings from such verses.
Practical outcome: commands like 'O Prophet, struggle against the disbelievers' apply to all Muslims.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية
نقول بعموم
سمي العموم قطعاً، وتحقيق كلامنا أن المقصود أن خطاب من له رتبة الاقتداء يدل عرفاً على شمول الحكم لمن يقتدي به لا بأن اللفظ الموضوع بإزاء من له رتبة الاقتداء مستعمل فيه وفي أغياره مقتديه حق يكون خلاف البديهة، بل نقول إن هذا التركيب أي تعلق الخطاب بمن له رتبة الاقتداء عرفاً لطلب الحكم منه ومن أتباعه كما أن قولك : مثلك لا يبخل فإنه يدل على الحكم على المثل بعدم البخل لغة لكن أمثال هذا التركيب في العرف لنفي البخل عن المخاطب، كذا هذا (و) تمسكوا :(ثانياً) : لو كان الخطاب المذكور عاماً (يلزم أن يكون التنصيص على أنه المراد فقط تخصيصاً وليس كذلك إجماعاً (ويجاب) في «شرح المختصر» بمنع بطلان اللازم ولا نسلم الاتفاق عليه فإنا قائلون بكونه تخصيصاً فإنه كما يرد على العام يرد على العام عرفاً وهذا عام عرفي قد يخصص بالبعض، والتحقيق أنك قد عرفت أن هذا التركيب عرفاً لتناول الحكم للمقتدى به وأتباعه، فإذا أريد الاختصاص به فقد تغير مما له في العرف إلى ما ليس له من الحكم على البعض قطعاً، فإن هذا تغير تخصيصاً فتخصيص للتركيب والتخصيص كما يرد على المفرد يرد على المركب كسائر المجازات فإنها كما ترد على المفردات ترد على المركبات كما بين في علم البيان ، فإن أراد شارح المختصر بالتزام التخصيص، هذا فهو حق، وإن أراد التخصيص في المفرد فهو إحسان إلى من لا يقبله، فإنا لا المفرد الذي وضع بإزاء من له رتبة الاقتداء به للإتباع حتى يكون معنى لفظ النبي هو صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم وأتباعه حتى يكون إرادته صلى الله عليه وآله وسلم فقط منه تخصيصاً له واحتج المعممون أولاً بأن الرسول له منصب الاقتداء به في كل شيء فإنه بعث لذلك (إلا بدليل صارف وكل من هو كذلك يفهم من أمره شمول أتباعه عرفاً) لا بجامع مشترك حتى يكون عمومه كعموم ما للواحد للكل فإنه مع قطع النظر عن الجامع يدل هذا التركيب في العرف على العموم ( ومنع الشيخ ابن الحاجب) هذا الفهم (مكابرة) وإنكار للضروري، وقد يقرر المنع بأن الشمول للإتباع بواسطة وجوب الاقتداء مسلم والفهم عن نفس اللفظ ممنوع والكلام فيه وجوابه أن المقصود أن هذا التركيب يدل عرفاً على الشمول، وإن كان حدوث ذلك العرف بواسطة الاقتداء، لا أن الشمول يفهم بأن حكم الإتباع والمتبوع واحد بدلالة نص أو قياس، وإنكار هذا مكابرة، وأما منع دلالة المفرد الموضوع بإزاء المتبوع على الشمول فليس بمكابرة لكنه في غير محل النزاع فتدبر (و) احتجوا (ثانياً) بقوله: ﴿يَأَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ إِذَا طَلَقْتُمُ النساء [الطلاق: ( الخطاب للنبي لله والمراد هو وأتباعه (و) بقوله تعالى: ﴿فَلَمَّا قَضَى زَيْدُ منها وَطَراً زَوَحْنَكَهَا لِكَيْ لَا يَكُونَ عَلَى الْمُؤْمِنِينَ حَرَجٌ فِي أَزْوَج أَدْعِيَابِهِمْ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٣٧] فإنه لو لم يكن الخطاب له متناولاً للأتباع لما تحصل هذه الفائدة وتزويجه صلى الله عليه وسلم زوجة زيد (و) بقوله وَامْرَأَةٌ مُؤْمِنَةٌ إِن وَهَبَتْ نَفْسَهَا لِلنَّبِي إِنْ أَرَادَ النَّبِيُّ أَن يَسْتَنكِحَهَا خَالِصَةً لَكَ مِن دُونِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ ﴾ [الأحزاب: ٥٠] فإنه لو لم يكن الحكم له عليه وآله السلام عاماً للأتباع لما كان لهذا القول فائدة، وأجاب الشافعية عن الأول بأن ذكر النبي الا للتشريف، والمقصود ذكر الخطاب العام، وعن الثاني بأنه تنصيص على ثبوت الأتباع وإشارة إلى الإلحاق بالقياس، وعن الثالث أن الفائدة المنع عن الإلحاق بالقياس، وأراد المصنف دفع هذه الأجوبة فقال: (اعلم أن المراد) من هذه الأمثلة (بيان) التناول العرفي واستقراره في النفوس، وهذه أمارات مفهمة) للتناول العرفي (فمناقشات المخالفين) فيها (طائحة) فإنه لا يزيد على المناقشة في المثال، والمقصود أن هذه قرائن انفهام العموم فتدبر .
The third article on linguistic principles: We say regarding generality that it is definitively called generality. The gist of our speech is that the intended meaning is that the address to one who has the status of being followed indicates, by custom, the inclusion of the ruling for those who follow him—not that the word placed in front of the one who has the status of being followed is used for him and for others (his followers) which would be contrary to intuition. Rather, we say that this construction—i.e., the attachment of the address to one who has the status of being followed—is customarily for demanding the ruling from him and his followers, just as when you say 'The likes of you do not stingy' indicates the ruling of the like regarding lack of stinginess linguistically, but such constructions are customarily for negating stinginess from the addressee. Similarly here. And they argued, secondly: If the mentioned address were general, then specifying that only he is intended would be a specification, but it is not so by consensus. It is answered in Sharh al-Mukhtasar by denying the necessary consequence, and we do not concede agreement on that, for we hold it to be a specification just as it occurs in a general term, it occurs in a general customarily—this is a customary general which may be specified to some. The verification is that you know that this construction is customarily for the ruling to cover the followed one and his followers. If only he is intended, then it has changed from what it customarily is to what is not—ruling on only some parts definitely, for this change is a specification, so it is a specification of the construction, and specification just as it occurs in a single word occurs in a compound, like other metaphors that occur in single words and compounds as explained in rhetoric. If the author of Sharh al-Mukhtasar intended to affirm specification, that is correct; if he intended specification in the single word, then it is addressing someone who does not accept it, for we do not [accept] that the single word placed for one with status of being followed includes followers so that the meaning of the word 'Prophet' is he and his followers, so that intending only him would be a specification. And those who advocate generality argued first: that the Messenger has the status of being followed in everything, for he was sent for that (except if there is a contrary evidence) and everyone like that, from his command, the inclusion of his followers is understood customarily—not by a common factor so that his generality would be like the generality of a word for a whole—for apart from the common factor, this construction customarily indicates generality. And Shaykh Ibn al-Hajib denied this understanding as obstinacy and denial of the obvious. The denial may be formulated as: the inclusion of followers by virtue of obligation to follow is conceded, but the understanding from the word itself is denied, and the discussion is about that. The answer is that the intended meaning is that this construction customarily indicates inclusion, even if that custom arises through the obligation to follow, not that inclusion is understood because the ruling of followers and followed is one by textual or analogical evidence. Denial of this is obstinate. As for denying the indication of the single word placed for the followed on inclusion, that is not obstinate but it is not the point of contention, so reflect. And they argued secondly with His saying: 'O Prophet, when you divorce women'—the address is to the Prophet, but the meaning includes him and his followers. And with His saying: 'When Zayd had fulfilled his desire from her, We married her to you so that there should not be any difficulty for the believers in respect of wives of their adopted sons'—for if the address did not include followers, this benefit would not be achieved, i.e., his marriage to Zayd's wife. And with His saying: 'And a believing woman if she gives herself to the Prophet, if the Prophet wishes to marry her, exclusively for you, not for the believers'—for if the ruling for him were not general for followers, this statement would have no benefit. The Shafi'is answered the first by saying that mentioning the Prophet is for honor, and the intended address is general; the second by saying that it is a specification of the affirmation for followers and an indication of joining by analogy; and the third by saying that the benefit is the prevention of analogy. And the author intended to refute these answers by saying: Know that the purpose of these examples is to show the customary inclusion and its establishment in minds, and these are indicators of customary inclusion. So the objections of the opponents against them are futile, for they amount to no more than objecting to the example, and the point is that these are contextual indicators of the understanding of generality, so reflect.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute regarding whether an address to the Prophet (SAW) includes his followers by default. The author is explaining the Hanafi position that such addresses are 'urfi 'amm (customary general) and not literal or analytical generality. The proof involves linguistic custom (urf) and Quranic examples. The Shafi'i counterarguments are presented and refuted. The passage demonstrates that the disagreement is based on the nature of 'urf and the method of understanding texts. The Hanafi position relies on the idea that the construction itself (addressing a leader) has acquired a customary usage to include followers, which is a matter of linguistic convention.

Key Terms
عموم عرفي ('umum 'urfi): Customary generality where the construction is understood to include all relevant entities based on common usage.
تخصيص (takhsis): Specification, the act of limiting a general term to some of its individuals.
اقتداء (iqtida'): Status of being followed, leadership in religious matters.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why does addressing the Prophet (SAW) with a command include his followers?
A) Because the word 'Prophet' literally means 'the one with followers'.
B) Because of customary usage ('urf) where such constructions are understood to include followers.
C) Because of an explicit textual proof from the Quran that followers are included in every command.
D) Because of qiyas (analogy) between the Prophet and other leaders.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that addressing the Prophet customarily includes the ummah unless there is evidence to specify, based on established linguistic custom.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
8 min
Idafah of a Plural to a Plural: Distribution vs. Generality
Usul dispute on plural idafah and generality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of distribution (inqisam al-ahad ala al-ahad) to the construction jam' mudaf ila jam' and evaluate the opposing Shafi'i argument using induction (istiqra').

Core Idea
When a plural is added to a plural, Hanafis see distribution; Shafi'is see generality — the difference lies in the role of linguistic induction.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut examines the famous usul dispute over the Quranic verse 'Khudh min amwalihim sadaqah.' The issue centers on whether the idafah of a plural (amwal) to a plural (hum) requires taking charity from every type of wealth or merely from each individual. The Hanafis rely on induction from Arabic usage to argue for distribution; the Shafi'is argue for generality. Let us walk through the arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position and its dalil
The Hanafis hold that the phrase 'min amwalihim' indicates distribution of individuals over individuals, not comprehensive inclusion. Their proof is induction (istiqra') from common Arabic constructions such as 'rakibu dawabbahum' (they rode their mounts) and 'ja'alu asabi'ahum fi adhanihim' (they put their fingers in their ears), where each individual performs the action individually.
The Hanafi argument is that the immediate understanding (tabadur) of such constructions is distribution, without needing a contextual clue.
2
Identify the opposing views
Imam Zufar and al-Karkhi argue that taking one charity from the total wealth suffices to fulfill the command, while Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the idafah implies generality, so charity must be taken from every type of wealth of each owner.
The Shafi'i position is based on the principle that idafah to a definite plural gives generality, akin to 'amm.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
The Hanafis respond by denying the necessary connection in the opposing arguments. They assert that induction based on predominance (ghalabah) is valid even if some cases deviate due to contextual factors. The objection that some constructions (e.g., 'yahmiluna awzarahum') seem to indicate a different reading is countered by noting that those cases have a diverting factor (sarf) and do not undermine the predominant pattern.
The key distinction: istiqra' is based on what is prevalent in usage, not on universal application.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence is in zakat: Hanafis hold that zakat is due on each type of wealth that reaches nisab separately, but not on every item within a type because the verse suggests taking from each individual's wealth, not from every type. Shafi'is may require zakat on every type that reaches nisab separately, and possibly on more items.
This difference affects how zakat is calculated for someone with multiple types of wealth.
5
Evaluate the strength of the Hanafi argument
The Hanafi use of istiqra' is methodologically sound because language is learned through predominant usage. The existence of exceptions does not invalidate the general rule. The burden is on the opponent to prove that the construction in the verse is one of those exceptions.
This shows the Hanafi commitment to linguistic reality as the basis for usul.

Source Text

مسألة
وخُذْ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً ﴾ [التوبة : ۱۰۳] لا يقتضي أخذها من كل نوع يعني أن الجمع المضاف إلى جمع لا يقتضي عموم آحاد الأول بالنسبة إلى كل واحد واحد من آحاد الثاني، وتكلموا في جزئي من جزئياته وهو قوله تعالى : خُذْ مِنْ أَمْوَالِهِمْ صَدَقَةً ﴾ [التوبة : ١٠٣] (أما عند الحنفية فلأن مقابلة الجمع بالجمع تفيد انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد) فالمعنى خذ من مال غني مال ومن غني آخر صدقة أخرى وهذا لا يقتضي الأخذ من جميع أموال واحد واحد ولا يقصدا استغراق آحاد مال كل ولا أنواعه واستدلوا بالاستقراء نحو ركبوا دوابهم، وجعلوا أصابعهم في آذانهم فإن المعنى ركب كل واحد واحد على دابته وجعل واحد واحد أصبعه في أذنه (إلى غير ذلك) نحو ( فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] فإن قلت الانقسام ههنا لعدم صحة العموم، فإن رجلاً لا يركب إلا دابة ولا يجعل جميع أصابعه في آذان كل ولا يغسل إلاّ وجهه، فالاستقراء فيما فيه قرينة صارفة عن العموم مسلم لكن غير مفيد، ومطلقاً ممنوع، قلت : التتبع في الاستعمالات يحكم بأن المتبادر من مقابلة الجمع بالجمع الانقسام من غير توقف على القرينة (ونقض بقوله تعالى: ﴿وَهُمْ يَحْمِلُونَ أَوْزَارَهُمْ عَلَى ظُهُورِهِمْ ﴾ [الأنعام: ٣١] ) فإن المعنى يحمل يوم القيامة كل واحد وزره على ظهره، وليس المقصود الانقسام (أقول: التخلف في بعض المواد الصارف كما في المثال المذكور (لا يضر الاستقراء لأن مبناه على الغلبة) والغلبة في الاستعمال لإرادة انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد فتأمل، وأما عند الإمام (زفر) والشيخ الإمام أبي الحسن (الكرخي منا والآمدي ومن تبعهم فلأنه إذا أخذ صدقة واحدة من جملة أموالهم صدق أنه أخذ من أموالهم صدقة) فلا الأخذ من كل نوع من أنواع مال كل واحد (ويجاب عنه بمنع الملازمة) فإنها دعوى في قوة نفس المطلوب فمن لا يسلمه لا يسلمها، قيل : إن عموم الجمع مجموعي، فالمعنى من مجموع مال كل واحد ولا يقتضي هذا الأخذ من كل نوع وسيجيء ما فيه، ولك أن تبني هذا على أن من للتبعيض فالمعنى من بعض أموال واحد واحد، وهذا إنما يتم لو كان الكلام في خصوص هذه الآية لكنه عام سواء كان مدخول من أولا فتدبر والأكثر من غيرنا (ومنهم) الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه على أنه يوجب أخذ الصدقة من كل نوع وفي بعض شروح المنهاج أنه رحمه الله تعالى نص في رسالة الإمام (لأنه جمع مضاف إلى كل واحد من آحاد الجمع (وهو للعموم) فيعم كل نوع من مال كل واحد واحد من الملاك فالمعنى : خذ من كل مال لكل من المالكين (وأورد) عليه (أولاً)) أن كل دينار (مال فلو كان عاماً لدخل كل دينار فيه ويجب أخذ الصدقة منه ولا يجب
Issue: 'Take from their wealth a charity' [Quran 9:103] does not require taking from every type. Meaning that the plural added to a plural does not require generality of the individuals of the first relative to every single individual of the second. They spoke about a specific part of its parts, namely His saying: 'Take from their wealth a charity' [Quran 9:103]. (As for the Hanafis, it is because the pairing (muqabala) of plural with plural yields the distribution of individuals over individuals). So the meaning is: take from the wealth of a rich person a charity, and from another rich person another charity—this does not require taking from all the wealth of one person nor intending comprehensiveness of the individuals of his wealth nor its types. They provided evidence by induction (istiqra') such as 'they rode their mounts' and 'they put their fingers in their ears'—the meaning is each one rode his mount and each one put his finger in his ear. And others like 'wash your faces' [Quran 5:6]. If you say that distribution here is due to the impossibility of generality (since a man rides only one mount, does not put all his fingers in each ear, and washes only his face), then induction in cases where there is a context that diverts from generality is conceded but not useful, and absolute is rejected. I say: Investigation of usages shows that the immediate understanding from pairing plural with plural is distribution, without relying on context. And it is objected by His saying: 'And they shall bear their burdens on their backs' [Quran 6:31]—the meaning is each one carries his burden on his back, and distribution is not intended. I say: Deviation in some cases due to a diverting factor, as in the mentioned example, does not harm induction because induction is based on predominance. And the predominance in usage is the intention of distribution of individuals over individuals, so reflect. As for Imam Zufar, Shaykh Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, al-Amidi, and those who follow them, it is because if one takes a single charity from the totality of their wealth, it is true that he took from their wealth a charity, so it does not require taking from every type of each person's wealth. And it is answered by denying the necessary connection, for that claim is in itself the strength of the very thing sought; whoever does not concede it does not concede. It is said that the generality of the plural is collective, so the meaning is from the total wealth of each one, and this does not require taking from every type. Something will come on this. And you may build this on that 'min' is partitive, so the meaning is from some wealth of each one. This is only complete if the discussion is about this specific verse, but it is general whether 'min' enters or not, so reflect. And most of the others—among them Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him—hold that it requires taking charity from every type. In some commentaries on al-Minhaj, it is stated that he explicitly said in a letter (Risalah) (because it is a plural added to each individual of the plural, and it is for generality) so it covers every type of wealth of each individual owner. Thus the meaning is: take from every wealth of each of the owners. And it is objected, firstly: that every dinar is wealth, so if it were general, it would include every dinar and taking charity from it would be obligatory, but it is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed fiqh-usul discussion on the interpretation of idafah construction where a plural noun is added to a plural noun (مضاف إلى جمع). The specific verse under discussion is 'Take from their wealth a charity' (9:103). The issue is whether this requires taking charity from each and every type of wealth of each person. The Hanafi position, supported by induction (istiqra') from common Arabic usage, is that such constructions indicate distribution (انقسام) of individuals over individuals, not universal inclusion. For example, 'they rode their mounts' means each person rode his own mount, not that each person rode all mounts. Similarly, 'take from their wealth' means take one charity from one wealthy person and another from another, but not necessarily from every type of their wealth. The text then discusses different opinions: Imam Zufar and al-Karkhi argue that taking a single charity from the total qualifies as taking 'from their wealth', while the Shafi'i position holds that it requires taking from every type because the idafah implies generality. The passage ends with an objection to the Shafi'i view: if every dinar is wealth, then one would have to take charity from every single dinar, which is not required.

Key Terms
جمع مضاف إلى جمع (jam' mudaf ila jam'): A plural noun that is added (in idafah) to another plural noun.
انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد (inqisam al-ahad 'ala al-ahad): Distribution of individuals over individuals, i.e., each single of the first applies to a single of the second.
استقراء (istiqra'): Induction, inferring a rule by examining many instances of usage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the primary evidence for interpreting 'take from their wealth a charity' as indicating distribution, not generality?
A) The literal meaning of the word 'min' as partitive.
B) Induction from common Arabic phrases like 'they rode their mounts'.
C) A hadith of the Prophet specifying that zakat is not due on every dinar.
D) The consensus of the companions.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle that a plural added to a plural indicates distribution is supported by linguistic induction, and this directly impacts the fiqh ruling on zakat obligations.

The Hanafi Interpretation of 'خذ من أموالهم صدقة': Distribution vs. Generality
Analyzing a disputed usul case about جمع مضاف to جمع

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the interpretation of the plural construct (الجمع المضاف) in the verse 'خذ من أموالهم صدقة' and apply the usul principle of distribution (انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد) to similar texts.

Core Idea
The Hanafi default for a plural in construct to a plural is distribution, not generality.

This lesson explores a classic usul dispute centered on the Quranic verse 'Take from their wealth a charity' (9:103). The Hanafi school argues that pairing a plural with a plural indicates that each individual's wealth is addressed separately, not that every type of wealth must be taken. The opposing view, held by al-Shafi'i, sees the plural in construct as general. By analyzing the arguments and inductive evidence, you will understand how Hanafi usul principles govern the interpretation of such ambiguous constructions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed phrase
Locate the Quranic phrase 'خذ من أموالهم صدقة' and note that 'أموالهم' is a plural in construct to a plural (the implied owners). This is the key grammatical structure under debate.
The construct state (إضافة) of a plural to another plural is called 'الجمع المضاف إلى الجمع'.
2
State the Hanafi position
The Hanafi school holds that this structure implies distribution (انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد): each individual's wealth is considered separately, so taking from some types of each person's wealth suffices.
The evidence is based on istiqra' (inductive survey) of common Arabic usage like 'ركبوا دوابهم' (each rode his own mount).
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
Imam al-Shafi'i argues that the plural in construct is general (للعموم), necessitating taking from every type of wealth from each individual.
Shafi'is rely on the apparent generality of the construct and reject the inductive argument as being confined to cases with contextual clues.
4
Analyze the Hanafi responses to objections
Review the three objections raised against the Shafi'i view and the Hanafi counterarguments, including the consensus excluding every single dinar, the differentiation from 'كل' structures, and the concept of collective (مجموعي) vs. distributive generality.
The Hanafi responses reinforce the default of distribution and the role of external evidence (e.g., consensus) in specifying general terms.
5
Apply the principle to a new text
Consider another Quranic phrase like 'وَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ' (wash your faces). Using the same principle, determine whether it implies washing each individual's entire face or just some part.
The inductive rule applies unless a contextual clue (qarina) diverts it.

في أذنه (إلى غير ذلك) نحو ( فَاغْسِلُوا وُجُوهَكُمْ ﴾ [المائدة: ٦] فإن قلت الانقسام ههنا لعدم صحة العموم، فإن رجلاً لا يركب إلا دابة ولا يجعل جميع أصابعه في آذان كل ولا يغسل إلاّ وجهه، فالاستقراء فيما لأن مبناه على الغلبة) والغلبة في الاستعمال لإرادة انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد فتأمل، وأما عند الإمام (زفر) والشيخ الإمام أبي الحسن (الكرخي منا والآمدي ومن تبعهم فلأنه إذا أخذ صدقة واحدة من جملة أموالهم للعموم) فيعم كل نوع من مال كل واحد واحد من الملاك فالمعنى : خذ من كل مال لكل من المالكين (وأورد) عليه (أولاً)) أن كل دينار (مال فلو كان عاماً لدخل كل دينار فيه ويجب أخذ الصدقة منه ولا يجب
أخذ الصدقة منه إجماعاً، ويجاب عنه بأنه خص بالإجماع) يعني أن مقتضى اللفظ ذلك، لكن الدليل الخارجي أوجب الخروج وهو الإجماع فصار العام مخصوصاً فيبقى حجة في الباقي) المذهب في العام المخصوص على أن ما ذكر إن تم اختص بهذه الآية الواردة في الزكاة ولا يطرد في سائر أمثاله، لأن المدعي عام (و) أورد (ثانياً) لو صح ما ذكرتم لما كان بين للرجال عندي درهم وبين لكل رجل عندي درهم ،فرق لأن كليهما للعموم وفرق بين الرجال عندي درهم وبين لكل رجل (بالاتفاق) فإن الأول يجب فيه درهم واحد يشترك فيه الكل، وفي الثاني لكل درهم تامّ فهذا معارضة أو نقض فإن قلت الكلام كان في الجمع المضاف، وليس بل محلى باللام قلت حكم الجمع المضاف والمحلى واحد فتدبر (ويجاب بأن البراءة الأصلية قرينة) صارفة عن حمله على كل على حمل الجمع على المجموع) يعني مقتضى اللفظ ههنا أيضاً كان وجوب درهم لكل واحد واحد لكنه عدل بصارف البراءة بخلاف ما نحن فيه، إذ لا صارف فيه فتبقى الآية على الظاهر، وفيه أن الإقرار ظاهره ثبوت الدين لكل واحد وثبوته للكل خلاف الظاهر، فلا مساواة حتى يرجح البراءة أحدهما، ولا تصلح صارفة عن الظاهر إلى خلافه، وإلا لم يكن إقرار ما ملزماً لصرف البراءة من الدين إلى الوديعة أو إلى الوعد وغير ذلك من المجازات، اللهم إلا أن يثبت عرف خاص في هذا اللفظ فافهم (قيل) تلك البراءة مشتركة بين الإقرار (والآية فإن الأصل براءة الذمة عن وجوب الزكاة، كما أن الأصل البراءة عن وجوب الدين، فلا تصلح فارقة بين الآية والإقرار (أقول احتياط الامتثال في الآية فإنها موجبة لوجوب الزكاة وفي الإخراج عن كل نوع امتثال بيقين بخلاف الإخراج عن نوع واحد ( أجدر وأحرى، وأجيب بأن الإقرار قد يكون كاذباً فلا وجوب فيه في نفس الأمر أصلاً عند العليم الخبير، فلا وجه للاحتياط، وفيه أنه ينبغي على هذا أن يفرق بين الإقرار الصادق والكاذب وما لم يظهر كذبه أن يحكم بما هو مجموع فلا يلزم من الآية إلا الوجود مجموع الأموال التي لكل واحد واحد لا الوجوب من كل نوع، وحاصل هذا يرجع إلى المنع بأنه أن أريد بعموم الجمع العموم المجموعي فمسلم لكن لا ينفعكم، وإن أريد أن كعموم كل لكل واحد واحد أو لكل فلم يقع ممن يعتد بهم والحق هو (الثاني) كما عرفت ( ورابعاً أقول في الإيراد في إضافة الجمع إلى الجمع (إضافة الجمع إلى كل واحد واحد من آحاد الجمع الآخر (ممنوع، بل يجوز أن يعتبر أولا
Issue: And 'Take from their wealth a charity' [Quran 9:103] does not necessitate taking from every type, meaning that the construct state of a plural to a plural does not necessitate the generality of the individuals of the first with respect to each individual of the second. They discussed a specific instance of this, namely the verse 'Take from their wealth a charity'. (As for the Hanafis, because the pairing of plural with plural indicates the distribution of individuals over individuals.) So the meaning is: take from a wealthy person's wealth some wealth, and from another wealthy person another charity. This does not necessitate taking from all the wealth of a single individual, nor does it intend the comprehensive inclusion of all the wealth of each individual or its types. They adduced induction (istiqra') such as 'They rode their mounts' and 'They put their fingers in their ears,' for the meaning is that each one rode his own mount and each one put his finger in his ear. And other examples like 'Wash your faces' [Quran 5:6]. If you say: The distribution here is due to the impossibility of generality, for a man only rides one mount, does not put all his fingers in his ears, and only washes his face. So induction where there is a contextual clue diverting from generality is conceded but not beneficial, and absolutely it is denied. I say: Investigation of usages rules that what is immediately understood from pairing plural with plural is distribution without need for a contextual clue. (And it was objected by His saying 'And they carry their burdens on their backs' [Quran 6:31] for the meaning is that each one carries his burden on his back on the Day of Resurrection, and distribution is not intended. (I say: The occasional deviation in some instances due to a diverting factor does not harm induction because it is based on predominance.) And predominance in usage is for intending the distribution of individuals over individuals, so ponder. (As for Imam Zufar) and Sheikh Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Karkhi from us, and al-Amidi and those who follow them, because if one takes one charity from the total of their wealth, it is true that he took a charity from their wealth, so there is no taking from every type of every individual's wealth. (And it is responded by denying the implication) for it is a claim that is essentially the same as the desired conclusion; whoever does not concede it does not concede it. It was said: The generality of the plural is collective (majmu'i), so the meaning is from the total wealth of each individual, and this does not necessitate taking from every type. And there will come what is in it. And you may build this on that 'min' is partitive, so the meaning is from some of the wealth of each individual. This only holds if the discussion were specific to this verse, but it is general whether 'min' is the object or not, so ponder. And most of others (including) Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, hold that it necessitates taking charity from every type. And in some commentaries on al-Minhaj that he explicitly stated in a treatise of the Imam (because it is a plural in construct state to each individual of the plural, and it is for generality) so it encompasses every type of wealth of every individual owner. So the meaning is: take from every wealth for each of the owners. (And it is objected) first: that every dinar is wealth; if it were general, every dinar would be included and taking charity from it would be obligatory, but it is not obligatory to take charity from it by consensus. (And it is responded that it is specified by consensus) meaning that the literal meaning demands that, but external evidence necessitated exclusion, which is consensus, so the general term becomes specified and remains proof for the rest. The relied-upon position on the specified general term is that what was mentioned, if valid, is specific to this verse on zakat and does not apply to other similar cases, because the claim is general. (And) it is objected second: If your statement were correct, there would be no difference between 'I have a dirham for the men' and 'For every man I have a dirham', because both are for generality, but there is a difference between 'the men have a dirham' and 'for every man' by agreement, for the former requires one dirham shared by all, while the latter requires a full dirham for each. So this is a contradiction or refutation. If you say: The discussion was about the construct plural, and it is not defined with 'al', I say: The ruling of the construct plural and the defined one is the same, so ponder. (And it is responded that the original exemption (bara'a asliyya) is a contextual clue) diverting it from being interpreted as 'each' to interpreting the plural as collective, meaning the literal meaning here also required the obligation of a dirham for each individual, but it was altered by the diverting factor of exemption, unlike our case, where there is no such diverting factor, so the verse remains on its apparent meaning. And there is in this that the acknowledgment (iqrar) apparently establishes a debt for each one, and establishing it for all is contrary to the apparent, so there is no equality such that the exemption would outweigh one of them, nor is it suitable as a diverting factor from the apparent to its opposite; otherwise, an acknowledgment would not be binding to divert the exemption from debt to deposit or promise or other metaphors, unless a specific custom is established for this term, so understand. (It was said) That exemption is common between the acknowledgment and the verse, for the original is exemption of the liability from the obligation of zakat, just as the original is exemption from the obligation of debt, so it cannot distinguish between the verse and the acknowledgment. (I say: The precaution of compliance (ihtiyat al-imtithal) in the verse, for it necessitates the obligation of zakat, and in giving from every type there is certain compliance, unlike giving from a single type, counteracts the exemption from obligation, so the generality remains free from a diverting factor. (So ponder) For one may say: The obligation is common between the acknowledgment and the verse; rather, debt is a right of a slave, so obligation is stronger in it, and the sin of refusal is greater, so it is more deserving of precaution. And it is answered that the acknowledgment may be false, so there is no obligation in reality at all with Allah, so there is no justification for precaution. And there is in it that on this basis one should distinguish between truthful and false acknowledgment, and as long as its falsity has not appeared, one should rule with what includes precaution due to the possibility of obligation for all, so the liability remains occupied as when the acknowledger dies without clarification — so ponder. (And) it is objected thirdly that the generality of the plural (construct or defined) is not like the generality of 'every' (kull), for that (i.e., the generality of the said plural) is for the whole as a whole, so the verse only implies the existence of the total wealth belonging to each individual, not the obligation from every type. And the gist of this returns to denial: if you intend by the generality of the plural collective generality, it is conceded but does not benefit you; if you intend it like the generality of 'every,' it is denied, for its distinction from the latter is clear. (And it is responded that this is a false statement) unworthy of consideration, for common usage is judging each individual, and the majority also choose that. (Yes, there is a difference: is it for every group or every individual? As for the difference over whether it is for each individual or for all, such has not occurred among those worthy of consideration, and the truth is the second, as you have learned. (And fourthly, I say regarding the objection about the construct state of a plural to a plural: the construct of the plural to each individual of the other plural is denied; rather, it is permissible to consider first
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute (ikhtilaf) concerning the interpretation of the Quranic verse 'خذ من أموالهم صدقة' (Take from their wealth a charity). The central question is whether the phrase, which involves a construct state of a plural (أموالهم) to another plural (the implied owners), implies generality (عموم) over all types of wealth, or merely distribution (انقسام) meaning each individual's wealth is subject to a separate obligation. The author argues from the Hanafi perspective, supported by Abu Hanifa and his school, that the pairing of plural with plural (مقابلة الجمع بالجمع) typically indicates distribution of individuals over individuals, not comprehensive inclusion. This is based on inductive evidence (استقراء) from common Arabic usage, such as 'ركبوا دوابهم' (they rode their mounts) meaning each rode his own mount. The Hanafis conclude that the verse does not require taking charity from every type of wealth (e.g., gold, silver, livestock) of each individual; rather, it suffices to take from some types. Imam Zufar and others argue that taking a single charity from the total wealth satisfies the wording. Imam al-Shafi'i, however, holds that the plural in construct is general and necessitates taking from every type. The text then presents three objections to the Shafi'i view: (1) if it were general, it would include every dinar, but consensus excludes that; (2) the difference between 'الرجال عندي درهم' and 'لكل رجل عندي درهم' shows the construct plural implies a single collective sum; (3) the generality of the plural is collective, not distributive. Each objection is met with a Hanafi response, emphasizing that the apparent meaning is distributive unless a contextual clue diverts it. The discussion also involves the concept of 'براءة أصلية' (original exemption) as a potential clue, but the author argues that the precaution of compliance in the religious obligation of zakat overrides the presumptive exemption, preserving the generality. The passage concludes with a fourth objection about the validity of the construct state, which is dismissed as a minority view. The author consistently upholds the Hanafi position that the default is distribution, not generality.

Key Terms
الجمع المضاف (al-jam' al-mudaf): A plural noun in a construct state to another noun (e.g., أموالهم).
انقسام الآحاد على الآحاد (inqisam al-ahad 'ala al-ahad): The distribution of individuals of the first plural over individuals of the second plural (e.g., each person's wealth individually).
الاستقراء (al-istiqra'): Inductive examination of common Arabic usage to determine the default meaning.
العموم المجموعي (al-umum al-majmu'i): Generality of the whole (collective), meaning the group as a totality rather than each individual item.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi view in the passage, what does the Quranic phrase 'خذ من أموالهم صدقة' primarily indicate?
A) It requires taking charity from every type of wealth of each individual.
B) It implies distribution: taking from some types of wealth of each individual.
C) It means taking a single charity from the entire community's wealth.
D) It is ambiguous and cannot be interpreted without additional context.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default for a plural in construct to a plural is distribution (انقسام), not generality, based on inductive evidence from Arabic usage.

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
intermediate
9 min
The Generality of the Construct Plural in Usul al-Fiqh
Analyzing whether الجمع المضاف indicates per-individual generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that the construct plural indicates per-individual generality and identify contextual indicators that divert to collective generality.

Core Idea
The construct plural indicates per-individual generality unless a contextual indicator diverts it.

In Hanafi usul, the scope of the construct plural (الجمع المضاف) is a key issue. Does 'their properties' (أموالهم) obligate zakat on each property individually or on the total? This lesson dissects the arguments, showing how the Hanafi school defends per-individual generality as the linguistic default, except when a contextual indicator like the original presumption of innocence redirects meaning.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi rule on the construct plural
Identify the claim: every construct plural indicates generality for each individual, like 'each' (كل), unless there is a contextual indicator (قرينة) to the contrary.
This is the foundational principle being defended.
2
Examine the objection from the 'dirham' example
The objection uses 'for the men I have a dirham' vs 'for every man I have a dirham' to argue the construct plural gives collective, not individual, meaning. Understand why the Hanafi response invokes the original presumption of innocence as a contextual indicator that diverts the meaning.
The key is the presence or absence of a contextual indicator.
3
Evaluate the third objection: generality of the plural is collective
Some argue the construct plural's generality is like collective generality (عموم مجموعي), not per-individual. Note how the Hanafi reply rejects this as 'worthless' (مزيف), citing common usage and majority opinion.
The text calls the majority view (مختار الجمهور) as supporting per-individual.
4
Analyze the fourth objection: adding plural to plural
When the construct is a plural added to a plural (e.g., أموالهم), the objector claims the addition is of individuals to individuals (distribution), not plural to plural as such. The Hanafi response distinguishes two types, showing that the intended generality is for individuals defined by ownership, not specific types.
Induction (استقراء) shows the construct plural only generalizes individuals of the first type (by adding property to owner), not specific types like camels, gold, etc.

The third article on linguistic principles: That taking charity from it is by consensus, and it is answered that it is specified by consensus, meaning that the apparent meaning of the wording is that, but external evidence necessitated departure, which is consensus, so the general becomes specified and remains proof for the rest. The doctrine regarding the specified general is that what was mentioned, if valid, is specific to this verse about zakat and does not apply to its other examples, because the claim is general. And a second objection: If what you mentioned were correct, there would be no difference between 'for the men I have a dirham' and 'for every man I have a dirham', because both indicate generality, but there is a difference by agreement: in the first, one dirham is owed shared by all, and in the second, a complete dirham for each. This is a contradiction or refutation. If you say the discussion was about the construct plural, not the one with lam, I say the ruling of the construct plural and the one with lam is the same, so reflect. And it is answered that the original presumption of innocence is a contextual indicator diverting from carrying it on each to carrying the plural on the collective. That is, the apparent meaning of the wording here also was an obligation of a dirham for each one, but it was diverted by the indicator of innocence, unlike our case, where there is no such indicator, so the verse remains on the apparent. And it is said that this presumption is shared between confession and the verse, for the original is innocence of liability for zakat as it is for debt, so it does not serve as a differentiator. I say precaution in compliance in the verse is that it imposes obligation of zakat, and in paying for each type there is certain compliance unlike paying for one type, so it counteracts the presumption, leaving generality without an indicator. Reflect, for one may say that obligation is shared between confession and the verse, rather the debt is a right of the servant so obligation is stronger, and sin in refusal is greater, so it is more deserving of precaution. And it is answered that confession may be false, so there is no obligation in reality at all before the All-Knowing, so no basis for precaution. It is argued that on this basis one should distinguish between true and false confession, and as long as falsehood is not apparent, one should rule with precaution due to possibility of obligation for all, so the liability remains. Then a third objection: The generality of the plural (construct or with lam) is not like the generality of 'each', for that generality is for the collective as a whole, so from the verse only the existence of the total of properties of each one is implied, not obligation from each type. This returns to denial: if by generality of the plural you mean collective generality, it is accepted but does not benefit you; if you mean like generality of 'each', it is denied. This is refuted as a worthless statement, for common usage and the choice of the majority is that it applies to each individual. There is disagreement whether it is for each group or each individual, but disagreement about whether it is for each one or not does not occur among reliable scholars, and the truth is the second. A fourth objection: In adding a plural to a plural, the addition of the plural to each one of the individuals of the other plural is denied; rather it is permissible to first consider addition of individuals to individuals, but because naming individuals is difficult or leads to length, it is expressed by adding the plural to the plural. Then consider adding the plural to the plural to achieve the purpose, so the individuals of the plural are the divided individuals, reflect. If you say: the sum of the argument is that 'their properties' is a construct plural and every construct plural indicates generality; the first is necessary and the second is accepted, so this speech has no direction. I say: Adding a plural to a plural is two types: one where the purpose is adding individuals to individuals, expressed by adding plural to plural for brevity, giving distribution; the other where the purpose is adding the plural as such, which gives generality of the ruling to each one. If they mean the second, the minor premise is denied; if the first, it does not benefit the arguer, and he avoided addressing this part because it is far from being intended. You may formulate the answer thus: The individuals of absolute property are two types: first, individuals obtained by adding property to owner, like Zayd's property and Bakr's property; second, specific properties of types like camels, cattle, etc., and specific items. The construct plural added to a plural only generalizes individuals of the first type, not the second, as shown by induction. If they mean by the major premise that every construct plural indicates generality including the first type, it is accepted but useless; if they mean generality for individuals of the second type, it is denied, rather it is the beginning of the issue, so reflect.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage is a detailed textual analysis of the scope of generality in the construct plural (الجمع المضاف) in Usul al-Fiqh. The author examines whether phrases like 'their properties' (أموالهم) indicate obligation for each individual property or only for the collective. The discussion arises in the context of interpreting a verse about zakat. Various objections are raised and answered: (1) Is the phrase exclusive to the zakat verse? (2) The difference between 'for the men I have a dirham' (collective) and 'for every man I have a dirham' (individual) shows that the construct plural implies collective, not individual. The answer invokes the original presumption of innocence as a contextual indicator that diverts the meaning from individual to collective in that example, but no such indicator exists in the zakat verse, so it remains on apparent individual generality. (3) The generality of the construct plural is like collective generality, not per-individual. This is refuted by common usage and majority opinion, which holds it is per-individual. (4) When adding a plural to a plural, the correct interpretation is to consider it as adding individuals to individuals (distribution) for brevity, not as adding plural to plural as such. This is supported by induction: the only individuals included are those defined by addition to owners, not specific types. The author concludes that the correct position is that the construct plural gives per-individual generality, and the opposing views are either misdirected or based on misunderstanding of the grammatical construction. The discussion reflects a classic Hanafi usul approach of rigorous linguistic analysis.

Key Terms
الجمع المضاف (al-jam‘ al-mudāf): A plural noun in the construct state (idafah), e.g., أموالهم (their properties).
العموم المجموعي (al-‘umūm al-majmū‘ī): Generality that applies to the group as a whole, not to each individual separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It indicates zakat on each individual property type.
B) It indicates zakat on the collective total of properties.
C) It is ambiguous and requires further context.
D) It indicates zakat only on properties exceeding nisab.

Key Takeaway
The construct plural (الجمع المضاف) indicates per-individual generality unless a contextual indicator diverts it to collective meaning.

العموم في معرض المدح والذم: هل يبقى عاماً؟
Dispute on 'amm in praise/blame contexts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that general expressions in praise or blame retain their generality and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the zakat-on-jewelry dispute.

Core Idea
A general remains general even in praise or blame — context does not restrict.

When the Quran praises the righteous or condemns the wicked, does it include every individual? This question might seem abstract, but it has a concrete impact: does the verse threatening hoarders of gold and silver apply to women's jewelry? The Hanafi school says yes — the general term is binding unless a specific exception is proven. This lesson unpacks the proof and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul issue
Recognize that the dispute is about 'amm (general) in a madh/dhamm context: does it still include all individuals?
The verse on hoarding (al-Tawba 34-35) is the test case.
2
State the two positions
Hanafis (and Malikis, Hanbalis): the general applies fully. Shafi'is: context of blame limits applicability.
The text calls the Hanafi view 'al-akthar' (majority).
3
Analyze the Shafi'i argument
They argue the hoarding verse is in dhamm context, so it may not include jewelry; thus no obligation of zakat on jewelry from this verse.
This is a negative argument: 'no generality because context'.
4
Examine the Hanafi response and dalil
Hanafis cite multiple hadith: 'Any property on which zakat is paid is not hoarded treasure.' This explicitly links zakat payment to the removal of the hoarding label, showing the general verse includes jewelry if zakat is paid.
The hadith is ahad, but its dalala is clear. The Hanafis use it as a bayan (clarification) that the general remains.
5
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: Hanafis obligate zakat on women's jewelry (if it reaches nisab); Shafi'is do not derive obligation from this verse (they may base it on other evidence).
This shows how a usul dispute changes a fiqh ruling.

Issue (12): It is narrated that the general may include praise and blame, such as 'Indeed the righteous will be in pleasure and indeed the wicked will be in blaze' (al-Infitar 13-14). So this general—does it apply to all its individuals or not? The majority of Hanafis, Malikis, and Hanbalis said yes, it applies. Contrary to al-Shafi‘i, may Allah have mercy on him, for it does not apply according to him, so much so that some Shafi‘is prevented arguing by the verse of Allah: 'And those who hoard gold and silver and spend them not in the way of Allah, give them tidings of a painful punishment on the Day when that will be heated in the fire of Hell and branded on their foreheads and their sides...' The evidence for the argument is that al-Bayhaqi reported from Umm Salama (may Allah be pleased with her) that she said: O Messenger of Allah, I have ornaments of gold or silver; is it hoarded treasure? He said: Everything for which zakat is paid is not hoarded treasure. And similar from 'Umar (may Allah be pleased with him): What is paid zakat on is not hoarded treasure, in the narration of Ibn Abi Shayba. And from Jabir as a stop report in Ibn Abi Shayba and as a raised report in Ibn ‘Adi: Any wealth for which zakat is paid is not hoarded treasure. And from Ibn ‘Umar in the narration of Malik and Ibn Abi Shayba as a stop report, and in Ibn Mardawayh as a raised report: What is paid zakat on is not hoarded treasure even if under layers of earth, and what is not paid zakat on is hoarded treasure even if apparent. And from Ibn ‘Abbas as a stop report: What is paid zakat on is not hoarded treasure in the narration of Ibn Abi Shayba. And from ‘Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) he said: O Prophet of Allah, it has become too hard for me on seven...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This lesson addresses a specific usul dispute: When Allah's speech contains a general term in a context of praise or blame, does that general still include all its individuals? The majority of Hanafis, Malikis, and Hanbalis say yes—the general remains general regardless of context. Imam al-Shafi‘i holds that such general terms are not general; the context of praise or blame restricts the applicability. The practical impact (thamar al-khilaf) is shown in the ruling on zakat on jewelry (حلي). Some Shafi‘is argue that the verse condemning hoarders (التوبة 34-35) mentions gold and silver in a blame context, so it does not necessarily include jewelry, and thus zakat is not obligatory on jewelry used as adornment. Hanafis, however, maintain that the general is unrestricted; they support this with multiple hadith reports (from Umm Salama, ‘Umar, Jabir, Ibn ‘Umar, Ibn ‘Abbas) showing that any wealth on which zakat is paid is not 'hoarded treasure' (كنز), implying that jewelry is subject to zakat if it reaches the nisab. The text is cut off but shows the beginning of a hadith from ‘Umar asking the Prophet about difficulty on seven things. Overall, the passage illustrates how usul disputes about the scope of 'amm affect fiqh rulings.

Key Terms
العام في معرض المدح والذم (al-‘āmm fī ma‘riḍ al-madḥ wa al-dhamm): A general expression that appears in a Quranic or textual context praising or blaming a group.
تخصيص (takhṣīṣ): Specification or restriction of a general term by a specific textual evidence.
ثمرة الخلاف (thamarah al-khilāf): The practical consequence of a scholarly disagreement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It retains generality and applies to all individuals mentioned.
C) It is considered ambiguous (mujmal) and requires external evidence to clarify.
D) It depends on the strength of the blame.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, context of praise or blame does not restrict the generality of an 'amm expression; only a specific textual exception can do so.

Two Types of Idafah al-Jam' ila al-Jam' and Their Effect on 'Umum
Distinguishing two types of plural-to-plural construct for 'umum

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the distributive and collective types of idafah al-jam' ila al-jam' and apply the Hanafi rule that only the distributive type indicates generality over individuals of possession, not over specific instances.

Core Idea
Not every plural-in-construct gives generality; only the distributive type covers individuals of possession, not particular instances.

In Hanafi usul al-fiqh, understanding how a phrase like 'amwāluhum' conveys generality is crucial for correct istinbat. A common mistake is to assume that every plural noun in construct automatically includes all possible individuals of the second noun. This lesson unpacks a subtle distinction between two types of idafah al-jam' ila al-jam'—distributive (tawzī') and collective (jami')—and shows how only the former yields generality over the singulars that arise from possession, not over specific types or instances. Mastering this distinction will sharpen your ability to interpret Quranic and hadith texts accurately within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of idafah al-jam' ila al-jam'
The commentator explains that when a plural is added to another plural, the intended meaning can be either: (1) the addition of singulars to singulars (distributive), or (2) the addition of the plural itself (collective). This distinction is not immediately apparent from the grammar but is inferred from context and usage.
Distributive: e.g., 'amwāluhum' meaning 'the wealth of each one'. Collective: e.g., 'amwāluhum' meaning 'their collective wealth'.
2
Understand the Hanafi critique of the universal claim
The opponent argues that 'every plural in construct indicates generality'. The Hanafi response shows that this is false because the collective type only indicates generality over the group as a whole, not over every individual of the second noun's instances. The claim is only true for the distributive type.
The commentator says: 'If he intended the second (collective), then the minor premise is prevented.'
3
Distinguish between two types of singulars
The singulars of the second noun (e.g., 'wealth') are divided into: (a) singulars obtained by adding wealth to an owner (like Zayd's wealth), and (b) specific types or instances (like gold, silver, this gold coin). The distributive type only generalizes the first kind, not the second.
This is based on induction (istiqra').
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take the Quranic phrase 'amwāluhum' in verse 2:188 ('And do not consume their wealth...'). According to Hanafi usul, does this forbid consuming every specific type of wealth (gold, silver, etc.) of theirs? No—it only forbids consuming the wealth that belongs to each individual owner. The construct is distributive here, so it applies to each owner's wealth, not to every category of wealth.
A full analysis requires context (qarinah), but the principle is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إضافة الآحاد إلى الآحاد ويكون المقصود إفادة ذلك لكن لما كان تسمية الآحاد متعسرة أو مؤدية إلى التطويل عبر عنه بإضافة الجمع إلى الجمع كما قال (ثم ) اعتبر إضافة الجمع إلى الجمع) لأداء المقصود (فأفراد الجمع هي الآحاد المنقسمة فتدبر) فإن قلت: كان حاصل الاستدلال أن أموالهم جمع مضاف وكل جمع مضاف للعموم أما الأول فضروري والثاني مسلم فلا توجيه لهذا الكلام قلت : إضافة الجمع إلى الجمع نوعان نوع يكون المقصود فيه إضافة الآحاد إلى الآحاد ويعبر بإضافة الجمع إلى الجمع للاختصار فيفيد التوزيع ونوع يقصد فيه إضافة الجمع بالذات، وأوّلا فيفيد عموم الحكم لكل واحد واحد، فإن أراد الثاني فالصغرى ممنوعة كما يفصح عنه ،عبارته، وإن أراد الأول فلا يفيد المستدل وإنما أعرض عن التعرض لهذا الشق لكونه بعيداً من أن يريده محصل في إثبات العموم بالنسبة إلى كل واحد واحد، ولك أن تقرر الجواب هكذا أن آحاد مطلق المال نوعان: الأول : الآحاد التي تحصل بإضافة المال إلى المالك، فمال زيد فرد من المال وكذا مال بكر ،وهكذا الثاني: الأموال المعينة من الأنواع، كالإبل والبقر والغنم والذهب والفضة والأشخاص كهذا الذهب وهذه الفضة وغير ذلك، فالجمع المضاف إلى الجمع إنما يعم الإفراد من النوع الأول دون الثاني بدليل الاستقراء، فإن أرادوا بالكبرى القائلة إن كل جمع مضاف للعموم ما يشمل العموم للإفراد من النوع الأول فمسلم غير مفيد، وإن أرادوا العموم لجمع الإفراد من النوع الثاني فممنوع بل هو أول المسألة فتدبر .
The addition of singulars to singulars, and the intended meaning is to convey that. But since naming the singulars is difficult or leads to prolongation, it is expressed by adding a plural to a plural, as he said (then) he considered adding a plural to a plural to convey the intended meaning. (So the individuals of the plural are the divided singulars, so reflect.) If you say: the gist of the inference is that 'their wealth' is a plural in construct, and every plural in construct indicates generality; as for the first, it is necessary, and the second is conceded, so there is no direction for this speech. I say: adding a plural to a plural is of two types: a type in which the intended meaning is the addition of singulars to singulars, and it is expressed by adding a plural to a plural for brevity, so it indicates distribution; and a type in which the addition of the plural itself is intended, and it indicates the generality of the ruling for every single one. If he intended the second, then the minor premise is prevented, as his expression reveals. If he intended the first, then it does not benefit the arguer, and he avoided addressing this aspect because it is far from what a researcher would intend in proving generality with respect to every single one. You can phrase the response as follows: the singulars of absolute wealth are of two types: first: the singulars obtained by adding wealth to the owner, so Zayd's wealth is an individual of wealth, likewise 'Amr's wealth, etc. Second: the specific wealth from types, such as camels, cattle, sheep, gold, silver, and specific items like this gold and this silver, etc. The plural in construct to a plural only generalizes individuals of the first type, not the second, by the evidence of induction. If they intend by the major premise that every plural in construct indicates generality that includes generality for individuals of the first type, it is conceded but not useful. If they intend generality for the totality of individuals of the second type, it is prevented; rather, that is the very point of the issue, so ponder.
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage is a nuanced usul discussion about the nature of idafah al-jam' ila al-jam' (adding a plural to a plural) and its implication for 'umum (generality). The commentator explains that such constructs can be of two types: (1) a distributive type where the intended meaning is the addition of singulars to singulars (e.g., 'a'māluhum' meaning 'the deeds of each one'), which is used for brevity and indicates distribution among individuals; and (2) a collective type where the addition of the plural itself is intended (e.g., 'amwāluhum' meaning 'their collective wealth'), which indicates a general ruling covering all individuals as a class. The key point is that when a plural is added to a plural, it does not automatically give a general ruling over every single individual of the second type (specific instances like particular goods). The commentator refutes the argument that every plural in construct indicates absolute generality by distinguishing between the two types of singulars: those that arise from the relationship of possession (e.g., Zayd's wealth) and those that are particular instances of categories (e.g., this gold coin). The ruling here is that idafah al-jam' ila al-jam' only generalizes the first type, not the second, based on inductive evidence (istiqra'). This is a classic Hanafi usul position that nuances the understanding of 'umum in construct phrases.

Key Terms
إضافة الجمع إلى الجمع (idafat al-jam' ila al-jam'): A grammatical construct where a plural noun is added to another plural noun (e.g., 'amwāluhum').
عموم (‘umūm): Generality of a ruling covering all individuals of the class.
توزيع (tawzī‘): Distribution; the distributive type of idafah where the intended meaning is adding singulars to singulars.
جمع (jami‘): Collective; the collective type where the plural itself is intended as a whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It indicates generality over all types of wealth (gold, silver, etc.).
B) It indicates the wealth of each individual owner, but not necessarily every type of wealth.
C) It indicates the collective wealth of the group as a whole.
D) It indicates both collective wealth and distribution to individuals.

Key Takeaway
When a plural is added to a plural, do not assume absolute generality; first determine whether the construct is distributive or collective, and even if distributive, the generality only covers possession-based singulars, not specific types – a rule derived from linguistic induction.

Generality of 'Amm in Praise and Blame Contexts
Usul dispute with dalil naqli

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a general term ('amm) in a praise or blame context retains its generality, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith on hoarding.

Core Idea
Praise and blame do not strip a general term of its literal generality — the wording governs.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute: when Allah uses a general term in a verse of praise or blame, does it still apply to every individual of that class? The Hanafi school, along with Malikis and Hanbalis, holds that it does, while the Shafi'i school argues that hyperbole in such contexts may limit literal scope. The key test case is the verse on hoarding gold and silver (al-Tawbah 34-35). We will walk through the arguments and the supporting hadith evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Read the opening: 'Issue (12): The general term may include praise and blame... Does it apply to all its individuals?' Note the majority position (Hanafis, Malikis, Hanbalis) and the opposing view (Shafi'i).
The dispute is about the effect of context, not the definition of 'amm itself.
2
Locate the test case
The passage uses the verse on hoarding (al-Tawbah 34-35) as a concrete example. Some Shafi'is argue that because the verse is in a blame context, it may not include jewelry. Identify how the verse is used by each side.
The thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence) is whether zakah is obligatory on jewelry.
3
Follow the Hanafi dalil
The Hanafi position cites multiple hadith (Umm Salamah, 'Umar, Jabir, Ibn 'Umar, Abu Hurayrah) that define 'kanz' as wealth on which zakah has not been paid. This shows that the general term 'alladhina yaknizuna' includes all who avoid paying zakah, including jewelry owners.
The hadith serve as a mukhassis (specifier) that clarifies the intended meaning, but the term remains general in its scope.
4
Analyze the hyperbole rebuttal
The Shafi'i argument claims that praise and blame contexts involve hyperbole, so general terms may not be intended literally. The Hanafi response argues: (a) hyperbole can be achieved by mentioning all real instances, (b) Allah's speech is truthful and free from false exaggeration, and (c) the generalization itself is more emphatic in praise/blame.
The textual operation here is a critical refinement of the opponent's usul principle.
5
Synthesize the tarjih
The passage concludes (though cut off) that the multitude of hadith and the principle that 'amm remains general unless there is a valid mukhassis (which the blame context does not constitute) support the Hanafi position. The mufta bihi is the Hanafi view.
Authority weight: the hadith are from zahir al-riwayah sources, strengthening the Hanafi argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
