رواية الإمام أبي يوسف وقد مر والآخر ما أشار إليه بقوله : ( ويدل عليه لفظ وليدة فإنه فعيلة بمعنى فالوليدة بمعنى والدة وإذا أضيف إلى زمعة يتبادر منه إنها ولدت من مائه وهي أمته فلا يكون وطؤه إياها زنا وهذه التسمية كانت من قبل فلا بد أن يكون لولد آخر ولدته له، والظاهر أن يقر الرجل بولده فتثبت أمومية الوالدة بالولد السابق، فلا يرد أن الوالدة أعم من أن تكون بالزنا أو بكون الولد له أو لغيره، ثم على تقدير أن يكون له أعم من أن يكون مع الدعوى أو مع غيرها ثم تنزل وقال : (على أنه منع أنه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أثبت نسبه بقوله : هو لك بل معناه هو إرث لك فأنت مالكه ومعنى قوله : الولد للفراش» أن دعواكما باطلة، فإن الولد إنما يكون للفراش وليس ههنا فراش لأحد ، أما زمعة فلعدم الدعوى، وأما عتبة فلأنه عاهر فلا يرد إنه على هذا لا يرتبط قوله : «الولد للفراش الخ، مع قوله : «هو لك» ولا يطابق الجواب السؤال أصلاً، فإن الدعوى كانت في النسب دون الملك، ولا يرد أيضاً أن كون اللام للملك ممنوع بل لمطلق الاختصاص، وهو قد يكون بالنسب فإنه منع على منع خارج عن قانون التوجيه ويؤيده قوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (السودة بنت زمعة أم المؤمنين (وأما أنت فاحتجبي منه فإنه ليس لك بأخ فإن سلب الأخوة عنه لبنت زمعة وإثبات البنوة لزمعة ،متنافيان وأشار بصيغة المجهول إلى ضعفه فإنه ورد في «صحيح البخاري» أنه شركه في الميراث وفي بعض الروايات هو أخوك»، وأما الأمر بالحجاب فلعله أنه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم علم أنه ليس من ماء زمعة فأمرها احتياطاً كما جاء لما رأى من شبه عتبة أو يقال أمهات المؤمنين مخصوصات بالحجاب ممن لم يصدق القلب بالقرابة المحرمة، فإنهن لسن كأحد من النساء فتدبر (وقالوا) ثانياً (لو عم) الوارد على سبب خاص (لم يكن لنقل السبب إلينا (فائدة، وقد دونوا) الصحف فيه قلنا لا نسلم الملازمة، وإنما يلزم لو
By preventing invalidity after specifying it with a definitive [proof], and the consequent is false by consensus. (We said: the entailment is prevented because of certainty of its inclusion, for it is a response and correspondence is obligatory, so this correspondence is a contextual indication of inclusion. Specification by ijtihad is only permissible for individuals for which the contextual indication does not indicate inclusion definitively, so its relation is not like the relation of other individuals. (And it was also answered: the necessary [consequence] is not [accepted], nor is there consensus. (For) Imam Abu Hanifa extracted by ijtihad the child of the enslaved woman who is cohabited with for her master from the generality of his statement, peace and blessings be upon him: 'The child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone.' So Abu Hanifa did not establish his lineage, i.e., the lineage of the child of the enslaved woman who is cohabited with, from him, i.e., from the master, except by his claim, along with its occurrence in the case of the child of the slave-girl of Zam'a. And she was a concubine (mustafarrasha) as narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari and others that Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas and Abd ibn Zam'a disputed. Sa'd said: O Messenger of Allah, my brother Utbah ibn Abi Waqqas entrusted me that he is his son; look at his resemblance. And Abd ibn Zam'a said: This is my brother, O Messenger of Allah, born on my father's bed. In the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf in al-Amali, he said: O Messenger of Allah, he is my brother, born on my father's bed, my father acknowledged him. So the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, looked and saw a clear resemblance to Utbah. He said: 'He is yours, O Abd ibn Zam'a; the child belongs to the bed, and for the fornicator is the stone. And veil yourself from him, O Sawdah.' So Sawdah never saw him. And since excluding the particular [instance] is not rational, it was said in explanation of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him: Abu Hanifa did not exclude the specific cause which is the child of Zam'a, such that excluding the particular [instance] would be necessary, but rather he excluded others. In the marginal gloss by the commentator al-Shirazi from the Shafi'is, it was said against him: 'Tahqiq al-manat indicates that the cause is istifrash (taking as a concubine) and there is no entry for particularity,' i.e., the particularity of being the child of the slave-girl of Zam'a, as is clear. There is no doubt about the strength of this speech, except that a forced interpreter could say: The indication of tahqiq al-manat does not prove the exclusion of the cause, for the cause is the specific [instance]. As for the absolute, just as tahqiq al-manat necessitates its causality, likewise another ijtihad excludes it, and there is no corruption in that, and there is no exclusion of the cause at all. So reflect on it. The correct explanation of Abu Hanifa's statement is what was transmitted from Imam Ghazali: that the hadith did not reach him, and if it had reached him, he would not have excluded. (And by that), i.e., the non-reach of the hadith, Imam al-Haramayn stated explicitly in al-Burhan. I say, following Shaykh Ibn al-Humam: All that is due to their lack of knowledge of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. And the statement that the hadith did not reach him is incorrect, for it is mentioned in his Musnad. (For the enslaved woman, as long as she does not become an umm walad, is not a bed (firash) in his view, and exclusion depends on inclusion). So there is no exclusion for the enslaved woman whose child is not claimed, even if she is cohabited with. The author of Matali' al-Asrar al-Ilahiyya said: 'Al-firash is a metaphor for a man's association with his wife, like association with his bed. So the enslaved woman who is touched becomes a firash by touch. How? It is understood customarily from the word firash: the claim (al-da'wa). Yes, the mentioned association is a subtle matter requiring evidence indicating it, which is the claim and the absence of denial with the appearance of connection and extension of union. If the claim were a condition, then children born from a master who admits intercourse but does not claim them would all be slaves, and one would say 'this slave.' This is a strong statement, except that it is impossible that by firash is meant the cohabited woman, as it is close to the literal meaning, for it would include fornication as well, and exclude the married woman who is not cohabited. So firash must be an expression for lawful intercourse, which, despite being polysemous, includes the enslaved woman who is not cohabited. So another interpretation is necessary for what it has been applied to: that she is placed for seeking offspring, and this is 'fa'ila' (effective agent) by valid marriage and the master's acknowledgment of the child or pregnancy, as mentioned in the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf. Or else it is not understood customarily. Even if it is granted, it is not harmful, because this is a legal meaning known by contextual indications. As for her being cohabited or married as per al-Shafi'i, it is not understood customarily nor indicated legally, unlike acknowledgment, for it is indicated in the narration of Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, for he deduced brotherhood from being born on the bed of the acknowledger, that what is in her womb is his child. This indicates that they were aware of the condition of acknowledgment. Then, acknowledgment is obligatory upon the master when he thinks it is from his fluid. If he does not acknowledge, it is known that it is not from his fluid, so it does not necessitate that children born from the master are slaves in the absence of acknowledgment, for it is not free from acknowledgment. Even if he does not acknowledge while knowing it, he has abandoned the obligatory, and then they are deemed slaves, and there is no remoteness in that, because abandoning the obligatory is suitable for prescribing this punishment. Also, out of this fear, he acknowledges and fulfills the obligatory, for man by nature recoils from enslaving what was created from his fluid. So understand and reflect. As for what they said about excluding the child of the slave-girl of Zam'a, he replied by saying: (As for the slave-girl of Zam'a, she was an umm walad for him, as it is said; meaning we do not concede that the slave-girl of Zam'a's child was not claimed. Whoever claims must provide evidence. This suffices us. But since the claim was established, the discussion is in the case of the claim. On this, it is objected that it is a claim without evidence. Then the evidence to prove it is two matters: one is what is in the narration of Imam Abu Yusuf, which has passed; and the other is what he indicated by his statement: (And the word 'walida' indicates that it is fa'ila in the sense of walida (one who gives birth). When added to Zam'a, it is understood that she gave birth from his fluid and she is his slave-girl, so his intercourse with her is not fornication. This naming was from before, so there must have been another child she bore for him. The apparent is that the man acknowledges his child, so the motherhood of the mother is established by the previous child. So it is not objected that 'walida' is more general: whether by fornication or by the child being his or another's. Then, assuming it is his, it is more general: whether with claim or without. Then he descended and said: (On the basis that he denied that the Prophet, peace be upon him, established his lineage by his statement 'He is yours'; rather its meaning is 'He is an inheritance for you, so you are his owner.' And the meaning of his statement 'The child belongs to the bed' is that both your claims are invalid, for the child belongs only to the bed, and there is no bed here for anyone. As for Zam'a, due to lack of claim; as for Utbah, because he is a fornicator. So it is not objected that on this, his statement 'The child belongs to the bed' does not connect with his statement 'He is yours,' nor does the answer match the question at all, for the claim was about lineage, not ownership. Nor is it objected that the lam (in 'laka') is for ownership is denied but rather for absolute attribution, and it may be by lineage. For it is a denial based on a denial outside the principles of interpretation. Supporting it is his statement, peace be upon him: 'Sawdah bint Zam'a is the Mother of the Believers. As for you, veil yourself from him, for he is not your brother.' For negating brotherhood from him for the daughter of Zam'a while establishing daughterhood for Zam'a is contradictory. And he indicated by the passive voice its weakness, for it is reported in Sahih al-Bukhari that he included him in inheritance, and in some narrations: 'He is your brother.' As for the command to veil, perhaps the Prophet, peace be upon him, knew that he was not from the fluid of Zam'a, so he ordered her as a precaution, as it came when he saw the resemblance to Utbah. Or it is said: The Mothers of the Believers are specifically veiled from those whose heart does not accept the prohibited relationship, for they are not like other women. So ponder. (And they said) secondly: (If it were general) the occurrence on a specific cause (would not have served the purpose of transmitting the cause to us, and they recorded) the pages about it. We said: We do not concede the entailment. It would only be necessary if
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about the validity of Abu Hanifa's ruling that the child of a slave-girl (who is not an umm walad) is not attributed to the master unless the master acknowledges him, despite the general hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' (the child belongs to the bed). The author defends Abu Hanifa against the charge that he arbitrarily excluded the hadith. The passage systematically addresses objections: (1) That Abu Hanifa's ijtihad amounts to takhsis of a qat'i text by a zanni proof—the author argues that the hadith is not qat'i in its application to slave-girls because the word 'firash' is interpreted in a specific legal sense requiring acknowledgment. (2) That Abu Hanifa had no basis to exclude the specific case of Zam'a—the author shows that Zam'a's slave-girl was actually a well-known umm walad (mother of a child) based on the narration, and that 'walida' itself implies prior motherhood. (3) That the hadith did not reach Abu Hanifa—the author rejects this, noting it is in his Musnad. The author explains the Hanafi interpretation of 'firash' as lawful intercourse with the condition of acknowledgment (iqrar), not mere cohabitation. The commentary presents the Shafi'i objection based on tahqiq al-manat (verification of the ratio legis) and refutes it by arguing that the particular cause (the specific case of Zam'a) was not excluded but rather other cases were differentiated. Finally, the passage discusses the meaning of the Prophet's statement 'He is yours' in the Zam'a incident, arguing it refers to ownership, not lineage, reconciling the hadith's general rule with the specific case. This is a classic Hanafi usul defense against Shafi'i critique.

Key Terms
الفراش (al-firash): In this text, the author defines firash as a metaphor for lawful marital association with the condition that the husband/master acknowledges the child or the wife's status as a proper bed.
وليدة (walida): A female slave who has given birth (the active participle). In the context of Zam'a, it implies she had previously borne a child from Zam'a, making her an umm walad.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): The master's voluntary acknowledgment that the child is his, which in Hanafi fiqh is essential for establishing lineage from a slave-girl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position defended in this passage, what is the ruling on the child of a slave-girl who is cohabited by her master but not acknowledged by him?
A) The child is free and attributed to the master.
B) The child is a slave and not attributed to the master.
C) The child is free but not attributed to the master.
D) The child is attributed only if the slave-girl is an umm walad.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school restricts the general hadith 'al-walad lil-firash' based on the legal definition of 'firash' which requires the master's acknowledgment (iqrar).

Amm and Khass — General and Specific
advanced
12 min
Refutation of Objections to Generalization of Texts on Specific Causes
Analyzing and responding to five objections against generalizing texts with specific causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the five objections raised against generalizing texts revealed on specific causes and explain the Hanafi response to each using the usul principles of 'amm and haqiqah.

Core Idea
A general text on a specific cause remains general unless evidence restricts it; the cause prevents ijtihad, not specification.

This passage is a usul dispute defending the Hanafi position that texts revealed on specific causes ('amm al-wārid 'alā sabab khāṣṣ) are general in application, contrary to opponents who argue they are restricted. The author presents five objections against generality and systematically refutes each, clarifying key concepts like literal meaning (haqiqah), metaphorical usage (majaz), and specificity (nususiyya). Understanding this dispute is essential for deriving rulings from revelation without unwarranted restrictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
State the principle: a general text revealed on a specific cause retains generality unless evidence restricts it. Note the emphasis on literal meaning (haqiqah) and the role of the cause in preventing ijtihad, not specifying the ruling.
This is the foundational step – ensure clarity on the Hanafi view before examining objections.
2
Map the five objections
List each objection from the opponents: (1) no benefit in transmitting cause if general; (2) linguistic custom (oath example) shows non-generality; (3) answer would not match question; (4) word is text only for cause; (5) generalization leads to metaphor. Note the reasoning behind each.
Each objection targets a different aspect: utility, custom, consistency, specificity, and linguistic category.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response to each objection
For each objection, extract the Hanafi rebuttal: (1) benefit is to prevent ijtihad and provide context; (2) custom in oath example is specific and not universal; (3) answer provides additional benefit beyond matching; (4) word is not text for cause only, generality is preserved; (5) no metaphor because the word is used in its literal sense for all individuals.
Focus on how each response reinforces the principle of 'amm and the definition of haqiqah/majaz.
4
Evaluate the final point on nususiyya and haqiqah
Understand the deeper usul point: specificity (nususiyya) does not entail metaphor (majaz). The word is used for all individuals, so it remains haqiqah. The author argues that if nususiyya were from the word itself, it would require metaphorical interpretation, but here it is from external evidence (qarīna).
This step distinguishes Hanafi usul from others: nususiyya is determined externally, not internally.
5
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Consider a concrete fiqh example: the verse 'Flog the fornicator' revealed on a specific case. Hanafis apply it to all fornicators; opponents restrict it to that case unless other evidence extends it. This shows the real-world impact of the principle.
Connect the theoretical dispute to actual ruling derivation.

المقالة الثالثة في المبادىء اللغوية فاعلة) بالنكاح الصحيح وإقرار السيد بالولد أو الحمل كما ورد في رواية الإمام أبي يوسف، وإما عدم انفهامه عرفاً، فلو سلم فليس ضاراً، لأن هذا معنى شرعي عرف بالقرائن، بقوله : ( ويدل عليه لفظ وليدة فإنه فعيلة بمعنى فالوليدة بمعنى والدة وإذا أضيف إلى زمعة يتبادر منه إنها ولدت من مائه وهي أمته فلا يكون وطؤه إياها زنا وهذه التسمية كانت من قبل فلا بد أن يكون لولد آخر ولدته له، والظاهر أن يقر الرجل بولده فتثبت أمومية الوالدة بالولد السابق، فلا يرد أن الوالدة أعم من أن تكون بالزنا أو بكون الولد له أو لغيره، ثم على تقدير أن يكون له أعم من أن يكون مع الدعوى أو مع غيرها ثم تنزل وقال : (على أنه منع أنه صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم أثبت نسبه بقوله : هو لك بل معناه هو إرث لك فأنت مالكه ومعنى قوله : الولد للفراش» أن دعواكما باطلة، فإن الولد إنما يكون للفراش وليس ههنا فراش لأحد ، أما زمعة فلعدم الدعوى، وأما عتبة فلأنه عاهر فلا يرد إنه على هذا لا يرتبط قوله : «الولد للفراش الخ، مع قوله : «هو لك» ولا يطابق الجواب السؤال أصلاً، فإن الدعوى كانت في النسب دون الملك، ولا يرد أيضاً أن كون اللام للملك ممنوع بل لمطلق الاختصاص، وهو قد يكون بالنسب فإنه منع على منع خارج عن قانون التوجيه ويؤيده قوله عليه وآله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (السودة بنت زمعة أم المؤمنين (وأما أنت الملازمة) ويقول يعم أيضاً (و) قالوا (رابعاً على تقدير العموم) أي عموم الوارد على سبب خاص لم يكن الجواب مطابقاً) للسؤال، والمطابقة واجبة (قلنا) ليس المطابقة إلا كون الجواب بحيث يفهم منه حال المسؤول ، وهو في الكل) فلا مجاز كذا في «التحرير») وعبارته لا مجاز أصلاً لأنه بالاستعمال في المعنى لا بكيفية الدلالة، وقد استعمل في الكل فهو حقيقة والظاهر أنه معارضة على كونه مجازاً فلا يرد عليه شيء، والمصنف حمل على المنع بعد تسليم النصوصية، فأورد عليه بإثبات المقدمة الممنوعة، وقال: (أقول : تساوي النسبة إلى الجميع أي تساوي نسبة اللفظ إلى جميع الأفراد مع قطع النظر عن الخارج لازم الحقيقة بالضرورة فإذا انتفى التساوي (انتفت الحقيقة فلا مجال لمنع المجازية بعد تسليم النصوصية في البعض، بل لا يكون نصوصية من اللفظ أصلاً لا في الكل ولا في البعض، فإن قيل : لعله سلم النصوصية من الخارج ، يقال : آل إلى الجواب الأول حينئذ فتدبر
The third article on linguistic principles. ... 'fāʿilah' by valid marriage and the master's acknowledgment of the child or pregnancy as narrated from Imam Abu Yusuf. ... They said, secondly: If it were general, the text revealed on a specific cause, then there would be no benefit in transmitting the cause to us, and they have recorded it in the books. We say: We do not accept the entailment. It would only entail if the benefit were confined to specifying the ruling, but it is not so; rather its benefit is to prevent its specification by ijtihad and to know that it is a text on that matter. ... They said, thirdly: If someone says 'I will not eat lunch' in response to someone who said 'Come eat lunch with me', it is not general, so he is not oath-breaking unless he eats at his place. We say: The requirement of the literal meaning is generality, but it is diverted to the specific eating due to a specific custom. ...
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute defending the Hanafi position that a text revealed on a specific cause is general in its ruling unless there is evidence to restrict it. The text begins with a specific fiqh example regarding the status of a slave woman (umm walad) and the case of the daughter of Zama'a, illustrating how the word 'walida' implies she is a mother, and the master's acknowledgment is required. Then five objections from opponents are presented and rebutted: (1) If general, no benefit in transmitting the cause; (2) analogy with the oath 'I will not eat lunch' shows non-generality; (3) inconsistency between question and answer; (4) the word is only text for the cause; (5) the word becomes metaphorical if generalized. The Hanafi responses argue that the general meaning remains unless a specific indicator (like custom or context) restricts it, and that using the word for all individuals is still a literal usage if the word is used with the intention of generality. The discussion ends with a subtle point about the nature of linguistic reality.

Key Terms
العموم الوارد على سبب خاص (al-ʿumūm al-wārid ʿalā sabab khāṣṣ): A general expression revealed with a specific cause or context, which in Hanafi usul remains general in application unless there is evidence to the contrary.
المجاز (al-majāz): Metaphorical usage where a word is used for a meaning other than its original, requiring a contextual indicator (qarīna).
النصوصية (al-nuṣūṣiyya): The quality of being a clear text (naṣṣ) in a specific meaning, indicating unequivocal indication.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this passage, what is the primary benefit of transmitting the specific cause of a general text?
A) To specify the ruling to that cause
B) To prevent the ruling from being specified by ijtihad
C) To indicate that the word is metaphorical
D) To show that the text is no longer applicable

Key Takeaway
A general text revealed on a specific cause remains general in Hanafi usul unless evidence restricts it; the cause is transmitted to prevent ijtihad, not to limit the ruling.

The General Text on a Specific Cause
Debate on the scope of general texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the key arguments in the debate over whether a general text revealed on a specific cause remains general, and apply the Hanafi position to a similar case.

Core Idea
A general text revealed for a specific cause remains general unless there is clear evidence to restrict it.

One of the most important debates in usul al-fiqh is whether the specific cause (sabab) for which a general text was revealed restricts its legal application. The Hanafi school maintains that the wording remains general, while opponents argue it becomes specific to that cause. This lesson walks through the objections and responses found in 'Fawatih al-Rahamut'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The debate is whether a general text ('amm) revealed on a specific cause (sabab khas) applies only to that cause or remains general. The opponents raise five objections.
2
Examine the opponents' objections
Read the source carefully: they argue that the benefit is only to specify the ruling, that custom shows specificity, that correspondence requires matching the question, and that the text is explicit only in the cause, making generalization metaphorical.
Pay attention to the lunch example and the correspondence argument.
3
Analyze the Hanafi responses (qulna)
Note how the Hanafis refute each objection: (1) the real benefit is to prevent ijtihad, (2) custom is not universal, (3) correspondence includes additional benefits, (4) the text is not inherently explicit in the cause alone; explicitness comes from external context.
The key is that metaphorical usage requires actual use in a non-literal meaning, which is absent here.
4
Understand the linguistic argument on haqiqah and majaz
The opponent claims that if the text generalizes, it becomes a metaphorical meaning. The Hanafi response: the word is used in all its literal meanings (istimal fi al-kull), so it remains haqiqah. Metaphor is based on usage, not on indication.
See the quote from 'al-Tahrir' stating there is no metaphor at all.
5
Apply the principle to a new case
Given a Quranic verse revealed about a specific incident (e.g., the verse on zihar or on false accusation), determine its scope according to the Hanafi rule: it applies generally to all similar cases unless there is a specific indicator (qarinah) to restrict it.
Consider why this matters for deriving rulings in new situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كانت الفائدة منحصرة في تخصيص الحكم وليس كذلك، بل فائدته منع تخصيصه بالاجتهاد ومعرفة أنه نص فيه (وربما تكون معرفة الأسباب قرينة على فهم المراد) وهذا أجل فائدة (و) (ثالثاً : لو قال لا أتغدى في جواب من قال : تعال تغد عندي لم يعم) فلا يحنث إلا بالتغدي عنده (قلنا) إن مقتضى حقيقة الكلام العموم، لكن صرف عنه إلى خصوص التغذي، (ذلك بعرف خاص فيه دون غيره ولهذا لو زاد اليوم) وقال : لا أتغدى اليوم، عم وحنث بالتغدي ولو في بيته (على أن الإمام زفر يمنع الملازمة) ويقول يعم أيضاً (و) قالوا (رابعاً على تقدير العموم) أي عموم الوارد على سبب خاص لم يكن الجواب مطابقاً) للسؤال، والمطابقة واجبة (قلنا) ليس المطابقة إلا كون الجواب بحيث يفهم منه حال المسؤول عنه، وقد حصل ههنا معرفة حال المسؤول عنه مع معرفة أشياء أخر غيره فطابق وزاد، إذ (الزيادة لفائدة) أخرى لا تنفي المطابقة، و قالوا (خامساً) العام الوارد على سبب خاص (نص في السبب فقط) بالاتفاق (وقد كان في وضع اللغة (ظاهراً في الكل فانصرف عن الموضوع له إلى معنى مجازي وهو متعدد السبب فقط، والسبب مع كل ما عداه والسبب مع بعض ما عداه (فلو عم الكل فقد أريد أحد معانيه المحتملة المجازية وكان تحكماً بأحد مجازات محتملة) وهو باطل فلا (أقول : بل يكون حكماً بمجاز مرجوح لأن الراجح السبب فقط بقرينة السؤال أو الحادثة قلنا أولاً لا نسلم أنه نص في السبب بمجرد اللفظ بل محتمل للكل بسواء، و(القطع) بدخوله من خارج فهو المحقق للنصوصية ولا يلزم منه كون اللفظ مجازاً، وإنما ( يلزم لو كانت النصوصية من نفس اللفظ (و) قلنا (ثانياً) سلمنا النصوصية لكن (النصوصية في البعض لا تستلزم المجازية (لأنها أي المجازية تكون بالاستعمال ، وهو في الكل) فلا مجاز كذا في «التحرير») وعبارته لا مجاز أصلاً لأنه بالاستعمال في المعنى لا بكيفية الدلالة، وقد استعمل في الكل فهو حقيقة والظاهر أنه معارضة على كونه مجازاً فلا يرد عليه شيء، والمصنف حمل على المنع بعد تسليم النصوصية، فأورد عليه بإثبات المقدمة الممنوعة، وقال: (أقول : تساوي النسبة إلى الجميع أي تساوي نسبة اللفظ إلى جميع الأفراد مع قطع النظر عن خارج لازم الحقيقة بالضرورة فإذا انتفى التساوي (انتفت الحقيقة فلا مجال لمنع المجازية بعد تسليم النصوصية في البعض، بل لا يكون نصوصية من اللفظ أصلاً لا في الكل ولا في البعض، فإن قيل : لعله سلم النصوصية من الخارج ، يقال : آل إلى الجواب الأول حينئذ فتدبر
The benefit was confined to specifying the ruling, but that is not so; rather its benefit is to prevent its specification by ijtihad and to know that it is explicit in it. (And perhaps knowledge of the causes is an indication for understanding the intended meaning) and this is the greatest benefit. (And) (Thirdly: If he said 'I will not eat lunch' in response to someone who said 'Come eat lunch with me', it does not generalize) so he is not guilty of oath-breaking unless he eats lunch with him. (We said): The requirement of the literal meaning of speech is generality, but it is diverted to the specific act of eating lunch (by a particular custom in this case and not in others; for this reason if he added 'today' and said 'I will not eat lunch today', it generalizes and he breaks the oath even if he eats lunch in his house). (Also, Imam Zufar denies the implication) and says it generalizes as well. (And) They said (Fourthly: Assuming generality) meaning the generality of the text revealed on a specific cause, the answer would not correspond) to the question, and correspondence is obligatory. (We said): Correspondence is only that the answer be such that the state of the questioned matter is understood from it, and here the state of the questioned matter is understood along with the understanding of other things besides it, so it corresponds and adds, since (the addition is for another benefit) that does not negate correspondence. And they said (Fifthly): The general text revealed on a specific cause is (explicit in the cause only) by agreement (and it was in the linguistic usage (apparent in all, so it turned from the literal meaning to a metaphorical meaning, which is multiple: the cause only, the cause with everything else, and the cause with some of what is besides it. So if it generalizes everything, then one of its possible metaphorical meanings has been intended, and it is a ruling by one of the possible metaphorical meanings) and that is invalid. (I say: Rather, it is a ruling by a preponderated metaphorical meaning, because the preponderant is the cause only, by the context of the question or event. We said: First, we do not accept that it is explicit in the cause by mere wording; rather it is equally probable for all, and (the certainty) of its inclusion of other matters is from external evidence, so that is what realizes its explicitness, and it does not necessitate that the wording be metaphorical. It would (only necessitate if the explicitness were from the wording itself). (And) We said (Secondly): Even if we accept explicitness, (explicitness in some does not necessitate metaphoricity) (because metaphoricity is by usage, and it is in all) so there is no metaphor, as in 'al-Tahrir'. And his expression: there is no metaphor at all because it is used in the meaning, not by the manner of indication, and it is used in all so it is literal. And apparently it is an objection to it being metaphorical, so nothing is objected to it. And the author adopted the prevention after accepting explicitness, so he objected by proving the prevented premise and said: (I say: The equal proportion to all, i.e., the equal proportion of the word to all individuals when considered independently from external evidence, is necessary for literalness. So if the equality is negated, (literalness is negated, and there is no room to deny metaphoricity after accepting explicitness in some. Rather, there is no explicitness from the wording at all, neither in all nor in some. If it is said: Perhaps he accepted explicitness from externals, it is said: then it returns to the first answer. So reflect.
Source pages p. 288

Commentary
This passage is a detailed usul dispute about whether a general text revealed on a specific cause remains general in its legal implication. The opponents raise five objections: (1) the benefit is only to specify the ruling, (2) knowledge of the cause is the greatest benefit, (3) the example of 'I will not eat lunch' shows specificity due to custom, (4) answering a specific question with a general statement fails correspondence, and (5) the text becomes explicit only in the cause, making generalization metaphorical and arbitrary. The Hanafi response, framed as 'qulna' (we said), refutes each: (1) the real benefit is to prevent speculation, (2) causes aid understanding, (3) the custom is specific, but adding 'today' makes it general, (4) correspondence is achieved because the questioner learns both the specific and additional benefits, and (5) the text is not inherently explicit only in the cause; rather, its explicitness is from external context, and even if it were explicit in some, that does not entail metaphor, as metaphor depends on usage not indication. The author then critiques the opponent's claim by asserting that equal probability is necessary for literalness, but if the equality is negated (due to external context), literalness is negated, but that does not lead to metaphoricity unless the word is used in a non-literal meaning. The discussion ends with a caution to reflect.

Key Terms
عام وارد على سبب خاص (amm waarid ala sabab khas): A general text that was revealed in response to a specific incident or question.
نص (nass): A text that is explicit in its meaning, leaving no room for other interpretations.
مجاز (majaz): Metaphorical usage of a word, i.e., using it in a meaning other than its original literal meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the primary justification for keeping a general text revealed on a specific cause general?
A) The specific cause is included in the general wording.
B) The text becomes metaphorical if generalized.
C) Correspondence requires the answer to match the question exactly.
D) Custom forces specificity in all such texts.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position is that a general text revealed for a specific cause remains general in its legal implication unless there is clear evidence (dalil) to restrict it.

Al-Muqtada: The Implied Meaning and Its Legal Status
Definition of muqtada and the debate on its generality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define al-muqtada according to the Hanafi school and distinguish it from uttered words, and identify when a statement requires an implied element.

Core Idea
Al-muqtada is what speech demands for truth or correctness, yet remains unspoken — its ruling depends on whether it is considered like an uttered word.

This lesson introduces the concept of al-muqtada (implied meaning) in usul al-fiqh. The passage defines muqtada as the unstated element necessary for a statement to be true or correct. We will explore the two main interpretations: those who treat the implied element like an uttered word (so it can be general or specific) and those who deny it any generality. The debate has practical consequences for how we understand textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Read the definition: muqtada is what speech demands for its truthfulness or correctness without being mentioned. Identify the two conditions: truth (sidq) and correctness (sihha).
Truthfulness refers to correspondence with reality; correctness refers to linguistic and legal soundness.
2
Identify the two views
Note that some scholars (Shafi'is and Imam Abu Zayd) consider the implied element like an uttered word, so it can have generality/specificity. Others (Fakhr al-Islam, Shams al-A'imma) deny generality because it is not uttered.
The denial of generality here means that the muqtada cannot be specified (takhsis) and does not carry rulings of general words.
3
Analyze the example
Consider the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (The ummah is excused for error and forgetfulness). What must be implied? Possibilities: removal of sin, removal of punishment, removal of the act itself. Determine the legal implications.
If only one possibility is accurate, it is specified; if multiple, the statement may become ambiguous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقتضى ما استدعاه صدق الكلام أو صحته من غير أن يكون مذكوراً في اللفظ، أي الأمر الغير المذكور اعتبر لأجل صدق الكلام أو صحته، ولولاه لاختل أحدهما (وعلى هذا فالمحذوف منه فإنه غير مذكور اعتبر لتوقف الصدق أو الصحة عليه وهذا اصطلاح الشافعية والقاضي الإمام أبي زيد منا ( ويمثل بقوله عليه وعلى آله وأصحابه الصلاة والسلام (رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان فإن صدقه لا يتصوّر إلا بتقدير شيء فإن كان خاصاً أو عاماً بعينه) من دون احتمال آخر (لزم ذلك الخاص أو العام فيقدّر ومنع عمومه لعدم كونه لفظاً) بعد إدخال المحذوف فيه كما وقع عن القاضي الإمام كما وهم ليس بشيء، لأن المقدر كالملفوظ) في الاتصاف بالعموم والخصوص، وأما من فسره بمعنى يفهم التزاماً لأجل تصحيح الكلام أو صدقه، مثل المأكول في لا آكل والبيع في أعتق عبدك عني بألف لا مثل الحديث المذكور، ادعى عدم العموم بناء على كونه غير ملفوظ لا حقيقة ولا تقديراً كما فعل الإمامان فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة وعشيرته الكرام فلا يتوجه إليه هذا الرد ثم مقصودهم من نفي العموم عموم تترتب عليه الأحكام من صحة التخصيص وغيره لا نفي الاستغراق مطلقاً، كيف وقد أجمعوا هم على الحنث بأكل كل مأكول وسيبين المصنف هذا المقتضى ويصرح بأنه لا عموم له، فليس المقصود الرد عليهم، بل على من نفى العموم عنه مع إدخال المحذوف فيه، وأشار في «التحرير» حيث قال : ومنع عمومه هنا لعدم كونه لفظاً ليس بشيء، كقوله ههنا إلى أن المقتضى لا عموم له في غير صورة الحذف هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم هذا المقام (وإن كان ثم تقديرات يصح كل بدلا من حيث إنه مصحح للكلام، يعني يكون بحيث يصح هذا الكلام بواحد أياً كان وبعد اعتبار واحد لا يحتاج إلى آخر فلا يضمر الكل عندنا معاً (خلافاً للشافعي رحمه الله تعالى فإنه يضمر الكل عنده بل إن اختلفت أحكامها بأن يكون مع تقدير مفيد الحكم، ومع آخر لحكم آخر، ولا معين فمجمل فيتوقف إلى أن يتبين المراد (وإن لم تختلف أحكامها (فالمقدر المنتشر ) يعني الخيار إلى المخاطب في التقدير، ولا يتوقف في العمل، قال في الكشف»، ورأيت في بعض كتب أصحاب الشافعي دل العقل أو الشرع على إضمار شيء في كلام صيانة له عن التكذيب ونحوه، وثمة تقديرات يصح الكلام بأيها كان لا يجوز إضمار الكل، وهو المراد من قوله : المقتضى لا عموم له، أما إذا تعين التقديرات بدليل فيقدر كظهوره في العموم والخصوص حتى لو كان مظهره عاماً كان مقدره كذلك، وكذا لو كان خاصاً، وعلى هذا فلا نزاع فافهم لنا في تقدير (الواحد كفاية) لأجل التصحيح بالفرض فتقدير الزائد من غير ضرورة والضرورة تقدر بقدرها) إذ التقدير إنما كان لضرورة التصحيح ، فلا يقدر الزائد الشافعية قالوا :أولاً : إضمار الكل كرفع أحكام الخطأ أقرب إلى الحقيقة، كرفع ذات الخطأ فإن انتفاء جميع أوصاف الذات أقرب إلى نفي الذات والمجاز الأقرب أولى من الأبعد أقول كليته أي كلية أقربية إضمار الكل ممنوعة، لجواز أن يكون المقتضى في الإثبات نحو : إنما الأعمال بالنيات على أن إضمار الكل كأنه مجازات) كما أن الإضمار والمجاز في مرتبة وقلة المجاز (أولى فههنا مانع عن الحمل على الأقرب، ويحتمل أن يكون معارضة ومن (ههنا أي ومن أجل لزوم كثرة المجاز (قلنا: الإجمال) وإن كان كان خلاف الأصل أولى من التعميم) أي تعميم التقديرات (وقد يجاب تارة كما في «التحرير» بأن الحمل على المجاز الأقرب إنما هو إذا لم ينفه الدليل كون الموجب لإضمار البعض ينفي إضمار الكل لأنه بلا مقتض، أقول) موجب إضمار البعض لا ينفي إضمار الكل مر أنه متى أحد
Al-muqtada is what the truthfulness or correctness of speech demands without being mentioned in the wording, i.e., the unmentioned thing considered for the sake of the truthfulness or correctness of the speech, without which one of them would be defective. According to this, the deleted (element) from it is unmentioned, considered because truthfulness or correctness depends on it. This is the terminology of the Shafi'is and the chief Imam Abu Zayd among us. And it is exemplified by his saying ﷺ: 'The ummah is excused for error and forgetfulness.' For its truthfulness cannot be conceived except by supposing something. If it is specific or general with certainty, without other possibility, that specific or general is necessitated and is supposed. And its generality is prevented because it is not a wording, after including the deleted in it, as occurred from the chief Imam, which is a mistake and is nothing, because the supposed is like the uttered in being described by generality and specificity. As for those who interpreted it as a meaning understood by implication for the sake of correcting speech or its truthfulness, like 'the eaten' in 'I do not eat' and 'the sale' in 'Free your slave from me for a thousand' – not like the mentioned hadith – they claimed lack of generality based on it being not uttered, neither in reality nor by supposition, as did the two imams Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma and their noble group. So this refutation does not apply to them. Then their purpose in negating generality is a generality upon which rulings like validity of specification etc. are built, not negation of all-inclusiveness absolutely; how could they all agree on the oath being violated by eating every edible? The author will explain this muqtada and clarify that it has no generality. So the intended refutation is not against them, but against those who negated generality from it while including the deleted in it. He indicated in al-Tahrir where he said: 'And preventing its generality here because it is not a wording is nothing,' like his statement here that the muqtada has no generality in other than the case of deletion. This is how this position should be understood. (And if there are suppositions, each of which is valid as a substitute insofar as it corrects the speech, meaning that this speech is correct by any one, and after considering one, no other is needed, then we do not suppose all together. Contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah have mercy on him, for he supposes all according to him. Rather, if their rulings differ – such that with one supposition a certain legal ruling is useful, and with another a different ruling, and there is no specific determinant – then it is ambiguous and one must wait until the intended meaning is clarified. And if their rulings do not differ, then the supposed is widespread, meaning the choice is left to the addressee in supposition, and action is not suspended. It is stated in al-Kashf: 'I saw in some books of the Shafi'i followers: if reason or revelation indicates the suppression of something in speech to protect it from falsification and the like, and there are suppositions with any of which the speech is correct, it is not permissible to suppose all.' This is what is meant by the statement: 'the muqtada has no generality.' However, if the suppositions are specified by evidence, then it is supposed according to its appearance in generality and specificity, so that if its manifest form is general, its supposed is likewise, and similarly if it is specific. On this, there is no dispute, so understand. For us, supposing one is sufficient for correction by hypothesis; supposing extra without necessity is not done, and necessity is measured by its extent. The Shafi'is said: first, supposing all, like removing the rulings of error, is closer to reality, like removing the essence of error, because the negation of all attributes of the essence is closer to negating the essence, and the closer metaphor is preferable to the farther. I say: the totality of the claim that supposing all is closer is denied, because it is possible that the muqtada in affirmative contexts like 'Actions are by intentions' – the supposing all is like multiple metaphors. Just as suppression and metaphor are of the same rank, and the sparsity of metaphor is preferable, so here there is an obstacle to the closer interpretation. It is possible that there is an objection. And from here, due to the necessity of many metaphors, we say: ambiguity (ijmal), although it is contrary to the original principle, is preferable to generalization (ta'mim). It may also be answered, as in al-Tahrir, that the application of the closer metaphor is only when evidence does not negate it, because the factor that necessitates supposing some negates supposing all since it is without a need. I say: the factor that necessitates supposing some does not negate supposing all; it was mentioned that whenever one...
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage discusses the concept of al-muqtada (the implied meaning) in usul al-fiqh. It defines muqtada as the unstated element required for the truthfulness (sidq) or correctness (sihha) of a statement. The example given is the hadith 'رفع عن أمتي الخطأ والنسيان' (The ummah is excused for error and forgetfulness). For this statement to be true, something must be implied, such as 'رفع أحكام الخطأ والنسيان' (the rulings of error and forgetfulness are lifted) or 'رفع ذات الخطأ' (the essence of error is lifted). The debate centers on whether such implied elements carry generality. According to the Shafi'is and some Hanafis like Imam Abu Zayd, the implied element is considered like an uttered word in terms of generality/specificity if the deletion is specified. However, other Hanafis (like Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma) argue that since the muqtada is not uttered, it has no generality, meaning it does not allow specification (takhsis) or other operations that apply to general words. The text clarifies that the disagreement is only about cases where the deleted element is considered part of the wording; if not, the muqtada indeed has no generality. When multiple suppositions are possible and equally correct, the Hanafis (contrary to al-Shafi'i) do not suppose all simultaneously but only one sufficient for correctness. If the rulings differ according to different suppositions and no evidence determines which, the statement is considered ambiguous (mujmal) until clarification. The Shafi'is argue for supposing all possibilities as more faithful to reality, but the Hanafis respond that this would lead to excessive metaphor, so ambiguity is preferable to forced generalization.

Key Terms
مقتضى (muqtada): The unmentioned element required for the truthfulness or correctness of speech.
تقدير (taqdir): The act of mentally supplying the implied element in a statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the Hanafi ruling when there are multiple possible implied elements (taqdirat) that can correct a statement, and their legal rulings differ?
A) All must be considered together.
B) The statement becomes ambiguous (mujmal) until the intended meaning is clarified.
C) The most common meaning is chosen.
D) The speaker's intention is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Al-muqtada is an unspoken element essential for understanding speech, but its legal status (generality or specificity) is contested among Hanafis and between schools.
